Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
Liability of the Agent: Exception for Interdicted and Sale vs Purchase Distinction
Explaining the exception for interdicted persons and liability distinctions in sale vs purchase agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of liability for an agent in Hanafi law and identify the exception when the agent is interdicted, distinguishing between agency for sale and purchase with immediate or deferred price.

Core Idea
The agent bears liability for sale and immediate purchase; the principal bears liability for deferred purchase if the agent is interdicted.

The general principle is that an agent (wakil) incurs liability (uhda) for the transaction he executes. However, when the agent is interdicted (mahjur alayh) — such as a slave without master's permission or a minor — liability may shift to the principal. This lesson examines the detailed distinctions from al-Dhakhira regarding sale and purchase agency, including the effect of immediate versus deferred payment.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule from the author’s speech
The apparent meaning of the author’s words is that the liability is on the authorized agent absolutely, regardless of the principal’s presence.
This is the default position before exceptions.
2
Identify the exception for the interdicted person
According to Qadikhan, the interdicted due to folly is like a minor: liability does not bind him. Rights attach to the principal, akin to a messenger or judge’s trustee.
The exception is limited to four cases not detailed here.
3
Distinguish between agent for sale and agent for purchase
In al-Dhakhira, if the agent is for sale, liability is on him whether price is immediate or deferred. If for purchase, liability depends on payment terms: immediate price → agent liable; deferred price → principal liable (as guarantee).
The deferred purchase is akin to kafala (suretyship).
4
Analyze the dispute in al-Idah regarding deferred purchase
Al-Idah holds that in deferred purchase, the purchase is for the agent himself, not the principal, so liability is on agent. The text corrects this, maintaining the principal’s liability as in al-Dhakhira.
The preferred view is al-Dhakhira’s distinction.
5
Apply the ruling to a contested scenario
If a slave agent claims interdiction after selling, his word is not accepted when denying liability; the origin is validity. However, if he claims interdiction to negate liability, his word may be accepted.
This is based on the principle that absence of binding does not imply impermissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهو المعقول) قال الرملي : وسيجزم - أعني البزازي - بما هو المعقول كما في هذا الشرح منقول آخر هذه المقولة هذا الشارح بأنه أفتى به بعد ما احتاط والله تعالى أعلم. وسيصرح مع صح قبضه وإنما زدته هنا لدخوله تحت المحجور عليه في كلامهم ولقول قاضيخان في الحجر إن المحجور عليه بالسفه بمنزلة الصبي إلا في أربعة فلا تلزمه عهده ،كهو، وظاهر كلام المصنف أن العهدة على المأذون مطلقاً. وفصل في الذخيرة بين أن يكون وكيلاً بالبيع فالعهدة عليه سواء باع بثمن حال أو مؤجل، وبين أن يكون وكيلاً بالشراء فإن كان بثمن مؤجل فهي على الموكل لأنه في معنى الكفالة وإن كان بثمن حال فهي على الوكيل لكونه ضمان ثمن اهـ. وخالف في الإيضاح فيما إذا اشترى بثمن مؤجل فجعل الشراء له لا للموكل لأن الشراء للموكل والعهدة عليه كما في الذخيرة وإيضاحه في الشرح وقيد بقوله «إن لم يكن محجوراً لأن المحجور تتعلق الحقوق بموكله كالرسول والقاضي وأمينه ولو قبضه هذا العاقد فكان أصيلاً فيه وانتفاء اللزوم لا يدل على انتفاء الجواز. ثم العبد إذا عتق تلزمه تلك العهدة والصبي إذا بلغ لا تلزمه لأن المانع المولى مع أهليته وقد زال وفي الصبي حق نفسه ولا يزول بالبلوغ، ولو وقع التنازع في كونه محجوراً أو مأذوناً حال كونه وكيلاً لم أره. وفي الخانية من الحجر عبد اشترى من رجل شيئاً فقال البائع لا أسلم إليك المبيع لأنك محجور وقال العبد أنا مأذون كان القول قول العبد فإن أقام البائع بينة على أن العبد أقر أنه محجور قبل أن يتقدم إلى القضاء بعد الشراء لم تقبل بينته . ثم قال : عبد باع من رجل شيئاً ثم قال هذا الذي بعتك لمولاي وأنا محجور وقال المشتري بل أنت مأذون كان القول قول المشتري ولا يقبل قول العبد ا هـ . لأن الأصل النفاذ وإقدامهما يدل عليه، ومن هنا يقع الفرق بينهما وبين ما إذا كان وكيلاً فإن النفاذ حاصل بدون الإذن ولزوم العهدة شيء آخر فينبغي أن يقبل قول العبد أنه محجور عليه لتنتفي العهدة عنه. وشمل كلامه المرتد فإن العهدة عليه لكن موقوفة عند الإمام فإن أسلم كانت عليه وإلا فعلى الموكل، وعندهما هي عليه مطلقاً. وهي معروفة وظاهر كلام المصنف أن للوكيل بالإجارة قبض الأجرة وعليه تسليم العين إلى المستأجر، وفي منية المفتي خلافه قال الوكيل بالإجارة ليس له قبض الأجرة وحبس المستأجر به ولو وهب الأجرة قبل القبض جاز إن لم يكن شيئاً بعينه ا هـ. وهو سبق قلم والصواب ما في كافي الحاكم أن للوكيل بالإجارة المخاصمة في إثباتها وقبض الأجرة وحبس المستأجر به فإن وهب الأجرة قبل القبض جاز إن لم يكن بعينه ويضمنه وإن
He said: '(And it is the rational).' Al-Ramli said: 'Al-Bazzazi will decide - I mean - what is rationally understood, as in this commentary, transmitted from the last of this statement from this commentator that he gave fatwa after being cautious. Allah knows best. And he will clearly state with the correctness of his receipt. And I only added it here because it falls under the interdicted person in their speech, and because of the saying of Qadikhan in al-Hajr that the interdicted person due to folly is like a minor except in four things, so the liability does not bind him, like him. And the apparent meaning of the author's speech is that the liability is on the authorized person absolutely. And he distinguished in al-Dhakhira between if he is an agent for sale, then the liability is on him, whether he sold for immediate or deferred price; and if he is an agent for purchase, then if the price is deferred, it is on the principal because it is in the sense of guarantee, and if the price is immediate, it is on the agent because it is a guarantee of the price. End. And he disagreed in al-Idah regarding the case of purchase with deferred price, making the purchase for himself, not for the principal, because the purchase is for the principal and the liability is on him, as in al-Dhakhira and its clarification in the commentary. And he restricted by his saying 'if he is not interdicted' because the interdicted person's rights are attached to his principal like the messenger, the judge, and his trustee, and if this contractor took possession, he is an original party in it, and the absence of binding does not indicate the absence of permissibility. Then the slave, if he is emancipated, that liability becomes binding on him; and the minor, if he reaches puberty, it does not become binding on him because the impediment is the master along with his capacity and it has ceased, and in the minor there is his own right which does not cease with puberty. And if a dispute occurs about whether he is interdicted or authorized at the time of his agency, I have not seen it. And in al-Khaniya from al-Hajr: a slave bought something from a man, and the seller said: I will not hand over the sold item to you because you are interdicted, and the slave said: I am authorized; the word is the word of the slave. If the seller brings evidence that the slave confessed that he was interdicted before he approached the court after the purchase, his evidence is not accepted. Then he said: a slave sold something to a man, then said: this which I sold to you belongs to my master and I am interdicted, and the buyer said: rather you are authorized; the word is the word of the buyer and the word of the slave is not accepted. End. Because the origin is validity and their proceeding indicates it. From here the difference occurs between them and the case where he is an agent, for validity is obtained without permission and the binding of liability is another matter, so the word of the slave that he is interdicted should be accepted to negate liability from him. And his speech includes the apostate, for the liability is on him but suspended according to Imam Abu Hanifa: if he becomes Muslim, it is on him; otherwise on the principal. According to the two disciples, it is on him absolutely. This is known. And the apparent meaning of the author's speech is that the agent for hire has the right to receive the rent and must hand over the leased object to the lessee. And in Munyat al-Mufti there is a contrary view: the agent for hire does not have the right to receive the rent nor to detain the lessee because of it, and if he gives the rent as a gift before receipt, it is permissible if it is not a specific thing. End. It is a slip of the pen, and the correct view is what is in Kafi al-Hakim that the agent for hire has the right to litigate for its establishment and receive the rent and detain the lessee because of it, and if he gives the rent as a gift before receipt, it is permissible if it is not a specific thing, and he is liable for it.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings and clarifications by the commentator. First, he confirms that the relied-upon view is that the agent's liability remains even if the principal is present, as per rational understanding. Then he discusses the exception for the interdicted person (المحجور عليه), which includes the slave and the minor with permission. The general rule is that the agent bears liability, but if the agent is interdicted (like a slave without master's permission or a minor without guardian's permission), the liability may be on the principal because the agent lacks full capacity. However, there is a distinction: for a sale agent, the liability is on the agent regardless of the payment terms; for a purchase agent, if the price is deferred, the liability is on the principal (as it is like a guarantee), but if immediate, on the agent. There is also a discussion about the apostate agent: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the liability is suspended until his return to Islam; if he returns, he bears it; otherwise, the principal bears it. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the liability is on the apostate absolutely. Finally, the author addresses the agent for hire: contrary to a view in Munyat al-Mufti, the correct view is that the agent for hire has the right to receive rent and detain the lessee, and can gift the rent before receipt if not a specific item.

Key Terms
محجور عليه (mahjur alayh): Interdicted person, one who is legally prevented from managing his own property due to folly, slavery, or minority.
عهدة (uhda): Liability for the price or compensation in a sale; the obligation to bear loss if the transaction fails or if a defect appears.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dhakhira, who bears the liability when an agent buys goods on deferred payment?
A) The agent bears all liability
B) The principal bears liability because it is like a guarantee
C) The liability is shared
D) The contract is void due to deferred payment

Key Takeaway
The liability of an agent depends on the type of transaction and payment terms, with an exception for the interdicted agent.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Apostate Agent and Agent for Hire
Addressing the apostate agent dispute and correcting the ruling on agent for hire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the liability of an apostate agent and state the correct ruling for an agent for hire regarding receipt of rent.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa suspends the apostate agent’s liability; the two disciples hold it absolute. The agent for hire may receive rent and detain the lessee.

The previous lesson focused on the general rule and the interdiction exception. This lesson addresses two additional cases: the apostate agent (مرتد) and the agent for hire (وكيل بالإجارة). Regarding the apostate, there is a well-known disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples. For the agent for hire, the author corrects a mistaken view from Munyat al-Mufti, establishing that the agent has full rights to receive rent and detain the lessee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for the apostate agent
The passage includes the apostate in the general rule: liability is on him, but with a suspension according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
The two disciples disagree, making it absolute.
2
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa’s position
The liability is suspended (موقوفة): if the apostate returns to Islam, he bears it; otherwise, the principal bears it.
This is based on the principle that the apostate's transactions are suspended.
3
Understand the two disciples’ position
They hold that the liability is on the apostate absolutely, regardless of his return to Islam or execution.
This is known as the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Determine the practical consequence
If the apostate dies without repentance, the principal bears the loss according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the disciples, the apostate’s estate bears it (if any).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Analyze the ruling for the agent for hire
The author corrects a mistaken view from Munyat al-Mufti that the agent for hire cannot receive rent or detain the lessee. The correct view from Kafi al-Hakim affirms these rights, and also allows gifting rent before receipt if not a specific item.
The author calls the former a 'slip of the pen' (سبق قلم).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings and clarifications by the commentator. First, he confirms that the relied-upon view is that the agent's liability remains even if the principal is present, as per rational understanding. Then he discusses the exception for the interdicted person (المحجور عليه), which includes the slave and the minor with permission. Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam; one who has renounced the Islamic faith.
وكيل بالإجارة (wakil bi al-ijarah): Agent for hire, an agent appointed to lease out a property or service.

According to the correct Hanafi view, what can an agent for hire do regarding the rent?
A) Only collect rent but cannot detain the lessee

D) Can gift the rent without any restriction

Key Takeaway
The apostate's liability is suspended under Imam Abu Hanifa; an agent for hire may actively manage rent collection and enforcement.

Agent Delivering Sold Goods Before Collecting Price
Ruling on agent delivering before price collection despite prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent delivering sold goods before collecting the price when prohibited by the principal, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
If the principal forbids delivery until payment, the agent's delivery before payment is valid according to the Sahibayn but invalid according to Abu Hanifa.

In this passage, al-Marghinani expands on a scenario where a principal instructs his agent not to deliver the sold item until the price is collected. The agent disobeys and delivers early. What is the legal effect? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its practical conditions, drawing from later authoritative manuals like al-Khulasa, al-Qunyah, and al-Bazzaziyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the prohibition
The act is the agent handing over the sold item (tasleem al-mabee') to the buyer. The principal prohibits this until the price (thaman) is collected. We must determine the validity of the agent's delivery despite the prohibition.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to the text, delivery before price collection despite prohibition is valid according to 'them' (the two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and invalid according to Abu Yusuf (a possible textual error; standard is Abu Hanifa). Actually, Abu Hanifa holds it invalid and the agent liable for loss; the Sahibayn hold it valid.
The passage mentions 'contrary to Abu Yusuf' but the standard madhhab position is that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it. We follow the standard ruling.
3
Distinguish scenarios based on possession
Al-Bazzaziyyah distinguishes: if the goods are in the agent's hand, the rules apply; if in the principal's hand, the principal may refuse delivery until payment. If the principal sold on credit, he is compelled to deliver. If the principal takes the goods from the agent and the agent retakes them, liability depends on timing of the prohibition.
4
Determine consequences of loss or death
If the goods perish after delivery but before price collection, under the Sahibayn the agent is not liable for the goods themselves (the buyer owes the price). However, if the agent took possession of the goods after the principal's prohibition, he may be liable for loss. If the agent dies before delivery, the sale is rescinded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في تسليم المبيع فشمل ما إذا قبض الوكيل الثمن أو لا، وما إذا قال له الموكل لا تدفع المبيع بعد البيع حتى تقبض الثمن فدفع الوكيل قبل قبض الثمن جاز عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهي مسألة الوكيل إذا أقال؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفي القنية : لو نهاه عن تسليم المبيع حتى يقبض الثمن كان باطلاً ا اهـ. البزازية : وهذا إذا كان المبيع في يد الوكيل ، فلو كان في يد الموكل وأبي الدفع قبل قبض ثمنه له ذلك، وإن باعه نسيئة وأبى الموكل من دفعه قبل قبضه يجبر عليه، وإن كان في يد الوكيل وأخذه الموكل وأراد أن لا يدفع قبل بقبض الثمن فأخذه الوكيل من بيته وهلك في يد الوكيل أن الأخذ بعد البيع لا يضمن وإن قبله وقد نهاه عن القبض يضمن، ولو لم يهلك حتى باعه جاز، فإن مات قبل أن يسلم إلى المشتري انفسخ البيع ا هـ . وقيدنا بالنهي عن تسليم المبيع، سواء كان قبل بيعه أو بعده، لأنه لو نهاه عن البيع حتى يقبض الثمن لم يجز بيعه حتى يقبض الثمن من المشتري ثم يقول بعتك بهذه الدراهم التي قبضت منك كذا في البزازية
And he made absolute in delivering the sold item, so it includes whether the agent collected the price or not, and whether the principal said to him 'Do not hand over the sold item after the sale until you collect the price' and the agent handed over before collecting the price: it is permissible according to them (the two companions) contrary to Abu Yusuf, and it is the issue of the agent when he revokes; as in al-Khulasa. And in al-Qunyah: If he forbade him from delivering the sold item until he collects the price, it is void. In al-Bazzaziyyah: This is when the sold item is in the hand of the agent. If it is in the hand of the principal and he refuses to hand over before collecting its price, he has that right. And if he sold it on credit and the principal refuses to hand it over before collecting it, he is compelled to do so. And if it is in the hand of the agent and the principal took it and wanted not to deliver before collecting the price, and the agent took it from his house and it perished in the hand of the agent: the taking after the sale does not make him liable, but if he took it before and the principal had forbidden him from collecting, he is liable. And if it did not perish until he sold it, it is permissible. And if he died before delivering to the buyer, the sale is rescinded. And we restrict it with the prohibition of delivering the sold item, whether it is before its sale or after, because if he forbade him from selling until he collects the price, his sale is not valid until he collects the price from the buyer, then he says 'I sold it to you with these dirhams that I collected from you' as in al-Bazzaziyyah.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling of an agent delivering the sold item before collecting the price. The base text made the ruling absolute, covering both cases where the agent collected the price or not. However, when the principal explicitly forbids delivery until price collection, the agent's delivery before collection is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, contrary to Imam Abu Hanifa (though the text says 'contrary to Abu Yusuf'—this appears to be a textual error; the standard position is that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it while Abu Hanifa forbids). The reference to 'وكيل إذا أقال' (agent when he revokes) in al-Khulasa refers to the same principle. Al-Qunyah states that such a prohibition renders the delivery void. Al-Bazzaziyyah distinguishes various scenarios: if the goods are in the agent's hand, the rules apply; if in the principal's hand, he may refuse delivery until payment; if the principal sold on credit, he is compelled to deliver; if the principal takes the goods from the agent and wants to withhold, and the agent retakes them, the agent is not liable for loss if the loss occurs after sale, but if he took them before sale against the principal's prohibition, he is liable. If the goods perish before delivery, the sale is rescinded. The key condition is that the prohibition must be specifically about delivery, not about selling. If the principal forbade selling until collecting the price, the agent cannot sell at all until collection.

Key Terms
تسليم المبيع (tasleem al-mabee'): The act of handing over the sold item to the buyer.
قبض الثمن (qabd al-thaman): Taking possession of the sale price.
أقال (aqala): The agent revokes or rescinds a sale (iqalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazzaziyyah, if the sold item is in the principal's hand and he refuses to deliver before collecting the price, what is the ruling?
A) He must deliver immediately.
B) He has the right to refuse delivery until price collection.
C) He is compelled to deliver by the court.
D) The sale becomes void.

Key Takeaway
When an agent delivers sold goods before collecting the price despite the principal's prohibition, the sale remains valid per the relied-upon Hanafi view, but the agent may face liability for any loss.

Agent's Guarantee for Goods Given to a Broker: Custom as Implicit Permission
Ruling on agent's guarantee with custom exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's guarantee when giving goods to a broker, and apply the exception based on prevailing custom.

Core Idea
An agent guarantees goods given to a broker unless prevailing custom gives him implicit permission.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a practical commercial scenario: an agent for sale hands over the sold item to a broker (dallal) to find a buyer. The key legal question is whether the agent guarantees the goods if they are lost or damaged while in the broker's possession. The Hanafi ruling evolves from an initial non-guarantee to a chosen view of guarantee, then finally a nuanced position conditioned by custom. Understanding this ruling requires grasping how custom ('urf) can modify default liability rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
The act is an agent for sale giving the sold item to a broker to display. In the base text (author's implication), the initial ruling is that the agent does not guarantee if the item is lost in the broker's hand.
2
Recognize the chosen view (al-mukhtar)
The later chosen view in al-Bazzaziyyah is that the agent does guarantee, because he disposed of another's property without explicit permission. This is presented as the default rule.
The commentator says 'lakin al-mukhtar al-daman' (but the chosen is guarantee).
3
Analyze the custom exception
The commentator then qualifies the chosen view: the guarantee applies only when there is no prevailing custom to the contrary. If custom dictates that agents hand goods to brokers, the agent is deemed authorized and does not guarantee.
The phrase 'bi-muqtada al-'adah yakunu ma'dhunan bi-dhalik' (by virtue of custom he is deemed permitted) is key.
4
Understand the role of custom in Hanafi muamalat
Custom ('urf sahih) is a recognized secondary source in Hanafi fiqh. It can create implicit permission (idhn 'urfi) that removes liability for what would otherwise be unauthorized disposal.
This is an application of istihsan based on custom.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a scenario, determine first whether a custom exists in that trade or locality for agents to use brokers. If yes, the agent does not guarantee; if no, he does.
The burden of proving custom rests on the agent claiming the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف من كونه أصيلاً في تسليم المبيع إلى أن الوكيل بالشراء يطالب بالثمن وإن لم يقبضه من الموكل وإلى أن وكيل البيع لو دفع المبيع إلى دلال ليعرضه على من يرغب فيه فغاب أو ضاع في يده لم يضمن لكن المختار الضمان كما في البزازية لكونه دفع ملك الغير بغير إذنه وإن كان أصيلاً في الحقوق وفي البزازية وكيل البيع قال بعته وسلمته من رجل لا أعرفه وضاع الثمن قال القاضي : يضمن لأنه لا يملك التسليم قبل قبض ثمنه. قوله : (لكن المختار (الضمان أقول : ينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تكن العادة جارية في ذلك، أما إذا لأنه كان شيئاً لا يبيعه الوكيل بنفسه بل يدفع في العادة إلى دلال ليعرضه على البيع لا يضمن بمقتضى العادة يكون مأذوناً بذلك
And the author indicated, from his being an original party in delivering the sold item, that the agent for purchase is demanded for the price even if he has not collected it from the principal, and that the agent for sale, if he hands over the sold item to a broker to show it to those interested, and it becomes lost or perishes in his hand, he does not guarantee it, but the chosen view is guarantee as in al-Bazzaziyyah, because he handed over the property of another without his permission, even if he is an original party in rights. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: An agent for sale said, 'I sold it and handed it over to a man I do not know, and the price was lost.' The judge said: He guarantees it because he does not have the right to deliver before collecting the price. His statement: (But the chosen view is guarantee.) I say: It should be restricted to when the custom is not operative in that regard. However, if it is something the agent does not sell himself but rather customarily hands it to a broker to display for sale, he does not guarantee, because by virtue of custom he is deemed permitted to do so.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability of an agent for sale when he gives the goods to a broker (دلال) to find a buyer. The author points out that the base text's implication that the agent is an original party in delivery leads to the rule that an agent for purchase can be sued for the price even without receiving it from the principal. However, for an agent for sale, if he hands the item to a broker and it is lost, the initial view is that he does not guarantee it. But the chosen view (المختار) in al-Bazzaziyyah is that he does guarantee, because he disposed of another's property without explicit permission. An example is given where the agent sells to an unknown person and the price is lost—the judge rules guarantee because he delivered before collecting the price. However, the author (the commentator) qualifies this: the guarantee applies only when there is no custom (عادة) to the contrary. If it is customary in the trade that the agent does not sell directly but hands the goods to a broker, then by custom the agent is considered authorized to do so, and he does not guarantee. This is an application of the Hanafi principle that custom ('urf) can give implicit permission.

Key Terms
دلال (dallal): A broker or middleman who displays goods for sale to find a buyer.
مختار (mukhtar): The chosen or preferred ruling among conflicting opinions.
عادة جارية (aadah jariyah): Prevalent custom that forms the basis of legal rulings in muamalat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the agent does not know the broker.
B) When the custom is that agents hand goods to brokers for sale.
C) When the principal explicitly forbade delivery.
D) When the goods are sold before loss.

Key Takeaway
An agent for sale guarantees goods given to a broker only if prevailing custom does not permit such delivery.

Agent Delivery Despite Prohibition: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's delivery of goods despite the principal's prohibition to wait for price, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
An agent's delivery despite principal's prohibition is valid; the prohibition is ineffective—agent not liable.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q examines a dispute within the Hanafi school: when a principal instructs his agent not to deliver sold goods until payment is received, may the agent still deliver? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani allow it, while Abu Yusuf holds the agent liable for overstepping. The text then explores variations depending on who holds the goods and whether the prohibition was on sale itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What is the principal prohibiting? Is it the delivery or the sale itself? Note that the ruling differs if the prohibition is on the sale rather than delivery.
Prohibition on delivery vs. on sale has different rulings.
2
State the majority position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: delivery is valid, agent not liable. Justification: prohibition on delivery is ineffective because the agent is completing the sale as an amin.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Delivery violates instruction, agent is liable. Rationale: the agent must obey the principal's explicit order.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: if goods perish or buyer defaults, who bears loss? Majority: principal bears loss; Abu Yusuf: agent bears.
5
Recognize nuanced conditions
Goods in agent's hand vs. principal's hand. Also, prohibition on sale itself vs. delivery. If principal forbids sale, agent cannot sell until receiving price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في تسليم المبيع فشمل ما إذا قبض الوكيل الثمن أو لا، وما إذا قال له الموكل لا تدفع المبيع بعد البيع حتى تقبض الثمن فدفع الوكيل قبل قبض الثمن جاز عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف وهي مسألة الوكيل إذا أقال؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفي القنية : لو نهاه عن تسليم المبيع حتى يقبض الثمن كان باطلاً ا اهـ. البزازية : وهذا إذا كان المبيع في يد الوكيل ، فلو كان في يد الموكل وأبي الدفع قبل قبض ثمنه له ذلك، وإن باعه نسيئة وأبى الموكل من دفعه قبل قبضه يجبر عليه، وإن كان في يد الوكيل وأخذه الموكل وأراد أن لا يدفع قبل بقبض الثمن فأخذه الوكيل من بيته وهلك في يد الوكيل أن الأخذ بعد البيع لا يضمن وإن قبله وقد نهاه عن القبض يضمن، ولو لم يهلك حتى باعه جاز، فإن مات قبل أن يسلم إلى المشتري انفسخ البيع ا هـ . وقيدنا بالنهي عن تسليم المبيع، سواء كان قبل بيعه أو بعده، لأنه لو نهاه عن البيع حتى يقبض الثمن لم يجز بيعه حتى يقبض الثمن من المشتري ثم يقول بعتك بهذه الدراهم التي قبضت منك كذا في البزازية.
And [the author] made it absolute regarding delivery of the sold item, so it includes whether the agent received the price or not, and whether the principal said to him: 'Do not deliver the sold item after the sale until you receive the price,' and the agent delivered before receiving the price—it is permissible according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad], contrary to Abu Yusuf. This is the issue of the agent when he cancels the sale; as in Al-Khulasa. And in Al-Qunyah: If he forbade him from delivering the sold item until he receives the price, it is invalid. End quote. Al-Bazzaziyya: This applies if the sold item is in the agent's hand. If it is in the principal's hand and he refuses to deliver before receiving its price, he has that right. If he sold it on credit and the principal refuses to deliver it before receiving payment, he is forced to do so. If it is in the agent's hand and the principal took it and wanted not to deliver before receiving the price, then the agent took it from his house and it perished in the agent's hand: the taking after the sale does not incur liability, but before the sale and he had forbidden him from taking it, he is liable. If it did not perish but he sold it, it is valid. If the agent dies before delivering to the buyer, the sale is rescinded. End quote. And we restricted [the issue] to the prohibition of delivering the sold item, whether it was before its sale or after, because if he forbade him from selling until he receives the price, his sale is not valid until he receives the price from the buyer, then he says: 'I sold it to you with these dirhams that I received from you,' as in Al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school about an agent for sale who delivers the sold goods to the buyer despite the principal instructing him not to deliver until the price is received. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the 'two'), such delivery is valid and the agent is not liable even if the price is lost. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the agent acts beyond his authority and is liable. The passage then cites Al-Qunyah ruling that such a prohibition is invalid (i.e., ineffective), which aligns with the majority view. Al-Bazzaziyya further elaborates that the ruling depends on who holds the goods. If the goods are in the principal's hand, he may withhold delivery until payment. But if the goods are in the agent's hand, the principal forbidding delivery does not prevent the agent from delivering; however, if the principal physically takes the goods from the agent and they perish, liability hinges on timing: after sale, no liability; before sale, the agent is liable if he took despite prohibition. The passage also clarifies that forbidding delivery is different from forbidding sale itself: if the principal forbids the sale until receipt of price, the agent must not sell until he receives the price, then he may sell by saying 'I sold it to you with these dirhams that I received from you.' This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of the agent's authority.

Key Terms
عندهما (indahumā): Referring to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two imams), as contrasted with Abu Yusuf.
النهي عن تسليم المبيع (al-nahy 'an taslīm al-mabī'): The principal's prohibition against delivering the sold goods to the buyer before receiving the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The agent is liable for the price.
B) The delivery is invalid and the sale is rescinded.
C) The delivery is valid and the agent is not liable.
D) The delivery is valid only if the buyer is known.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, an agent's delivery despite the principal's prohibition to wait for price is valid and the agent is not liable; the prohibition is ineffective.

Agent Delivering Goods to a Broker: Liability and the Role of Custom
Ruling on agent liability with custom as overriding factor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on an agent's liability when goods are lost with a broker, distinguishing between cases where custom authorizes the delivery and cases where it does not.

Core Idea
Custom can discharge an agent from liability for goods lost with a broker if it is the prevalent practice.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq examines a critical refinement in Hanafi law: while the default ruling holds an agent liable for handing over another's property without explicit permission, custom can transform this into an implied authorization. The commentator restricts the relied-upon view (al-mukhtār) to non-customary situations, supported by a fatwa from al-Fatāwā al-Khairiyya and the well-known maxims on custom. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly determining dāmān (liability) in commercial agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The matn (base text) states that if an agent for sale gives the sold item to a broker (dallāl) and it is lost, the agent is not liable. This is because the agent is considered an original party (aṣīl) in delivery.
This view is from the author of Kanz al-Daqā’iq, not necessarily the relied-upon position.
2
Contrast with the chosen view (al-mukhtār)
The commentator notes that the chosen view in al-Bazzāziyya is liability (ḍamān). The reason: the agent handed over property of another (the principal) without permission.
This reflects a stricter view protecting the principal’s property.
3
Understand the commentator’s restriction
The commentator restricts the liability view to cases where no custom exists. If it is customary for such goods to be given to brokers, the agent is deemed authorized (ma’dhūn) by custom, and thus not liable.
Custom operates as an implicit condition (ka al-mashrūṭ shartan).
4
Examine the supporting evidence
The passage cites a fatwa from al-Fatāwā al-Khairiyya involving merchants sending goods and prices via trusted carriers based on custom, and the sender is not liable if the carrier absconds. This is supported by the maxims 'al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shartan' and 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah'.
These maxims are fundamental in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh.
5
Distinguish from the qumquma case
The passage contrasts with mas’alat al-qumquma: there, an agent gives a vessel for repair and forgets to whom; he is not liable because it is similar to misplacing his own property. In the broker case, the goods are given for sale, and custom is the decisive factor.
The distinction helps define the scope of custom-based authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف من كونه أصيلاً في تسليم المبيع إلى أن الوكيل بالشراء يطالب بالثمن وإن لم يقبضه من الموكل وإلى أن وكيل البيع لو دفع المبيع إلى دلال ليعرضه على من يرغب فيه فغاب أو ضاع في يده لم يضمن لكن المختار الضمان كما في البزازية لكونه دفع ملك الغير بغير إذنه وإن كان أصيلاً في الحقوق وفي البزازية وكيل البيع قال بعته وسلمته من رجل لا أعرفه وضاع الثمن قال القاضي : يضمن لأنه لا يملك التسليم قبل قبض ثمنه. قوله : (لكن المختار (الضمان أقول : ينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تكن العادة جارية في ذلك، أما إذا كان شيئاً لا يبيعه الوكيل بنفسه بل يدفع في العادة إلى دلال ليعرضه على البيع لا يضمن بمقتضى العادة يكون مأذوناً بذلك. وفي الفتاوى الخيرية سئل فيما إذا جرت عادة التجار أن يبعث بعضهم إلى بعض بضاعة يبيعها ويبعث بثمنها مع من يختاره ويعتقد أمانته من المكارية بحيث اشتهر اشتهاراً شائعاً فيهم وباع المبعوث إليه البضاعة المبعوثة في مدينته وأرسل مع من اختاره ذلك بينهم منهم لباعثها ثمنها على دفعات متعددة حسبما تيسر له وأنكر المبعوث إليه بعض الدفعات، هل يكون القول قول باعث الثمن بيمينه وإن لم يعلم تفاصيل ذلك لطول المدة، أم لا بد له من البينة؟ أجاب : القول قوله بيمينه إذ له بعثه مع من يختاره ويراه أميناً لأنه أمين لم تبطل أمانته والحالة هذه بالإرسال مع من ذكر، وقد ذكر الزاهدي رامزاً (بخ) لبكر خواهرزاده جرت عادة حاكة الرستاق أنهم يبعثون الكرابيس إلى من يبيعها لهم في البلد ويبعث بأثمانها إليهم بيد من شاء ويراه أميناً، فإذا بعث البائع ثمن الكرابيس بيد شخص ظنه أميناً وأبق ذلك الرسول لا يضمن الباعث إذا كانت هذه العادة معروفة عندهم. قال أستاذنا رحمه الله تعالى وبه أجبت أنا وغيري .اهـ وقد عضد بقولهم المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً والعادة محكمة والعرف قاض إلى غير ذلك من كلامهم اهـ. ما في الخيرية قوله : (وهذه المسألة تخالف مسألة القمقمة قال في متفرقات الوكالة من التتارخانية عازياً للظهيرية : الوكيل إذا دفع قمقمة إلى إنسان لإصلاحها بأمر الوكيل ونسي من دفعها إليه لا يضمن. النوازل: وصار كالذي وضعه في موضع من داره ثم نسيه فلا ضمان عليه، كذا هذا اهـ.
And the author indicated from his being an original party in delivering the sold item that the agent for purchase is demanded for the price even if he did not receive it from the principal, and that the agent for sale, if he gave the sold item to a broker to display it to those who might want it, and it was lost or perished in his hand, he is not liable. But the chosen [view] is liability, as in Al-Bazzaziyya, because he handed over the property of another without his permission, even though he is original in rights. And in Al-Bazzaziyya: An agent for sale said, 'I sold it and delivered it to a man I do not know, and the price was lost.' The qadi said: He is liable because he does not have the authority to deliver before receiving its price. His statement: (But the chosen [view] is liability). I say: It should be restricted to when custom is not prevalent in that. As for if it is something that the agent does not sell by himself but rather he customarily gives it to a broker to display for sale, he is not liable by virtue of custom, as he is thereby authorized. And in Al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya: He was asked about the case where the custom of merchants is that some send to others goods to sell and send the price with someone they choose and trust among the carriers, such that it is widely known among them, and the addressee sold the sent goods in his city and sent with someone he chose from among them to the sender its price in multiple payments as was possible, and the addressee denied some payments. Is the statement of the sender of the price accepted on his oath even if he does not know the details due to the length of time, or must he provide evidence? He answered: His statement is accepted on his oath because he has the right to send with whomever he chooses and deems trustworthy, for he is a trustee and his trusteeship is not invalidated by sending with the mentioned person. And al-Zahidi mentioned, alluding to Bikr Khuwahrizadah: The custom of weavers of Rustaq is that they send coarse cloth to those who sell it for them in the city, and they send their prices to them via whomever they wish and deem trustworthy. If the seller sends the price of the cloth with a person he thinks is trustworthy, and that messenger absconds, the sender is not liable if this custom is known among them. Our teacher, may Allah have mercy on him, said: and with this I and others have given fatwa. End quote. And this is supported by their statements: 'What is known by custom is like a condition stipulated,' and 'Custom is authoritative,' and 'Urf judges,' and other statements of theirs. End quote from Al-Khairiyya. His statement: (And this issue differs from the issue of the qumquma). He said in Mufarraqat al-Wakalah from Al-TatarKhaniyya citing Al-Zahayriyya: If the agent gave a qumquma (a vessel) to a person to repair it by the agent's order and forgot who he gave it to, he is not liable. Al-Nawazil: And it becomes like one who placed it in a spot in his house and then forgot it, so there is no liability. Thus this.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability of an agent for sale when he hands over the sold goods to a broker (dallal) for display and the goods are lost. The author of the commentary initially states that the agent is not liable because he is an original party in delivery (asil fi al-taslim). However, the preferred view (al-mukhtar) in Al-Bazzaziyya is that the agent is liable because he handed over another's property without permission. The commentator then restricts this to cases where no custom exists. If it is customary for such goods to be given to brokers, the agent is not liable because custom implies permission. The passage then cites a lengthy fatwa from Al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya about merchants sending goods and prices through trusted carriers based on custom. If the custom is well-known, the sender is not liable if the carrier absconds. This is supported by the legal maxims 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan' (what is known by custom is like a stipulated condition) and 'al-ada muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative). The passage distinguishes this from the qumquma issue: in the qumquma case, an agent who gives a vessel for repair and forgets who he gave it to is not liable because it resembles placing it in his own house and forgetting. The difference is that in the qumquma case, the agent gave it for repair (a service), not for sale, and custom may not apply.

Key Terms
الدلال (al-dallāl): A broker or middleman who displays goods for sale and finds buyers.
مسألة القمقمة (mas'alat al-qumquma): A specific case where an agent gives a vessel (qumquma) to someone for repair and forgets to whom he gave it; he is not liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, when is an agent for sale NOT liable for goods handed to a broker that get lost?
A) When the agent is an original party in delivery.
B) When the principal explicitly forbade delivery to a broker.
C) When it is customary for such goods to be given to brokers.
D) When the broker is known to be trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
An agent's liability for goods lost with a broker depends on custom: if custom permits, the agent is deemed authorized and not liable; otherwise, he is liable for handing over another's property without permission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Buyer’s Right to Withhold the Price from the Principal
Explaining the buyer's right to prevent the principal from the price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that paying the agent discharges the buyer and apply the seller’s right to retain the sold item when the agent misappropriates the price.

Core Idea
Paying the agent is valid; the buyer is discharged even if the agent wastes the price.

In agency sales, who does the buyer owe the price to? This lesson clarifies that the agent is considered the original party for receiving the price. Once paid to the agent, the buyer is discharged even if the principal does not receive it. However, if the agent misappropriates, the seller (principal) has a remedy: he can retain the sold item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is paying the price to the agent. The ruling: it is valid and the agent cannot demand it again.
The agent is the original party in receipt.
2
Apply the principle of agency
Because the agent is the original party, the buyer may prevent the principal from demanding the price.
The buyer is a stranger to the principal regarding the price.
3
Consider the exception of agent insolvency
If the agent consumes the price while insolvent, the seller (principal) may retain the sold item and has no recourse against the buyer.
This is a remedy for the seller.
4
Note the practical consequence
The buyer is safe once he pays the agent; the seller bears the risk of agent default.
The buyer should ensure the agent is trustworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بتسليمها وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن وإن دفع إليه صح ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً.
يرجع القنية قولين فيما إذا قال وكيل الطلاق أنت طالق مني وقد فرع على رجوع الحقوق للموكل حكمين، ومنها أن وكيلها لا يلي قبض مهرها والوكيل بالخلع لا يلي قبض البدل كما في البزازية. ومنها أنه يصح ضمانه مهرها وتخير المرأة بين مطالبته أو الزوج فإذا أخذت من الوكيل لا على الزوج؛ كذا في البزازية. وفيها : وكيل الخلع خالع وضمن صح وإن لم تأمره المرأة بالضمان وكذا يرجع قبل الأداء ا هـ . وأشار بالكاف في قوله «كالنكاح» إلى بقية أفراد هذا النوع ولذا قال في الهداية : من أخواته العتق على مال والكتابة والصلح على إنكار والهبة والتصدق والإعارة والإيداع والرهن والإقراض لأن الحكم فيها يثبت بالقبض وأنه يلاقي محلاً مملوكاً للغير فلا يجعل أصيلاً، وكذا إذا كان الوكيل من جانب الملتمس، وكذا الشركة والمضاربة إلا أن التوكيل بالاستقراض باطل حتى لا يثبت الملك للموكل بخلاف الرسالة فيه ا هـ . ومن هذا النوع الوكيل بالقبض وقدمنا أحكامه. وفي المجتبى : وكله أن يرتهن عبد فلان بدينه أو يستعيره له أو يستقرض له ألفاً فإنه يضيف العقد إلى موكله دون نفسه فيقول إن زيداً يستقرض منك كذا أو يسترهن عبدك أو يستعير منك ولو قال هب لي أو أعرني أو أقرضني أو تصدق علي فهو للوكيل ا هـ .
قوله : (وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن لكونه أجنبياً عن الحقوق لرجوعها إلى الوكيل أصالة، وقدمنا أحكام قبض الثمن وأنه لا فرق بين حضرة الوكيل وغيبته وإن وصى الوكيل ترجع الحقوق إليه بعد موته لا إلى الموكل. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن الموكل لو كان دفع الثمن إلى الوكيل فاستهلكه وهو معسر كان للبائع حبس المبيع ولا مطالبة له على الموكل، فإن لم ينقد الموكل الثمن إلى البائع باع القاضي الجارية بالثمن إذا رضيا وإلا فلا؛ كذا في بيوع خزانة المفتين قوله : (وإن دفع إليه صح ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً لأن نفس الثمن المقبوض حق الموكل وقد وصل إليه ولا فائدة في الأخذ منه ثم في الدفع إليه، ولهذا لو كان للمشتري على الموكل دين تقع المقاصة، ولو كان له عليهما دين تقع المقاصة بدين الموكل دون دين الوكيل، وبدين الوكيل إذا كان وحده عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لكونه يملك الإبراء عنه عندهما ولكنه بضمنه للموكل في الفصلين ؛ كذا في الهداية ولو أبراه عن الثمن معاً برئ المشتري بإبراء الموكل دون وكيله فلا رجوع على الوكيل كذا في النهاية. ويستفاد من وقوع المقاصة بدين الوكيل
With its delivery, and the buyer may prevent the principal from the price, and if he pays to him, it is valid, and the agent cannot demand it a second time. The Qunya refers to two opinions regarding when the agent in divorce says 'You are divorced from me', and he derived from the return of rights to the principal two rulings: one of them is that her agent is not authorized to receive her dower, and the agent in khul' is not authorized to receive the compensation, as in al-Bazzaziyya. And another is that his guarantee of her dower is valid, and the woman may choose between demanding from him or the husband; if she takes from the agent, she cannot recover from the husband; thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And in it: The agent in khul' gave khul' and guaranteed it, and it is valid even if the woman did not order him to guarantee, and likewise he can recover before payment, etc. And he indicated by the kaaf in his statement 'like marriage' the remaining individuals of this type. Therefore al-Hidaya said: Among its siblings are emancipation for money, kitaba, sulh on denial, gift, charity, loan for use, deposit, pawn, and loan, because the ruling in these is established by receipt and it meets a property owned by another, so he is not made an original party. Similarly if the agent is from the side of the applicant. Similarly partnership and mudaraba, except that authorization to borrow is invalid, so ownership does not become established for the principal (muwakkil), unlike the message in it, etc. And from this type is the agent for receipt (al-wakil bil-qabd), and we have pre-sent its rulings. And in al-Mujtaba: He authorized him to pawn the slave of so-and-so for his debt, or to borrow him for his use, or to borrow a thousand for him; then he attributes the contract to his principal, not to himself, so he says 'Zaid borrows from you such-and-such' or 'pawns your slave' or 'borrows for use from you'. And if he said 'Give to me' or 'Lend me' or 'Borrow me' or 'Give charity to me', it is for the agent, etc. His statement: (And the buyer may prevent the principal from the price) because he is a stranger to the rights, as they revert to the agent as original party. And we have presented the rulings of receipt of the price, and that there is no difference between the presence of the agent and his absence. And if the agent makes a bequest, the rights revert to him after his death, not to the principal. And the author indicated that if the principal paid the price to the agent and he consumed it while being insolvent, the seller has the right to retain the sold item and has no claim against the principal. If the principal does not pay the price to the seller, the judge sells the slave girl for the price if both agree, otherwise not; thus in Buyu' Khizanat al-Muftiyin. His statement: (And if he pays to him, it is valid, and the agent cannot demand it a second time) because the same price received is a right of the principal and has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it from him and then paying it to him. For this reason, if the buyer had a debt against the principal, set-off occurs. And if he had a debt against both, set-off occurs with the principal's debt but not with the agent's debt. And with the agent's debt alone, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because he has the power to cancel it in their view, but he guarantees it to the principal in both cases; thus in al-Hidaya. And if he (the principal) releases him from the price as a whole, the buyer is released by the principal's release, not by the agent's release, so there is no recourse against the agent; thus in al-Nihaya. And it is derived from the occurrence of set-off with the agent's debt...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with several rulings related to agency in sales and other contracts. It first states that the buyer may prevent the principal (the seller or the person who appointed the agent) from taking the price because the rights revert to the agent as the original party in the transaction. If the buyer pays the price to the agent, it is valid and cannot be demanded again by the agent. The passage then discusses a ruling from al-Qunya regarding an agent in divorce who says 'You are divorced from me.' It explains that if the agent guarantees the dower or khul' compensation, it is valid even without the woman's permission. The woman may choose to demand payment from the agent or the husband. However, the wife's agent is not allowed to receive her dower, and the agent in khul' cannot receive the compensation. Next, the passage cites al-Hidaya listing transactions that are considered 'siblings' of marriage, i.e., transactions where the agent cannot act as an original party but must act as a messenger. These include: emancipation for money, kitaba (contractual emancipation), sulh on denial, gift, charity, loan for use, deposit, pawn, and loan. In all these, the ruling is established by receipt (qabd) and the subject matter is owned by another. The agent is not considered an original party. If the agent is from the side of the applicant, the same applies. Partnership and mudaraba are also mentioned, except that authorization to borrow is invalid. The passage then discusses the agent for receipt (wakil bil-qabd) and quotes al-Mujtaba: if a person is appointed to pawn, borrow for use, or take a loan, he must attribute the action to his principal (e.g., 'Zaid borrows from you'). If he says 'Give to me' or similar, it is for himself. Finally, the passage returns to the buyer's right to prevent the principal from the price and explains the details. If the principal pays the price to the agent and the agent consumes it while insolvent, the seller may retain the sold item and has no recourse against the principal. If the principal does not pay, the judge may sell the item if both agree. The passage also discusses set-off (muqassa): if the buyer has a debt against the principal, it offsets; if against both, only the principal's debt offsets; if against the agent alone, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, set-off occurs because the agent can cancel the debt, but the agent must guarantee it to the principal. If the principal releases the buyer from the price, the buyer is freed; release by the agent does not help him.

Key Terms
حبس المبيع (habs al-mabīʿ): The seller's right to retain the sold item until payment of the price, even if the price was paid to the agent and lost.
وكيل بالقبض (wakīl bil-qabḍ): An agent appointed to receive (qabd) a thing, such as the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer pays the price to the agent, and the agent consumes it while insolvent, what is the seller’s remedy?
A) The seller can demand the price from the buyer again.
B) The seller may retain the sold item (habs al-mabi') and has no claim against the buyer.
C) The seller must sue the agent for the price.
D) The sale becomes void and the seller takes back the item.

Key Takeaway
The buyer who pays the agent is discharged; the seller’s remedy is to retain the sold item, not to demand the price from the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Set-off with the Agent’s Debt and Agent’s Guarantee in Khul‘
Explaining set-off with agent's debt and agent's guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on set-off (muqassa) with the agent’s debt and explain the validity of the agent’s guarantee of dower/khul‘ compensation.

Core Idea
Set-off with the agent’s debt is valid because the agent can release it.

This lesson explores nuanced agency rulings: when the buyer has a debt against the agent, can it be offset against the price? According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, yes, because the agent has the power to cancel the debt. Additionally, the agent in khul‘ or divorce can guarantee the compensation even without the woman’s permission, and she may choose whom to demand payment from.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: set-off with agent’s debt
The buyer has a debt against the agent, and the agent owes the price to the principal. Can the debts be offset?
This is a situation of muqassa.
2
State the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Set-off occurs because the agent can cancel (release) the debt. The agent then guarantees the debt to the principal.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Consider the opposite scenario
If the buyer has a debt against both principal and agent, only the principal’s debt offsets; the agent’s debt does not offset against the price.
Because the agent’s debt is separate.
4
Examine the agent’s guarantee of dower/khul‘
The agent in divorce or khul‘ can validly guarantee the compensation even without the woman’s permission. She may choose to demand from the agent or the husband.
This applies to both dower (mahr) and khul‘ compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The passage then discusses the agent for receipt (wakil bil-qabd) and quotes al-Mujtaba: if a person is appointed to pawn, borrow for use, or take a loan, he must attribute the action to his principal (e.g., 'Zaid borrows from you'). If he says 'Give to me' or similar, it is for himself. Finally, the passage returns to the buyer's right to prevent the principal from the price and explains the details. If the principal pays the price to the agent and the agent consumes it while insolvent, the seller may retain the sold item and has no recourse against the principal. If the principal does not pay, the judge may sell the item if both agree. The passage also discusses set-off (muqassa): if the buyer has a debt against the principal, it offsets; if against both, only the principal's debt offsets; if against the agent alone, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, set-off occurs because the agent can cancel the debt, but the agent must guarantee it to the principal. If the principal releases the buyer from the price, the buyer is freed; release by the agent does not help him.

Key Terms
مقاصة (muqassa): Set-off of debts, where a debt owed to the buyer by the agent is offset against the price due.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; here, the agent's guarantee of the dower or khul' compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when can set-off (muqassa) occur with the agent's debt?
A) Never, because the agent does not own the price.
B) Always, regardless of the agent's ability to release.
C) Only if the agent has the power to release the debt.
D) Only if the principal agrees.

Key Takeaway
Set-off with the agent’s debt is valid in the Hanafi school because the agent can release it; the agent must then compensate the principal.

Buyer's Right to Pay Principal Directly
Commentary on the matn line: 'And the buyer may prevent the principal from the price.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer pays the price directly to the principal and explain why the agent cannot demand payment again.

Core Idea
The buyer may pay the principal directly; the price is the principal's right and the agent's claim is discharged.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that the buyer has the option to pay the price directly to the principal, and that such payment is valid and discharges the buyer from the agent's claim. The source is Ibn Nujaym's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We will understand the rationale behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is the buyer paying the purchase price directly to the principal (the owner who appointed the agent). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih), and the agent cannot demand payment a second time.
The text states: 'And if he pays him it is valid and the agent does not demand him a second time.'
2
Understand the legal capacity of the buyer
The buyer is a stranger (ajnabi) to the rights that revert to the agent originally. The agent is the original recipient of the price, but the price ultimately belongs to the principal.
3
Trace the reason (ta'lil)
The reason is that 'the price itself is the right of the principal and has reached him.' Since the principal's right is fulfilled, there is no need for payment to the agent.
4
Consider the case of agent's insolvency
If the principal had paid the price to the agent and the agent consumed it while insolvent, the seller has the right to detain the sold item and cannot sue the principal. This shows the separation of liabilities.
5
Apply to a practical scenario
Example: A appoints B as agent to sell his car. C buys the car and pays the price directly to A. This payment is valid, and B cannot demand payment from C. If C also owes A a debt from another transaction, a set-off occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن لكونه أجنبياً عن الحقوق لرجوعها إلى الوكيل أصالة، وقدمنا أحكام قبض الثمن وأنه لا فرق بين حضرة الوكيل وغيبته وإن وصى الوكيل ترجع الحقوق إليه بعد موته لا إلى الموكل. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن الموكل لو كان دفع الثمن إلى الوكيل فاستهلكه وهو معسر كان للبائع حبس المبيع ولا مطالبة له على الموكل، فإن لم ينقد الموكل الثمن إلى البائع باع القاضي الجارية بالثمن إذا رضيا وإلا فلا؛ كذا في بيوع خزانة المفتين قوله : (وإن دفع إليه صح ولا يطالبه الوكيل (ثانياً لأن نفس الثمن المقبوض حق الموكل وقد وصل إليه ولا فائدة في الأخذ منه ثم في الدفع إليه، ولهذا لو كان للمشتري على الموكل دين تقع المقاصة، ولو كان له عليهما دين تقع المقاصة بدين الموكل دون دين الوكيل، وبدين الوكيل إذا كان وحده عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لكونه يملك الإبراء عنه عندهما ولكنه بضمنه للموكل في الفصلين ؛ كذا في الهداية ولو أبراه عن الثمن معاً برئ المشتري بإبراء الموكل دون وكيله فلا رجوع على الوكيل كذا في النهاية. ويستفاد من وقوع المقاصة بدين الوكيل أودعني لفلان. وعلى هذا فقولهم التوكيل بالاستقراض باطل معناه أنه في الحقيقة رسالة لا وكالة فلو أخرج الكلام مخرج الوكالة لم يصح بل لا بد من إخراجه مخرج الرسالة كما قلنا. وبه علم أن ذلك غير خاص بالاستقراض بل كل ما كان تمليكاً إذا كان الوكيل من جهة طالب التملك لا المملك، فإن التوكيل بالإقراض والإعارة صحيح لا بالاستقراض والاستعارة بل هو رسالة. هذا ما ظهر لي فتأمله قوله : (وقد فرع) أي المصنف.
من جهة
أن الوكيل لو باع من دائنه بدينه صح وبرئ وضمن الوكيل لموكله وهي في الذخيرة. أطلقه له فإنه يبرأ استحساناً كما في
كله
ذلك فشمل ما إذا نهاه الوكيل عن الدفع إلى موكـ ومع دفع البزازية. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن المسلم إليه لو دفع المسلم فيه إلى الموكل فإنه يبرأ ولو امتنع من دفعه إليه له ذلك كما في البزازية وإلى أن المأذون كالوكيل كما في البزازية. وذكر أبو بكر لا يملك المولى قبض ديون عبده المأذون إذا غاب لأنه فوق الوكيل لأنه يتصرف لنفسه والوكيل لغيره، وفي الوكيل إذا غاب لا يملك فالمأذون أولى، ومع ذلك لو قبضه المولى يبرأ المديون استحساناً إن لم يكن على العبد دين وإن كان عليه دين لا يبرأ لأن الحق للغرماء والمولى كالأجنبي ا هـ . والله تعالى أعلم.
His saying: 'And the buyer has the right to prevent the principal from the price' because he is a stranger to the rights as they revert to the agent originally, and we have presented the rulings of receipt of the price and that there is no difference between the presence of the agent and his absence, and if the agent makes a bequest, the rights revert to him after his death, not to the principal. And the author indicated that if the principal had paid the price to the agent and he consumed it while insolvent, the seller has the right to detain the sold item and there is no claim against the principal, and if the principal does not pay the price to the seller, the judge sells the slave-girl for the price if they both agree, otherwise not; as in the sales of Khizanat al-Muftin. His saying: 'And if he pays him it is valid and the agent does not demand him a second time' because the price itself is the right of the principal and has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it from him and then paying to him; for this reason, if the buyer has a debt against the principal, a set-off occurs; and if he has a debt against both of them, set-off occurs with the principal's debt not the agent's debt; and with the agent's debt alone, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because he has the power to discharge it in their view, but he guarantees it for the principal in both cases; as in al-Hidayah. And if he discharges him from the price altogether, the buyer is discharged by the principal's discharge not by the agent's, so there is no recourse against the agent, as in al-Nihayah. And it is understood from the occurrence of set-off with the agent's debt that he is like a depository. And on this basis, their statement that agency for borrowing is void means that in reality it is a message not agency; if he expresses it as agency it is not valid, but he must express it as a message as we said. And by this it is known that this is not specific to borrowing; rather, everything that is a transfer of ownership when the agent is from the side of the seeker of ownership, not the owner, then agency for lending and loan for use is valid, not for borrowing and borrowing for use; rather it is a message. This is what appeared to me, so reflect. His saying: 'And he branched' i.e., the author. From the side that if the agent sold to his debtor for his debt, it is valid and he is discharged and the agent guarantees for his principal, and it is in al-Dhakhirah. He made it absolute for him, so he is discharged by istihsan as in all of that, so it includes if the agent prohibited him from paying to the principal and with payment, al-Bazzaziyyah. And the author indicated that if the one to whom the salam is given pays the muslam fihi to the principal, he is discharged, and if he refuses to pay it to him, he has the right to do so, as in al-Bazzaziyyah; and that the ma'dhun is like the agent, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And Abu Bakr mentioned that the master does not have the right to receive the debts of his ma'dhun slave if he is absent because he is above the agent, since he acts for himself while the agent acts for others; and for the agent if he is absent, he does not have the right, so the ma'dhun is even more so; nevertheless, if the master receives it, the debtor is discharged by istihsan if the slave has no debt; but if he has debt, he is not discharged because the right belongs to the creditors and the master is like a stranger. End. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (expansion probe) on the matn line from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'And the buyer may prevent the principal from the price, and if he pays him it is valid and the agent does not demand him a second time.' The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, explains the reasoning: the buyer is a stranger to the rights that revert to the agent originally. The agent is the rightful recipient of the price. The commentator then branches into multiple subsidiary rulings: (1) If the principal paid the agent but the agent squandered the money while insolvent, the seller may detain the sold item and cannot sue the principal. (2) If the buyer pays the principal directly, it is valid and the agent cannot demand payment again because the price belongs to the principal and has reached him. (3) If the buyer owes a debt to the principal, a set-off (muqasa) occurs. If he owes to both, set-off is with the principal's debt only. If he owes only to the agent, according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, set-off occurs because the agent has the power to discharge, but the agent then guarantees the amount to the principal. (4) If the principal discharges the buyer from the price, the buyer is freed; the agent's discharge is ineffective. (5) The commentator reiterates that agency for borrowing is a message, not agency. (6) If the agent sells to his own debtor for the debt, it is valid and the agent guarantees the principal. (7) The one receiving salam (muslam ilayhi) can pay the principal directly and be discharged. (8) A ma'dhun slave is like a agent but higher; the master cannot receive his debts during his absence unless the slave has no debts. The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
الموكل (muwakkil): Principal: the person who appoints an agent.
الوكيل (wakil): Agent: the person appointed to act on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence if the buyer pays the price directly to the principal?
A) The payment is invalid and the agent may demand payment again.
B) The payment is valid and the agent cannot demand payment again.
C) The principal must return the money to the buyer.
D) The agent is discharged from his agency.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer may pay the purchase price directly to the principal, and this payment is valid and discharges the buyer from the agent's claim.

Set-off, Discharge, and Special Cases in Agency Sales
Commentary on subsidiary rulings branching from the main matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of set-off (muqasa) when the buyer owes debts to the principal and/or agent, and distinguish the rulings for agency and ma'dhun (authorized slave).

Core Idea
Set-off occurs with the principal's debt alone; if only an agent's debt, set-off is valid but agent guarantees to principal.

This lesson continues from the previous one, delving into the subsidiary rulings that the commentator Ibn Nujaym derives from the main ruling. These include set-off when the buyer owes debts to the principal or agent, discharge by the principal vs. agent, and the special status of the ma'dhun (authorized slave). Understanding these branches is essential for applying the law in complex transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the set-off scenarios
The text discusses three scenarios: (1) buyer owes debt to principal only → set-off occurs. (2) buyer owes debt to both principal and agent → set-off with principal's debt only. (3) buyer owes debt to agent only → set-off occurs according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but agent guarantees to principal.
This shows the priority of the principal's right.
2
Understand the agent's guarantee
When set-off occurs with the agent's debt, the agent must guarantee the amount to the principal because the agent's debt is not directly owed to the principal.
3
Analyze discharge (ibra)
If the principal discharges the buyer from the price, the buyer is released. The agent's discharge is ineffective because the price is the principal's right.
4
Compare agency with ma'dhun (authorized slave)
The ma'dhun is above an agent in legal capacity because he acts for himself. The master cannot receive debts of the ma'dhun if he is absent, unless the slave has no debts.
5
Apply to a practical scenario
Example: Buyer owes $100 to principal and $50 to agent from separate transactions. When buying an item through the agent, set-off applies to principal's debt only. The agent's $50 remains separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المقاصة (muqasa): Set-off: mutual cancellation of debts.
الإبراء (ibra): Discharge: a release from a debt.
المأذون (ma'dhun): Authorized slave: a slave permitted by his master to trade.

According to this passage, if the buyer owes a debt only to the agent, and a set-off occurs, what is the consequence for the agent?
A) The agent's debt is simply cancelled.

D) The buyer must pay both the principal and the agent.

Key Takeaway
In set-off involving agent's debts, the agent must guarantee the principal; only the principal's discharge is effective; and agency for borrowing is a message, not true agency.

The Three Types of Ignorance (Jahala) in Agency for Sale
Opening of the chapter on agency for sale and purchase with the classification of ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ignorance in agency into fahisha, yasira, and mutawassita, and state whether the agency is valid or invalid for each type, with and without price specification.

Core Idea
Agency is valid if the item's species is known, but invalid if only the genus is known; intermediate ignorance requires a price to become valid.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains a crucial principle for agency contracts: the degree of uncertainty about the subject matter determines validity. He classifies ignorance into three levels – gross, light, and intermediate – providing clear examples. Understanding this tripartite division helps you apply the correct ruling when drafting or reviewing sale agencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of jahala
From the commentary, list the three types: fahisha (gross), yasira (light), and mutawassita (intermediate). Note the Arabic terms.
These are defined by the level of specification: genus vs species vs intermediate.
2
Match each type to its example from the text
Gross: 'buy a cloth' without specifying type. Light: 'buy a horse' or 'Herawi cloth' without price. Intermediate: 'buy a slave' or 'buy a house' without price.
The examples are from the matn and al-Kifayah.
3
Determine the validity ruling for each type
Gross: invalid even if price is stated. Light: valid even without price. Intermediate: valid only if price is specified; otherwise invalid.
The commentary explicitly states these rulings.
4
Explain the rationale behind each ruling
Gross ignorance (unknown genus) makes the subject matter undefined. Light ignorance (known genus but unknown species) is tolerated due to istihsan. Intermediate ignorance (known genus but not species) is cured by specifying price, which indicates the desired type.
Price acts as a takhsis (specification) for intermediate cases.
5
Apply the classification to a new scenario
Consider agency to buy 'a car' (genus only, gross), 'a Toyota Camry' (species, light), or 'a car from a specific year' (intermediate, needs price).
Practice identifying the type before deriving the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء
أمره بشراء ثوب هروي أو فرس أو بغل صح سمى ثمناً أو لا بشراء دار أو عبد
جاز ان سمي ثمناً وإلا فلا لأنه بتقدير الثمن يصير النوع معلوماً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان ذلك الثمن يخصص نوعاً أو لا، وبه اندفع ما في
هـ.
باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء
قول المصنف : أمره) بشراء ثوب هروي الخ قال في الكفاية: الأصل أن الجهالة ثلاثة أنواع : فاحشة وهي جهالة الجنس كالتوكيل بشراء الثوب والدابة والرقيق وهي تمنع صحة الوكالة وإن بين الثمن. ويسيرة وهي جهالة النوع كالتوكيل بشراء الحمار والبغل والفرس والثوب الهروي والمروي فإنها لا تمنع صحة الوكالة وإن لم يبين الثمن ومتوسطة وهي بين الجنس والنوع كالتوكيل بشراء عبد وشراء أمة أو دار، فإن بين الثمن أو النوع تصح وتلحق بجهالة النوع، وإن لم يبين الثمن أو النوع لا .
Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. He ordered him to buy a Herawi cloth or a horse or a mule; it is valid whether he specified a price or not. For buying a house or a slave, it is valid if he specified a price, otherwise not, because with the specification of the price the type becomes known. He made it absolute so that it includes whether that price specifies a type or not; and by this what is in [the commentary] is refuted. The chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. The saying of the author: 'He ordered him to buy a Herawi cloth etc.' said in al-Kifayah: The principle is that ignorance is of three types: gross ignorance which is ignorance of the genus, like authorizing the purchase of a cloth, an animal, or a slave; it prevents validity of the agency even if the price is stated. And light ignorance which is ignorance of the type, like authorizing the purchase of a donkey, a mule, a horse, a Herawi or Marwi cloth; it does not prevent validity even if the price is not stated. And intermediate ignorance which is between genus and type, like authorizing the purchase of a slave, a female slave, or a house; if he specifies the price or the type, it is valid and follows the ruling of light ignorance; if he does not specify the price or the type, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on agency for sale and purchase (bâb al-wakâla bil-bay‘ wa al-shirâ’) from Kanz al-Daqâ’iq with commentary from al-Bahr al-Râ’iq. The matn states that if the principal orders the agent to buy a specific type of cloth (Herawi) or a horse or a mule, the agency is valid whether or not a price is named. But if he orders to buy a house or a slave, it is valid only if a price is named; otherwise invalid. The commentary explains the rationale: specifying the price makes the type (slave/house) known. The commentator broadens the rule to include cases where the price indicates a specific type. He then quotes from al-Kifâyah a classification of ignorance (jahâlah) in agency. Gross ignorance (fâhishah) is ignorance of the genus (jins), e.g., 'buy a cloth' without specifying type; this invalidates the agency even if a price is given. Light ignorance (yasîrah) is ignorance of the species (naw‘), e.g., 'buy a horse' or 'a Herawi cloth' without specifying price; this does not invalidate. Intermediate ignorance (mutawassiṭah) is between genus and species, like 'buy a slave' or 'buy a house'; if price is named it becomes like light ignorance and is valid; if not, it is invalid. This classification is key to understanding the limits of valid agency.

Key Terms
جهالة فاحشة (jahalah fahishah): Gross ignorance: ignorance of the genus (jins) of the item to be purchased, e.g., 'buy a cloth' without specifying type; it invalidates agency.
جهالة يسيرة (jahalah yasirah): Light ignorance: ignorance of the species (naw') within a known genus, e.g., 'buy a horse' without specifying which horse; it does not invalidate agency.
جهالة متوسطة (jahalah mutawassitah): Intermediate ignorance: ignorance between genus and species, e.g., 'buy a slave' without specifying male/female or type; it requires price specification to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on agency to purchase a slave without specifying a price?
A) It is valid because the genus is known.
B) It is valid only if the agent is a merchant.
C) It is invalid because it is intermediate ignorance.
D) It is valid by istihsan.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an agency for purchase depends on the level of certainty about the item: only light ignorance (known species) is tolerated without price; gross and intermediate ignorance require further specification.
