Agent's Liability for Destroyed Goods After Retention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for destroyed goods after agent's retention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the liability of an agent for destroyed goods after retention, explain the reasoning behind each position (Sahibayn, Abu Yusuf, Zufar), and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When an agent's purchased goods are destroyed after retention, the price is forfeited per Sahibayn, but Abu Yusuf treats it as a pledge and Zufar as usurpation.

Agency law (al-wakalah) often raises questions about possession and liability. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a classic scenario where an agent exceeds his instructions and the goods are destroyed. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the agent is like a seller, a pledgee, or a usurper, with different consequences for the principal's obligation to pay. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply qiyas and recognize the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
Outline the scenario
State the facts: principal says buy 10 lbs for 1 dirham, agent buys 20 lbs. After retention, goods are destroyed. The principal has a choice to take the excess or leave it.
The excess is a 'description' (wasf) not compensated separately.
2
Identify the agent's right of retention
The agent may retain the purchased item until the principal pays the price. This right is established in Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
State the three positions
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): price drops entirely. Abu Yusuf: like a pledge, loss is lesser of value or price. Zufar: like usurpation, agent guarantees full value.
The text summarizes: 'عندهما يسقط الثمن بهلاكه، وعند أبي يوسف يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومن الثمن، وعند زفر يضمن جميع قيمته'.
4
Analyze the qiyas for each
Sahibayn analogize to a seller's retention for price. Abu Yusuf analogizes to a pledge. Zufar argues prevention without right is usurpation.
These analogies derive from different views of the agent's possession.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical outcome: Under Sahibayn, principal pays nothing. Under Abu Yusuf, pays lesser of value/price. Under Zufar, agent pays full value to principal.
Knowing this difference is critical for advising clients.
6
Identify the mufta bihi
The author indicates the Sahibayn view is relied upon, stating the contract dissolves in respect of both parties (ينفسخ في حق الموكل والوكيل).
This tarjih is based on the nature of the agency contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء ولو وكله بشراء عشرة أرطال لحم بدرهم فاشترى عشرين رطلاً لو ذهبت عينه عنده بعد حبسه لم يسقط شيء من الثمن لأنه وصف والأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء لكن يخير الموكل إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك؛ كذا في زيادات قاضيخان ويكون مضموناً ضمان الرهن عند أبي يوسف، وضمان الغصب عند زفر لأنه منع بغير حق. ولهما أنه بمنزلة البائع منه فكان حبسه لاستيفاء الثمن فيسقط بهلاكه، ولأبي يوسف أنه مضمون بالحبس للاستيفاء بعد إن لم يكن وهو الرهن بعينه بخلاف المبيع لأن البيع ينفسخ بهلاكه، وههنا لا ينفسخ أصل العقد قلنا ينفسخ في حق الموكل والوكيل كما إذا رده الموكل بعيب ورضي الوكيل به. أشار المؤلف إلى أن الوكيل له حبس المبيع لاستيفاء الثمن سواء أداه إلى البائع أو لا. وقيد بالوكيل بالشراء لأن الوكيل باستئجار الدار إذا استأجر للموكل داراً سنة بمائة درهم وشرط التعجيل أو لم يشترط وقبض الوكيل الدار لا يكون له أن يحبسها من الموكل بالأجر، فإن حبسها حتى مضت المدة ذكر في بعض الروايات أن الأجر على الوكيل، ثم الوكيل يرجع على الموكل ولا يسقط الأجر عن الموكل بحبس الوكيل بخلاف ما إذا غصبها غاصب فإن ثمة لا يجب الأجر على الموكل ولا على الوكيل وفي بعضها يسقط الأجر عن الموكل بحبس الوكيل؛ في الخانية إلى هنا والحاصل في مسألة الاختلاف أن عندهما يسقط الثمن بهلاكه، وعند أبي يوسف يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومن الثمن حتى لو كان الثمن أكثر من قيمته رجع الوكيل بذلك الفضل على موكله، وعند زفر يضمن جميع قيمته. وفي بيوع البزازية : وإن نقد الوكيل بالشراء الثمن من ماله ثم لقيه الموكل في بلد آخر والمشتري ليس عنده وطلب منه الثمن فأبى إلا أن يسلم المشتري، فإن كان الأمر طالبه بتسليمه حين كان المشتري بحضرتهما ولم يسلمه حتى يقبض الثمن له أن لا يدفع الثمن حتى يقبض لأنه امتنع عن تسليم المشتري حال حضرته فلأن يمنع حال غيبته، وإن كان الأمر لم يطلبه منه حال حضرة المشتري ليس له أن يمتنع عن دفع الثمن لأنه صار ديناً في ذمة الآمر المشتري اه.
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. If he authorized him to buy ten pounds of meat for a dirham, and he bought twenty pounds, if the item perished in his possession after his retention, nothing of the price is dropped because it is a description and descriptions are not compensated by anything, but the principal has a choice: if he wishes, he takes it with the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Thus in Ziyadat Qadi Khan. And it is guaranteed with the guarantee of a pledge according to Abu Yusuf, and with the guarantee of usurpation according to Zufar because he prevented without right. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is like the seller from him, so his retention was to collect the price, so it falls by its destruction. According to Abu Yusuf, it is guaranteed by retention for collection after it was not, and it is the very pledge, unlike the sold item because the sale is dissolved by its destruction, and here the original contract is not dissolved. We say it is dissolved in respect of the principal and agent, as if the principal returned it due to a defect and the agent was pleased. The author indicated that the agent has the right to retain the sold item to collect the price, whether he paid it to the seller or not. And he restricted it to the agent for purchase because the agent for renting a house, if he rented for the principal a house for a year for one hundred dirhams and stipulated prompt payment or not, and the agent took possession of the house, he does not have the right to retain it from the principal for the rent. If he retained it until the period passed, it is mentioned in some narrations that the rent is upon the agent, then the agent recourse against the principal and the rent is not dropped from the principal by the agent's retention, unlike if a usurper usurped it, then the rent is not obligatory upon the principal nor upon the agent. And in some, the rent is dropped from the principal by the agent's retention. In al-Khaniyah up to here. The summary of the issue of disagreement is that according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the price falls by its destruction; according to Abu Yusuf, it perishes by the lesser of its value and the price, so if the price is more than its value, the agent returns that excess to his principal; according to Zufar, he guarantees all of its value. And in Buyu' al-Bazzaziyyah: If the agent for purchase paid the price from his own wealth, then met the principal in another city and the seller is not with him, and he requested the price from him but he refused unless he delivers the purchased item, if he (the principal) had requested delivery from him when the seller was present with them and he did not deliver until he receives the price, he has the right not to pay the price until he receives (the item) because he refrained from delivering the seller when he was present, so he should refrain when he is absent. And if he did not request it from him when the seller was present, he does not have the right to refrain from paying the price because it became a debt in the liability of the principal. End.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of agency for sale and purchase. It begins with a scenario: a principal instructs his agent to buy ten pounds of meat for one dirham, but the agent buys twenty pounds instead. The ruling, as stated in Ziyadat Qadi Khan, is that if the excess meat perishes after the agent retains it, the price is not reduced proportionally because the excess is a description, and descriptions are not compensated. However, the principal has the choice: he may take the entire twenty pounds at the full price (meaning he pays for twenty even though he only wanted ten) or he may refuse the transaction entirely. The passage then enters an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability of the agent if the purchased item is destroyed while in his possession. According to Abu Yusuf, the agent holds it as a pledge (رهن) for the price he paid, so if it is destroyed, the loss falls on the principal (the price is forfeited), similar to a pledge. According to Zufar, the agent is a usurper (غاصب) because he prevented the principal without right, so he guarantees the full value. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the agent is like a seller; since he retains for collection of the price, destruction nullifies the price (i.e., the principal does not have to pay). Abu Yusuf's view is that the agent's retention is like a pledge, so destruction extinguishes the debt up to the value. The author then confirms the agent's right to retain the purchased item until he receives the price from the principal, regardless of whether he already paid the seller. A distinction is made with an agent for rent: an agent who rents a house cannot retain it from the principal for the rent; if he does, the rent may become his liability per some narrations. The passage concludes with a practical scenario from Buyu' al-Bazzaziyyah about an agent who paid the price from his own money and later demands reimbursement from the principal; the principal's obligation depends on whether he previously demanded delivery when the seller was present. The core ruling is that the agent has a right of retention (حبس) for the price, and the liability rules for destruction follow the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The person authorized to act on behalf of another (principal) in a contract of sale.
حبس (habs): Retention of the purchased item by the agent until payment of the price by the principal.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee for the value of the item if destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent buys twenty pounds of meat when authorized to buy ten, and the meat is destroyed while in the agent's possession after he retained it, what is the ruling according to Abu Yusuf?
A) The principal must pay the full price for twenty pounds.
B) The price is forfeited completely; the principal pays nothing.
C) The agent guarantees the full value of the meat.
D) The agent is liable for the lesser of the price and the value, and if the price is more, the agent returns the excess to the principal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency law, when goods are destroyed after the agent's retention, the price is forfeited according to the relied-upon view, but other views exist based on different analogies.

Agent's Separation in Sarf and Salam: The Ruling and Its Refinement
Critical refinement (tahqiq) of the ruling on agent's separation in sarf and salam, resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the agent's separation in sarf and salam contracts, explain the internal Hanafi dispute on the principal's presence, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In sarf and salam, the agent's separation before qabd invalidates the contract, regardless of the principal's presence.

This lesson examines a critical refinement in Hanafi fiqh: while the base ruling is clear that separation before delivery invalidates sarf and salam, scholars debated whether the principal's presence alters this for an agent. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq navigates between conflicting opinions to settle on the relied-upon position. You will learn not only the ruling but also how later jurists resolve intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Ruling
Identify the general rule: in sarf and salam, the agent's separation from his counterpart before both items are delivered invalidates the contract. Note that the principal's separation is irrelevant because he is not the contracting party.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah; the agent is the immediate contracting party.
2
Identify the Ikhtilaf
Recognize the dispute: Some Hanafis (e.g., Khaharzadah, al-Ayni) hold that if the principal is present, the agent's separation is not considered. Others (al-Zayla'i, al-Hamawi) maintain it is always considered. Understand the basis of each view.
The dispute hinges on whether the agent is a 'deputy' or a 'principal' in the contract's rights.
3
Analyze the Proofs (Istidlal)
Follow al-Hamawi's argument: the agent is a deputy in the original contract but a principal in the rights (huquq). Hence, his separation matters regardless of the principal's presence. Contrast with al-Ayni's argument that the deputy's actions are attributed to the principal when he is present.
The key distinction: 'asil fi al-aqd' vs 'asil fi al-huquq'.
4
Apply the Tarjih from al-Minhah
See how the author resolves the issue: the relied-upon view is that liability (al-'uhdah) is on the principal when present. This reconciles the dispute: Khaharzadah's original restriction was based on this liability, but the base ruling remains that the agent's separation invalidates because the condition of tafarruq attaches to the contracting party.
The mufta bihi per the author is that the agent's separation always invalidates if it occurs before qabd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وتعتبر مفارقة الوكيل في الصرف والسلم فيبطل العقد إن فارق الوكيل صاحبه قبل القبض لوجود الافتراق عن غير قبض، ولا اعتبار بمفارقة الموكل لأنه ليس بعاقد والمستحق بالعقد قبض العاقد وهو الوكيل فيصح قبضه وإن كان لا يتعلق به الحقوق كالصبي والعبد المحجور عليه ولذا أطلق المؤلف رحمه الله وشمل ما إذا كان الموكل حاضراً أو غائباً، وما في النهاية من تقييده بما إذا كان الموكل غائباً، أما إذا كان حاضراً لم تعتبر مفارقة الوكيل قوله : (وما في النهاية من تقييده الخ مثل ما في النهاية في العيني وابن ملك ودرر البحار والجوهرة عن المستصفى. وقال الزيلعي بعد نقله عن النهاية وعزاه أي صاحب النهاية إلى خواهرزاده وهذا مشكل فإن الوكيل أصيل من باب البيع حضر الموكل العقد أو لم يحضر، ثم ذكر فيه ـ أي في النهاية ـ بعده فقال : المعتبر بقاء المتعاقدين في المجلس وغيبة الموكل لا تضر وعزاه إلى وكالة المبسوط وإطلاقه وإطلاق سائر الكتب دليل على أن مفارقة الموكل لا تعتبر أصلا ولو كان حاضراً اهـ. ورده العيني بأنه ليس بمشكل فإن الوكيل نائب عنه فإذا حضر الأصيل فلا يعتبر النائب اهـ. وتعقبه الحموي بأن الوكيل نائب في أصل العقد أصيل في الحقوق وحينئذ فلا اعتبار بحضرة الموكل اهـ. قلت : ومما يتضح به تزييف جواب العيني ما ذكره هو نفسه عند قول المصنف فيما سبق وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن من أن الموكل أجنبي عن العقد وحقوقه لأنها تتعلق بالعاقد على ما بينا اهـ؛ في حاشية مسكين وما استشكله الزيلعي استشكله صاحب العناية. وذكر في الحواشي السعدية أنه توارد مع الزيلعي في هذا الإشكال ثم نقل عبارة الزيلعي وقال : وعليك بالتأمل أقول وبالله التوفيق: الذي يقطع عرق الإشكال من أصله ما قدمناه عن المنح من أن المعتمد أن العهدة على الموكل دون الوكيل إذا حضر العقد وأنه أصح الأقاويل، فكلام الإمام خواهرزاده مبني على هذا لا على ما مشى عليه سابقاً من أنها على الوكيل وإن كان الموكل حاضراً وهو منشأ الإشكال، وبه اتضح الحال كذا والحمد لله وحده .
His saying: (And the separation of the agent is considered in currency exchange and salam; so the contract is invalidated if the agent separates from his counterpart before delivery, due to the occurrence of separation without delivery. And the separation of the principal is not considered because he is not the contracting party, and the one entitled by the contract is the delivery of the contracting party, which is the agent. So his delivery is valid, even if he does not have rights attached like the minor and the interdicted slave. Hence the author (may Allah have mercy on him) made it absolute and included whether the principal is present or absent. And what is in al-Nihayah of restricting it to when the principal is absent, but if he is present, the agent's separation is not considered. His saying: (And what is in al-Nihayah of restricting it, etc.) Like what is in al-Nihayah, in al-Ayni, Ibn Malik, Durar al-Bihar, and al-Jawharah from al-Mustasfa. And al-Zayla'i said after quoting from al-Nihayah and attributing it to Khaharzadah: This is problematic because the agent is a principal from the perspective of the sale, whether the principal attends the contract or not. Then he mentioned in it—i.e., in al-Nihayah—after it, saying: The relied-upon factor is the remaining of the two contracting parties in the session, and the absence of the principal does not harm. He attributed it to Wakalah of al-Mabsut, and its absoluteness and the absoluteness of other books indicate that the separation of the principal is not considered at all, even if he is present. End. And al-Ayni rejected it saying: It is not problematic, for the agent is his deputy; so when the principal is present, the deputy is not considered. End. And al-Hamawi criticized him (al-Ayni) saying: The agent is a deputy in the original contract but a principal in the rights; therefore, the presence of the principal is not considered. End. I say: And from what clarifies the refutation of al-Ayni's answer is what he himself mentioned at the saying of the author previously: 'And the purchaser may prevent the principal from the price,' that the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights because they pertain to the contracting party, as we explained. End. In Hashiyat Miskin, and what al-Zayla'i found problematic, the author of al-Inayah also found problematic. And it is mentioned in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah that he concurred with al-Zayla'i in this problem, then he transmitted al-Zayla'i's statement and said: You must reflect. I say: With Allah's success, what cuts the vein of the problem from its root is what we presented from al-Minhah: that the relied-upon is that the liability is on the principal, not the agent, when he attends the contract, and that is the soundest opinion. So the statement of Imam Khaharzadah is based on this, not on what he previously assumed that it is on the agent even if the principal is present, which is the source of the problem. And thus the matter became clear. So (it is) and praise be to Allah alone.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (tahqiq) of the ruling regarding the effect of the agent's separation in currency exchange (sarf) and salam contracts. The author begins by stating the general rule: in sarf and salam, the agent's separation from his counterpart before delivery of both exchanged items invalidates the contract, because the requirement of mutual delivery before separation (tafarruq qabl al-qabd) is violated. The principal's separation is irrelevant because the principal is not the contracting party; the agent is the actual party to the contract. Therefore, the agent's delivery is valid, even if he lacks certain legal capacities (like a minor or slave). This ruling is absolute, applying whether the principal is present or absent. However, al-Nihayah restricts this: the agent's separation is considered only if the principal is absent; if present, the agent's separation is not considered. The author then delves into a scholarly dispute: al-Zayla'i (in al-Nihayah) reports from Khaharzadah that this restriction is problematic because the agent is essentially the principal in the sale (asil min bab al-bay'), regardless of the principal's presence. Al-Ayni defends the restriction, arguing that the agent is a deputy, and when the principal is present, the deputy's actions are attributed to the principal, so the principal's presence overrides the need to consider the agent's separation. Al-Hamawi refutes al-Ayni by distinguishing: the agent is a deputy in the original contract but a principal in the rights (huquq) arising from the contract; thus, the presence of the principal does not affect the need to consider the agent's separation. The author sides with al-Hamawi, citing earlier commentary that the principal is a stranger to the contract's rights. Finally, the author resolves the issue by adopting the view from al-Minhah: the relied-upon position is that liability (al-'uhdah) is on the principal when he is present, not on the agent. Based on this, Khaharzadah's original statement (that the agent's separation is considered only if the principal is absent) is actually consistent, because if liability is on the principal, then the principal's presence makes him the focus, and the agent's separation becomes irrelevant. Thus, the apparent conflict is resolved. The core ruling remains: in sarf and salam, valid delivery must occur before separation; this condition applies to the agent as the contracting party, but the principal's presence may affect liability.

Key Terms
الصرف (al-sarf): Currency exchange contract where two different currencies are exchanged, requiring immediate delivery (qabd) before separation.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are delivered later; requires qabd of the price before separation.
مفارقة (mufaraqah): The physical parting of the contracting parties from the session of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contract is valid if the principal is present.
B) The contract is invalid because the agent's separation occurred before qabd.
C) The contract is valid only if the principal gives consent after separation.
D) The contract is suspended until the principal confirms.

Key Takeaway
In sarf and salam, the agent's separation before delivery of the required items invalidates the contract; the principal's presence does not affect this condition.

Agency for Accepting Salam: Invalid due to Shubhah
Ruling that agency is invalid for accepting salam from the Muslim ilayhi side

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency for accepting salam, explain the shubhah that invalidates it, and contrast it with valid agency for purchase.

Core Idea
Agency for accepting salam is invalid: the agent would own the capital, creating shubhah.

In a salam contract, the seller (Muslim ilayhi) receives payment in advance. May he appoint an agent to accept the contract on his behalf? The Hanafi school rules it impermissible. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: the disagreement on ownership transfer creates a legal doubt (shubhah) that breaks the analogy to valid agency for purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and conclusion
The act is appointing an agent to accept a salam contract on behalf of the seller (Muslim ilayhi). The Hanafi ruling is that it is not permissible (makruh tahrimi).
This is the mufta bihi position from later scholars.
2
Understand the objection by analogy
Some argue: agency for purchase is valid, where the price is on the principal but the agent is demanded to pay. Similarly, in salam, the agent could take the capital and owe delivery.
This qiyas appears plausible at first glance.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: shubhah
The Hanafi school rejects the analogy because of a fundamental difference: in purchase, ownership of the purchased item vests directly in the principal; in salam, if the agent accepts, ownership of the capital would initially vest in the agent due to scholarly disagreement on transfer.
This disagreement creates a shubhah (legal doubt) that necessitates impermissibility.
4
See the practical consequence
If the agent accepts salam, he becomes personally liable to deliver the goods; later transfer to the principal is a separate loan transaction. The contract is not valid as intended.
Thus the agent cannot act on behalf of the seller in accepting salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يجوز من جانب المسلم إليه بأخذ رأس المال عبارة الجوهرة بأن وكله يقبل له السلم وعبارة الهداية ومراده التوكيل بالإسلام دون قبول السلم قال الرملي : وقد تواردت الشراح وغيرهم على هذا قال في العناية : واعترض بأن قبول السلم عقد يملكه الموكل والواجب أن يملكه الوكيل حفظاً للقاعدة المذكورة عن الانتقاض وبأن التوكيل بالشراء جائز لا محالة والثمن يجب في ذمة الموكل والوكيل مطالب به فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المال للمسلم إليه والوكيل مطالب بتسليم المسلم فيه؟ وأجاب عن الإيرادين بجوابين ردهما الرملي ثم قال : ويختلج في صدري جواب لعله يكون صحيحاً إن شاء الله تعالى وهو أنه لما اختلف العلماء كما قرروه في الملك هل يثبت للموكل ابتداء أو للوكيل ثم ينتقل للموكل أثر هذا الاختلاف في المحل شبهة فأوجب عدم الجواز فيما القياس فيه
His saying: (And it is not permissible from the side of the Muslim ilayhi to take the capital. The expression of al-Jawharah: that he authorized him to accept salam for him. And the expression of al-Hidayah: and his intention is agency for salam (Islam) not acceptance of salam. Al-Ramli said: The commentators and others have concurred on this. He said in al-Inayah: And it was objected that acceptance of salam is a contract that the principal may perform, and it is necessary that the agent may perform it to preserve the mentioned rule from being violated, and that agency for purchase is absolutely permissible, and the price is obligatory in the principal's liability and the agent is demanded for it, so why is it not permissible that the capital be for the Muslim ilayhi and the agent is demanded for delivery of the subject matter? And he answered the two objections with two answers which al-Ramli rejected, then he said: And a response occurs in my mind which perhaps may be correct, Allah willing: that since the scholars differed, as they stated, whether ownership is established for the principal initially or for the agent and then transfers to the principal, this difference casts doubt (shubhah) on the matter, thus necessitating impermissibility where analogy would permit it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of agency in a salam contract from the perspective of the seller (Muslim ilayhi). The author states that it is not permissible for an agent to take the capital (i.e., receive the advance payment) on behalf of the Muslim ilayhi. The context: a person wants to sell goods on credit via salam; he appoints an agent to accept the salam contract. According to the text, this is invalid. Al-Ramli notes that commentators agree that the agency is only for giving salam (i.e., for the buyer to pay in advance), not for accepting salam (i.e., for the seller). Why? The basis: when the agent accepts salam, he becomes the contracting party. If he receives the price, the capital becomes his property, and the obligation to deliver the goods falls on his liability. Then when he transfers the goods to the principal, it becomes a separate transaction (like a loan). This creates a problematic structure. The text mentions an objection: if agency for purchase is valid (where the price is on the principal but the agent is demanded to pay), why not for salam? The answer given in al-Inayah is that there is a difference of opinion among scholars whether ownership (of the salam capital) transfers to the agent initially or to the principal. This difference creates a doubt (shubhah) that prevents the permissibility of such agency, even though analogy might allow it. The author (al-Ramli) offers this as a possible resolution. Thus, the ruling is that agency for accepting salam is not valid, preserving the integrity of the contract structure.

Key Terms
المسلم إليه (al-Muslim ilayhi): The seller in a salam contract who receives the price in advance and promises to deliver goods later.
قبول السلم (qabul al-salam): The acceptance of a salam offer, i.e., the contract formation from the seller's side.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or uncertainty that affects the permissibility of a transaction, here arising from scholarly disagreement on ownership transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the agent cannot take possession of the price.
B) Because it leads to a shubhah (doubt) due to differing views on whether the agent or the principal initially owns the capital.
C) Because salam contracts require the principal to be present.
D) Because the agent would become liable for the price.

Key Takeaway
Agency for accepting salam is invalid because the agent would initially own the capital, creating a shubhah that disrupts the contract structure.

Agency in Currency Exchange and Advance Sale
Explaining permissibility of agency in sarf and salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency in sarf and salam, distinguish between an agent and a messenger, and apply the qiyas that invalidates agency from the seller's side in salam.

Core Idea
Agency in sarf is valid from both sides; in salam only from the buyer's side.

This lesson examines two special sale contracts—currency exchange (sarf) and advance sale (salam)—through the lens of agency (wakalah). The Hanafi school treats the agent as a principal in rights and obligations, which has different implications depending on the contract type. Understanding these distinctions is critical for practitioners dealing with modern Islamic finance transactions such as spot currency trading or salam-based agricultural financing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts and their basic rules
Sarf is the exchange of gold/silver or currencies hand-to-hand; salam is a forward sale with spot payment for future delivery of fungible goods. Note that in sarf, possession must be immediate (hand-to-hand), while salam allows deferred delivery.
These rules are from general fiqh; the lesson focuses on agency implications.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on agency in each contract
Agency is valid in sarf from both the buyer's and seller's sides. In salam, agency is valid only from the buyer's side (rabb al-salam) for paying the capital or accepting the salam offer. Agency from the seller's side (muslim ilayh) to take the capital is invalid.
The text explicitly says 'mutilaq min al-janibayn' for sarf and 'la yajuz min janib al-muslim ilayh' for salam.
3
Explain the reason (qiyas) for the salam restriction
If the seller appoints an agent to take the capital, the subject matter (muslim fih) remains a debt in the agent's liability. This creates a situation where the agent sells something he does not possess, analogous to the prohibited 'sale of one's property on condition that the price is for another'.
The text quotes the qiyas: 'Wa la yajuz an yabia al-insan malahu bi-shart an yakuna thamanahu li-ghayrihi'.
4
Distinguish the agent (wakil) from a mere messenger (rasul)
An agent is considered a principal in rights and obligations arising from the contract; a messenger merely transmits the principal's will. In the context of these contracts, an agent's possession is his own, so taking capital by an agent from the seller's side invalidates the agency.
The text says the agent is 'asil fi al-huquq' whereas a messenger's acceptance is not valid because it is like possession by a non-contracting party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء ضعيف لكون الوكيل أصيلاً في الحقوق في البيع مطلقاً. وقيد بالوكيل لأن الرسول فيهما لا تعتبر مفارقته لأن الرسالة في العقد لا في القبض وينتقل كلامه إلى المرسل فصار قبض الرسول قبض غير العاقد فلم يصح، واستفيد من وضع المسألة صحة التوكيل بهما لأن كلاً منهما مما يباشره الموكل فيوكل فيه وهو في الصرف مطلق من الجانبين، وأما في السلم فيجوز من جانب رب السلم بدفع رأس المال أو بقبول السلم كما في الجوهرة، ولا يجوز من جانب المسلم إليه بأخذ رأس المال لأن الوكيل إذا قبض رأس المال بقي المسلم فيه في ذمته وهو مبيع ورأس المال ثمنه، ولا يجوز أن يبيع الإنسان ماله بشرط أن يكون ثمنه لغيره كما في بيع العين، وإذا بطل التوكيل كان الوكيل عاقداً لنفسه فيجب المسلم فيه في ذمته ورأس المال مملوك له وإذا سلمه إلى الآمر على وجه التمليك منه كان قرضاً، فلو قال المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى واسلام كما في المجمع بدل السلم لكان أولى لأن الإسلام خاص من رب السلم
Book of Agency: Chapter on Agency for Sale and Purchase. [The previous] is weak because the agent is a principal in rights in sale absolutely. It is restricted to the agent because for a messenger in both actions, his departure is not considered, because the messengership is in the contract not in the taking of possession, and his speech transfers to the sender, so the taking of possession by the messenger is the taking of possession by someone other than the contracting party, so it is not valid. It is derived from the formulation of the issue the validity of agency for both, because each of them is something the principal undertakes himself, so he may appoint an agent for it. As for currency exchange, it is absolutely valid from both sides. As for salam, it is valid from the side of the buyer of salam (rabb al-salam) by paying the capital or by accepting the salam, as in al-Jawhara. It is not valid from the side of the seller in salam (muslim ilayh) by taking the capital, because if the agent takes the capital, the subject matter of salam remains in his liability, and it is a sold item and the capital is its price. It is not permissible for a person to sell his property on condition that its price be for someone else, as in the sale of an 'ayn. If the agency is void, the agent becomes a contractor for himself, so the subject matter of salam is owed by him, and the capital is owned by him. If he gives it to the principal by way of ownership, it is a loan. If the author had said 'and Islam' as in al-Majma' instead of 'salam', it would have been better, because Islam is specific to the buyer of salam.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on agency (wakalah) for sale and purchase, specifically addressing two types of contracts: currency exchange (sarf) and advance sale (salam). The author begins by clarifying that the agent is considered a principal (asil) regarding the rights and obligations arising from the sale, meaning the agent is directly responsible for contractual duties. This is distinguished from a mere messenger (rasul), whose speech simply transmits the principal's will and whose acceptance or delivery is not legally effective. For agency in sarf, it is valid from both the buyer's and seller's sides, as both parties undertake the exchange themselves. For salam, however, agency is only valid from the side of the buyer (rabb al-salam), who appoints an agent to pay the capital or accept the salam. Agency is not valid from the side of the seller (muslim ilayh) to take the capital, because taking the capital would leave the subject matter (muslim fih) as a debt in the agent's liability, effectively making the agent a seller of what he does not possess or a sale with the condition that the price goes to another. The author notes that the term 'Islam' (surrender) would be more appropriate than 'salam' in this context, as 'Islam' specifically refers to the buyer's side.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange (sale of gold for gold, silver for silver, etc.)
سلم (salam): Advance sale where price is paid spot for future delivery of fungible goods.
وكيل (wakil): Agent who acts on behalf of the principal in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is agency from the seller's side (muslim ilayh) in salam not valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the agent cannot accept the capital as it becomes a loan.
B) Because the agent would be selling what he does not possess, as the subject matter remains in his liability.
C) Because the principal must be present for the contract.
D) Because sarf and salam are not valid forms of agency.

Key Takeaway
Agency in salam is valid only from the buyer's side because the agent from the seller's side would become liable for the subject matter, contravening the prohibition of selling what one does not own.

Who Bears Liability in Agency When the Principal Is Present?
Resolving intra-Hanafi dispute on liability in agency contracts when principal is present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding the effect of the principal's presence on agency contracts and identify the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) regarding liability.

Core Idea
The agent is a deputy in formation but a principal in rights; liability shifts to the principal if he attends the contract.

In Hanafi agency law, a key question is whether the principal's physical presence at the contract session alters the agent's role and liability. This passage presents an intra-madhhab dispute, with al-Ayni arguing that the agent's role is nullified when the principal appears, while al-Hamawi and others maintain that the agent retains rights as a principal. The commentator ultimately resolves the issue by affirming that liability (uhdah) falls on the principal when present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial position from al-Nihaya/al-Mabsut
The passage begins by citing al-Nihaya, which attributes to al-Mabsut the view that the continued presence of the contracting parties (agent and counterparty) matters, and the principal's absence or presence is irrelevant.
This implies the agent is fully responsible regardless.
2
Examine al-Ayni's objection
Al-Ayni refutes this, arguing that if the principal is present, the agent as deputy should not be considered; his role is nullified.
Al-Ayni's view makes the principal the direct party.
3
Analyze al-Hamawi's counter-response
Al-Hamawi responds that the agent is a deputy only in formation of the contract, but a principal in rights. Therefore, the principal's presence does not affect those rights.
This preserves the agent's capacity to hold rights and obligations.
4
Understand the commentator's resolution and refutation
The commentator supports al-Hamawi by citing an earlier statement that the buyer may prevent the principal from the price, showing the principal is a stranger to contract rights. He then quotes al-Manh that the relied-upon view is that liability (uhdah) is on the principal when present.
This resolves the difficulty raised by al-Zaylai.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator explicitly states that the mu'tamad (relied-upon) position is that liability rests on the principal if he attends the contract, not the agent.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النهاية - بعده فقال : المعتبر بقاء المتعاقدين في المجلس وغيبة الموكل لا تضر وعزاه إلى وكالة المبسوط وإطلاقه وإطلاق سائر الكتب دليل على أن مفارقة الموكل لا تعتبر أصلا ولو كان حاضراً اهـ. ورده العيني بأنه ليس بمشكل فإن الوكيل نائب عنه فإذا حضر الأصيل فلا يعتبر النائب اهـ. وتعقبه الحموي بأن الوكيل نائب في أصل العقد أصيل في الحقوق وحينئذ فلا اعتبار بحضرة الموكل اهـ. قلت : ومما يتضح به تزييف جواب العيني ما ذكره هو نفسه عند قول المصنف فيما سبق وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن من أن الموكل أجنبي عن العقد وحقوقه لأنها تتعلق بالعاقد على ما بينا اهـ؛ في حاشية مسكين وما استشكله الزيلعي استشكله صاحب العناية. وذكر في الحواشي السعدية أنه توارد مع الزيلعي في هذا الإشكال ثم نقل عبارة الزيلعي وقال : وعليك بالتأمل أقول وبالله التوفيق: الذي يقطع عرق الإشكال من أصله ما قدمناه عن المنح من أن المعتمد أن العهدة على الموكل دون الوكيل إذا حضر العقد وأنه أصح الأقاويل، فكلام الإمام خواهرزاده مبني على هذا لا على ما مشى عليه سابقاً من أنها على الوكيل وإن كان الموكل حاضراً وهو منشأ الإشكال، وبه اتضح الحال كذا والحمد لله وحده
Al-Nihaya - after it he said: The consideration is the continued presence of the two contracting parties in the session, and the absence of the principal does not harm. He attributed it to the agency of al-Mabsut, and its unrestrictedness and the unrestrictedness of all books are evidence that the departure of the principal is not considered at all, even if he were present. Al-Ayni refuted this, saying it is not problematic, for the agent is his deputy, so if the principal is present, the deputy is not considered. Al-Hamawi countered that the agent is a deputy in the original contract but a principal in rights, and therefore the presence of the principal is not considered. I say: What clarifies the refutation of al-Ayni's answer is what he himself mentioned at the author's statement earlier: 'And the buyer may prevent the principal from the price' because the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights, as they pertain to the contracting party as we explained; in the margin of Miskin. And what al-Zaylai found problematic, the author of al-Inayah also found problematic. It was mentioned in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah that he concurred with al-Zaylai in this difficulty. Then he quoted al-Zaylai's phrase and said: Upon you is reflection. I say, with Allah's facilitation, that which cuts the vein of difficulty from its root is what we presented from al-Manh: that the relied-upon view is that the liability (al-uhdah) is on the principal not the agent when he attends the contract, and that it is the soundest of opinions. Thus the speech of Imam Khwahrizadeh is based on this, not on what was previously acted upon that it is on the agent even if the principal is present, and this is the source of the difficulty. And thus the matter is clarified. So it is, and all praise is for Allah alone.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the principal's presence at the contract affects the agent's capacity. The initial view from al-Nihaya, attributed to al-Mabsut, states that the continued presence of the two contracting parties (i.e., the agent and the counterparty) is what matters, not the principal's absence or presence. Al-Ayni objected, arguing that the agent is merely a deputy, so if the principal is present, the deputy's actions are irrelevant. Al-Hamawi responded that while the agent is a deputy in the original formation of the contract, he is considered a principal in the rights and obligations arising from it; thus the principal's presence does not affect those rights. The commentator then supports al-Hamawi's position by referencing the author's earlier statement that the buyer can prevent the principal from claiming the price, because the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights belong to the contracting party (the agent). The difficulty raised by al-Zaylai is resolved by the view from al-Manh that the relied-upon position is that liability (al-uhdah) falls on the principal if he is present at the contract, not on the agent. This, the commentator says, is the correct view and clarifies the issue.

Key Terms
عهدة (uhdah): Liability or responsibility for the contract's obligations (e.g., price, delivery).
أصيل (asil): Principal; one who has direct rights and obligations in a contract.
نائب (na'ib): Deputy; someone acting on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, who bears the liability (al-uhdah) for the contract when the principal is present?
A) The agent alone.
B) The principal alone.
C) Both jointly.
D) It depends on the type of contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, the agent is a principal in rights; when the principal is present, liability (uhdah) rests on him, not the agent.

Prohibition of Agency from the Salam Seller: Shubha and Precaution
Explaining rational reasoning for prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency from the muslim ilayh in salam and explain the rational reasoning based on shubha and riba precaution.

Core Idea
Agency from the salam seller is prohibited due to shubha from ownership disagreements, as a precaution against riba.

In salam, the seller (muslim ilayh) receives capital upfront and delivers later. Can he appoint an agent to take the capital? The Hanafi school says no. But why? The passage we study today reveals that the prohibition is not from a direct text but from deep rational reasoning: a difference among scholars on ownership creates a shubha (doubt), and out of caution, the school treats this as akin to riba. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its sources
Identify that the passage begins with the ruling from al-Jawhara and al-Hidayah: agency from the muslim ilayh is not valid. Note that commentators uniformly agree.
The ruling is a prohibition (haram).
2
Understand the objection raised
Al-Inayah raises an objection: since the principal can accept salam, why can’t his agent? Also, agency for purchase (which resembles this) is allowed. This objection sets up the need for an explanation.
The objection is based on analogy (qiyas) with purchase.
3
Analyze the response: the role of shubha
The key response is that there is a scholarly disagreement about whether ownership of the salam subject is initially for the principal or transfers via the agent. This disagreement creates a shubha (doubt). Because invalid contracts are likened to riba, and riba is established by doubt, the safer path is to prohibit agency from the seller.
Shubha in riba is treated like actual riba (as in the olives-with-oil analogy).
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ownership
The passage points out that the prohibition rests on the fact that if ownership transfers (from agent to principal), then selling before possession could occur. If ownership is direct for principal, the concern is weaker, but the mere possibility creates shubha. This ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school.
Later Hanafis like Khalil al-Fattal critique this reasoning, but the relied-upon position remains prohibition.
5
Recognize the methodological takeaway
The Hanafi school uses rational reasoning (istidlal bi al-aql) and the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means) to forbid an act based on shubha, even without a direct textual prohibition. This shows the depth of usul applied to fiqh.
Contrast with literalist approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يجوز من جانب المسلم إليه بأخذ رأس المال عبارة الجوهرة بأن وكله يقبل له السلم وعبارة الهداية ومراده التوكيل بالإسلام دون قبول السلم قال الرملي : وقد تواردت الشراح وغيرهم على هذا قال في العناية : واعترض بأن قبول السلم عقد يملكه الموكل والواجب أن يملكه الوكيل حفظاً للقاعدة المذكورة عن الانتقاض وبأن التوكيل بالشراء جائز لا محالة والثمن يجب في ذمة الموكل والوكيل مطالب به فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المال للمسلم إليه والوكيل مطالب بتسليم المسلم فيه؟ وأجاب عن الإيرادين بجوابين ردهما الرملي ثم قال : ويختلج في صدري جواب لعله يكون صحيحاً إن شاء الله تعالى وهو أنه لما اختلف العلماء كما قرروه في الملك هل يثبت للموكل ابتداء أو للوكيل ثم ينتقل للموكل أثر هذا الاختلاف في المحل شبهة فأوجب عدم الجواز فيما القياس فيه المنع مطلقاً احتياطاً إذ العقود الفاسدة مجراها مجرى الربا، والأمر المتوهم في الربا كالمحقق كما في الزيتون بالزيت فعدم جواز التوكيل من المسلم إليه لما فيه من بيع المسلم فيه قبل القبض بيع عند من يقول إنه ينتقل من الوكيل للموكل ولاحتماله عند القائل بثبوته ابتداء للموكل لأنه مجتهد فيه وهو محل الاحتمال والفاسد ملحق بالربا والربا يثبت بالشبهة والتوهم اهـ. وفي حاشية الدر المختار للشيخ خليل الفتال ما نصه وتعقبه بعض حنفية زماننا حيث قال: قوله ولعله يكون صحيحاً تخلف فيه الرجا فأحسن التدبير يظهر لك ذلك. وحاصله أن بيع المسلم فيه قبل قبضه إنما يتأتى لو كان الوكيل من طرف رب السلم والمسألة في الوكيل من طرف المسلم إليه وأي بيع للمسلم فيه قبل قبضه نعم يمكن أن يكون المستفاد من هذا التقرير ما هو الحامل لتصحيح المشايخ القول بثبوت الملك للموكل ابتداء إذ على مقابله وهو القول بالانتقال يشكل صحة التوكيل بالإسلام لما فيه من بيع المسلم قبل قبضه اهـ . قلت : وفي قوله نعم يمكن الخ نظر ظاهر فقد بناه على ما نقضه فكيف يثبت غرضه قوله : (ولأن فيه عزل نفسه ولا يملكه الخ) قال في الحواشي السعدية: وما أن العزل الحكمي لا يتوقف على العلم فلا تعلق له بما نحن فيه إذ المراد هناك أن العزل سيجيء من
His saying: (It is not valid from the side of the muslim ilayh by taking the capital) is the wording of al-Jawhara: that he appointed him to accept the salam for him, and the wording of al-Hidayah: and his intention is agency for Islam (surrender) not for accepting the salam. Al-Ramli said: the commentators and others have concurred on this. Al-Inayah said: an objection was raised: accepting salam is a contract that the principal can perform, so it should be permissible for the agent to perform it to preserve the aforementioned rule from being breached; and because agency for purchase is absolutely permissible, and the price is owed by the principal, but the agent is demanded for it, so why is it not permissible that the capital goes to the muslim ilayh and the agent is demanded to deliver the subject matter? He answered both objections with two answers, but al-Ramli rejected them. Then he said: a response occurs to me that may be correct, Allah willing: that since the scholars differed, as they have established, regarding ownership—whether it is established for the principal initially or for the agent then transferred to the principal—the effect of this difference creates a doubt (shubha) in the matter, therefore it necessitates non-permissibility in what analogy would absolutely forbid as a precaution, because invalid contracts are treated like riba, and a matter suspected in riba is like a confirmed matter, as in olives with oil. So the non-permissibility of agency from the muslim ilayh is due to the sale of the subject matter of salam before possession in the view of those who say it transfers from the agent to the principal, and due to its possibility for those who say it is established for the principal initially, because it is a matter of ijtihad and is a place of probability, and the invalid is attached to riba, and riba is established by doubt and suspicion. In the hashya of al-Durr al-Mukhtar by Shaykh Khalil al-Fattal: some Hanafis of our time criticized him saying: his statement 'it may be correct' is a failure of hope, so improve the management and it will become clear to you. The summary is that the sale of the salam subject before possession would only occur if the agent were from the side of rabb al-salam, but the issue is about the agent from the side of the muslim ilayh—what sale of the salam subject before possession is there? Yes, it is possible that the benefit from this formulation is what led the early scholars to adopt the view that ownership is established for the principal initially, because on the opposite view (transfer) the validity of agency for salam would be problematic due to selling the salam subject before possession. I say: In his statement 'yes, it is possible' there is apparent oversight, for he built it on what he himself breached, so how can his objective be established? His saying: (Because it involves removing himself [from the agency] and he does not have the right to do so) said in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah: and that the constructive removal does not depend on knowledge, so it has no connection to our discussion, for the intention there is that the removal will come from
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed reasoning for the prohibition of agency from the seller in salam (muslim ilayh) by taking the capital. The author begins by citing the ruling from al-Jawhara and al-Hidayah that such agency is not valid. Al-Ramli notes that commentators agree. An objection is raised in al-Inayah: since accepting salam is a contract the principal can do, why not the agent? Also, since agency for purchase is allowed, why not here? The objection is answered by pointing to a difference among scholars on whether ownership of the salam subject is initially established for the principal or transfers from the agent. This difference creates a shubha (doubt), so the more cautious position is to prohibit agency from the seller to avoid resembling riba (usury). The later hashya of Khalil al-Fattal critiques this reasoning, arguing that the issue of selling before possession only arises if the agent were from the buyer's side, not the seller's. The discussion ends with a comment on al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah regarding 'constructive removal.'

Key Terms
المسلم إليه (muslim ilayh): The seller in a salam contract who receives the capital and is obliged to deliver the subject matter later.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that leads to precautionary prohibition in riba-related matters.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; prohibited transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in this passage for prohibiting agency from the muslim ilayh in salam?
A) The agent cannot take possession of the capital.
B) The difference in ownership theories creates a shubha that leads to precautionary prohibition.
C) The principal must be present for the contract.
D) The salam contract itself is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi prohibition of agency from the salam seller is based on rational precaution (shubha) to avoid riba, not on explicit textual prohibition.

Agent Buys More Than Instructed: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's excess purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position when an agent purchases more than instructed, explain the Sahibayn's counter-argument, and identify the conditions that determine whether the extra quantity binds the principal.

Core Idea
An agent's purchase of extra quantity binds the principal only if the increase is small and the commodity is weighed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding an agent who buys more than instructed. The key question is whether the extra quantity binds the principal or remains on the agent. Understanding this dispute clarifies Hanafi agency law and the distinction between weighed (mawzunat) and value (qimiyat) items. The ruling carries significant practical implications in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Case
Identify the scenario: principal instructs agent to buy 10 pounds of meat for 1 dirham at a market where the normal price is 10 pounds per dirham. The agent buys 20 pounds for the same 1 dirham.
This is the core hypothetical from which the dispute arises.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's Ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that only 10 pounds at half a dirham (proportional price) are binding on the principal. The extra 10 pounds are purchased by the agent for himself.
Rationale: the instruction was limited to 10 pounds; the agent exceeded authority.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's Ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that all 20 pounds are binding on the principal at the full 1 dirham because the agent fulfilled the instruction to spend the dirham on meat and delivered extra benefit.
They analogize to the case of selling a slave for more than the instructed price.
4
Note the Key Conditions
The ruling depends on: (1) the increase being substantial (a small increase like half a pound binds the principal); (2) the commodity being weighed (mawzun) rather than valued (qimi); (3) the agent buying the same quality (e.g., not lean instead of fat).
These conditions are derived from the text and consensus statements.
5
Apply to Other Examples
The text provides additional cases: buying with different currency of equivalent value binds (contrary to Zufar and Muhammad); buying with goods of equivalent value does not bind by consensus; a traveler sending for cooked food is free to choose.
These illustrate the limits of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وكله بشراء عشرة أرطال لحم بدرهم فاشترى عشرين رطلاً بدرهم مما يباع منه عشرة بدرهم لزم الموكل منه عشرة بنصف درهم وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يلزمه العشرون لأنه أمره بصرف الدرهم في اللحم وظن أن سعره عشرة أرطال فإذا اشترى به عشرين فقد زاده خيراً وصار كما إذا وكله ببيع عبده بألف فباعه بألفين. ولأبي حنيفة أنه أمره بشراء عشرة ولم يأمره بشراء الزيادة فنفذ شراؤها عليه وشراء العشرة على الموكل بخلاف ما استشهدا به لأن الزيادة هناك بدل ملك الموكل فتكون له قيد بالزيادة الكثيرة لأن القليلة كعشرة أرطال ونصف رطل لازمه للآمر لأنها تدخل بين الوزنين فلا يتحقق حصول الزيادة؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وقيد بقوله مما يباع إلى آخره لأنه لو اشترى ما يساوي عشرين منه بدرهم صار مشترياً لنفسه إجماعاً لأنه خلاف إلى شر لأن الأمر تناول السمين وهذا مهزول فلم يحصل مقصود الآمر . وقيد بالموزونات لأن في القيميات لا ينفذ شيء على الموكل إجماعاً فلو وكله بشراء ثوب هروي بعشرة فاشترى له ثوبين هرويين بعشرة مما يساوي كل واحد منهما عشرة لم يلزم الموكل لأن ثمن كل واحد منهما مجهول إذ لا يعرف إلا بالحزر بخلاف اللحم لأنه موزون مقدر فيقسم الثمن على أجزائه وفي البزازية : أمره أن يشتري بعشرة دنانير فاشتراه بمائتي درهم وقيمة الدراهم مثل الدنانير لزم الموكل خلافاً لزفر ومحمد، ولو بعروض قيمتها مثل الدراهم لا يلزم الآمر إجماعاً. وفي الملتقط: مسافر نزل خاناً وأمر إنساناً بدرهم وإنما يباع هناك المطبوخ والمشوي فأيهما اشترى جاز.
And if he appointed him to buy ten pounds of meat for a dirham, and he bought twenty pounds for a dirham from someone who sells ten for a dirham, then ten at half a dirham are binding on the principal — this according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the twenty are binding on him, because he instructed him to spend the dirham on meat, thinking its price was ten pounds, so if he bought twenty with it, he did him extra good, and it becomes like when he appointed him to sell his slave for a thousand and he sold him for two thousand. For Abu Hanifa: he instructed him to buy ten, not to buy the extra; so the purchase of the extra is [effective] on himself, and the purchase of the ten is on the principal, unlike what they analogized, because the increase there is a substitute for the principal's property, so it becomes his. [The ruling] is restricted to a large increase, because a small increase, like ten and a half pounds, is binding on the principal, because it falls between the two weights, so the occurrence of an increase is not realized; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. It is restricted by his saying 'from those who sell ten for a dirham' because if he bought something worth twenty for a dirham, he becomes a buyer for himself by consensus, because it is contrary to the instruction, as the instruction applied to the fat [meat] and this is lean, so the principal's purpose is not achieved. It is restricted to weighed items (mawzunat), because in value items (qimiyat), nothing is binding on the principal by consensus. So if he appointed him to buy a Herawi cloth for ten, and he bought two Herawi cloths for ten, each worth ten, it does not bind the principal, because the price of each is unknown, known only by estimation, unlike meat because it is weighed and measured, so the price is divided among its parts. In al-Bazzaziyya: He instructed him to buy with ten dinars, and he bought it for two hundred dirhams, and the value of the dirhams equals the dinars, it binds the principal, contrary to Zufar and Muhammad. And if it is with goods whose value equals the dirhams, it does not bind the principal by consensus. And in al-Multaqit: A traveler alighted at a khan and instructed a person to [buy] for a dirham, and only cooked and roasted food is sold there, so whichever he bought is permissible.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding when an agent buys more than instructed. The case: the principal instructs the agent to buy 10 pounds of meat for 1 dirham at a market where the price is 10 pounds per dirham. The agent buys 20 pounds for 1 dirham. According to Abu Hanifa, only 10 pounds at half a dirham (proportionally) are binding on the principal, and the extra 10 pounds are on the agent himself. According to the Sahibayn, all 20 pounds are binding on the principal because the agent fulfilled the instruction to spend the dirham on meat, and the extra quantity was a benefit. Abu Hanifa argues that the agent exceeded the instruction in quantity, unlike the analogous case of selling a slave for more than instructed where the extra amount is a substitute for the principal's property. The ruling is conditioned: (1) The increase must be substantial (e.g., 10 extra pounds); a small increase (like half a pound) binds the principal as it falls within acceptable variation. (2) The item must be of the same type as instructed; if the agent buys a different type (e.g., lean instead of fat), it does not bind. (3) The item must be a weighed commodity (mawzun) like meat; for value items (qimiyat) like cloth, the agent's purchase of extra does not bind because the price per unit is unknown. Other examples from al-Bazzaziyya: buying with different but equivalent currency binds; buying with goods of equivalent value does not bind. The final example from al-Multaqit: if the agent is sent to buy with a dirham and only cooked food is sold, whichever he chooses is permissible.

Key Terms
موزونات (mawzunat): Goods sold by weight (like meat, grains) where the price can be proportionally divided.
قيميات (qimiyat): Goods sold by value (like cloth) that are not uniform and require estimation of price.
الزيادة الكثيرة (al-ziyadat al-kathirah): A substantial increase in quantity that does not bind the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if an agent is instructed to buy 10 pounds of meat for 1 dirham and buys 20 pounds for the same price, what is binding on the principal?
A) All 20 pounds.
B) 10 pounds at half the price.
C) Nothing, the contract is void.
D) 10 pounds at the original price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency law, if an agent buys more than instructed at the same total price, only the instructed quantity binds the principal at a proportional price, provided the increase is substantial and the commodity is weighed.

Agent Buying with Different Currency: When the Principal is Bound
Rulings on agency with different currencies and goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a principal is bound by an agent's purchase with a different currency or goods, and identify the key factors (equivalence of value, type of consideration).

Core Idea
An agent's purchase with a different currency binds the principal if value matches; with goods it does not.

In previous lessons we learned the basic rules of agency (wakalah) in sales. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents additional cases from later Hanafi sources: what happens when an agent buys with a different currency or with goods? The rulings reveal a key principle: the type of consideration (currency vs. goods) and value equivalence determine whether the principal is bound. An intra-Hanafi dispute further illustrates the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Parse the two scenarios
Identify the two cases: (1) agent ordered to buy with 10 dinars but buys with 200 dirhams of equal value; (2) agent buys with goods of equal value instead of dirhams.
The first case binds the principal; the second does not by consensus.
2
Identify the key condition
Notice that in both cases the value is equal. The difference is the medium: currency vs. goods. For currency, the principal is bound; for goods, not.
This shows that type of consideration matters, not just value.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
In the currency case, Zufar and Muhammad hold the principal is not bound. The text opposes them, indicating the majority (including Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) binds the principal.
The dispute revolves around whether changing the currency is a fundamental change in the subject matter.
4
Understand the consensus on goods
When the agent buys with goods (instead of dirhams), the principal is not bound. The text says 'by consensus' (ijma'an), indicating agreement among Hanafis.
Goods are considered a different type of consideration, so the agent has not fulfilled the principal's order.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Test your understanding: if an agent is told to buy a sheep for 100 dirhams and instead buys a goat worth 100 dirhams, is the principal bound? No, because the subject is different, analogizing to the 'goods' case.
The key is whether the agent's act falls within the scope of the principal's order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Bazzaziyya: He ordered him to buy with ten dinars, so he bought with two hundred dirhams, and the value of the dirhams equals the dinars, then the principal is bound, contrary to Zufar and Muhammad. And if with goods the value of which equals the dirhams, the principal is not bound by consensus. And in al-Multaqat: A traveler stayed at a khan and ordered a man for a dirham, and there only cooked and grilled are sold; whichever he bought is permissible. He buys meat for him; the prohibition is absolute as a precaution, because the course of void contracts is the course of riba, and the matter suspected of riba is like verified, as with olive oil for oil. So the non-permissibility of appointing an agent from the one to whom salam is given because it involves selling the salam goods before receipt, according to the one who says it transfers from the agent to the principal, and because of possibility according to the one who says it is established initially for the principal. And the void is attached to riba, and riba is established by doubt and suspicion. End. And in the Hashiya of al-Durr al-Mukhtar by Shaykh Khalil al-Fattal, his text: And followed by some Hanafis of our time where he said: His statement 'perhaps it is valid' is lacking in hope, so good management appears to you. And its summary: That selling the salam goods before receipt occurs only if the agent is from the side of the owner of salam, and the issue is about the agent from the side of the one to whom salam is given. And any sale of salam before receipt, yes, it can be that what is used from this report is what leads to correct the statement of the shaykhs that ownership is established initially for the principal, because on its opposite, the statement of transfer, the validity of agency in salam is problematic because it involves selling salam before receipt. End. I said: And in his statement 'yes it can' etc., there is apparent consideration, because he built it on what he contradicted, so how does he establish his purpose?

Commentary
This passage presents additional rulings from other Hanafi sources (Bazzaziyya, Multaqat) and a detailed discussion on agency in salam (advance payment) contracts. The first ruling: if an agent is ordered to buy with ten dinars but buys with two hundred dirhams of equal value, the principal is bound (contrary to Zufar and Muhammad). However, if he buys with goods of equal value, the principal is not bound by consensus. The second example: a traveler orders someone to buy meat with a dirham where only cooked or grilled meat is sold; both are permissible. Then the text discusses a precautionary prohibition regarding agency in salam due to potential riba. The commentary from Hashiyat al-Durr al-Mukhtar debates the validity of the agent from the side of the recipient of salam, focusing on whether ownership transfers immediately. The author of the sharh critiques the reasoning. This passage is a later-era (mutakhkhirin) discussion with multiple views.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): An advance payment sale where the price is paid in advance for deferred delivery of goods.
المسلم إليه (al-muslam ilayhi): The recipient of the payment in a salam contract (the seller who will deliver later).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When an agent buys with a different currency of equal value, the principal is bound; but if the agent buys with goods of equal value, the principal is not bound by consensus.

The Agent's Prohibition from Buying the Specified Item for Himself
Commentary on agency for a specific item: ruling and derivations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a specific-item agent cannot buy for himself, identify the two reasons given, and apply the distinction when the principal is present or absent.

Core Idea
An agent appointed to buy a specific item cannot buy it for himself; any such purchase belongs to the principal.

This lesson examines a fundamental ruling in Hanafi agency law: when a principal appoints an agent to buy a specific item, the agent is prohibited from buying that item for himself. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides two reasons for this prohibition and discusses several scenarios that test its boundaries, including buying with different currency or at a different price, and the effect of the principal's presence or absence. Understanding this ruling protects the principal's trust and prevents the agent from exploiting his position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its two rationales
First, read the passage to find the explicit ruling: the agent cannot buy the specified item for himself. Then note the two reasons: (1) it leads to deceiving the principal, and (2) it constitutes the agent dismissing himself without the principal's knowledge.
The first reason focuses on the principal's reliance; the second on the agent's authority.
2
Distinguish the effect of the principal's presence or absence
If the principal is absent, the purchase by the agent for himself automatically falls for the principal. If the principal is present and the agent explicitly says he is buying for himself, the purchase is for the agent because the agent can dismiss himself in the principal's presence.
The key is the principal’s knowledge: self-dismissal requires the principal’s awareness.
3
Analyze the scenario of buying with different currency or price
If the agent buys the item with a different currency (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams) or at a different price than instructed, the purchase goes to the agent, not the principal, because the agent violated the instructions. However, if the agent adds the contract to the principal (e.g., says 'I bought for you'), it may require the principal's ratification.
This distinction shows that deviation from instructions shifts ownership to the agent unless the principal ratifies.
4
Connect the istihsan discussion on dirhams and dinars
The passage includes a note that for riba matters, dirhams and dinars are treated as different genera, but for other transactions (including agency) they are treated as one genus by istihsan. This affects whether buying with a different currency is considered a violation.
Istihsan here facilitates transactions by treating them as one type, so buying with dinars when instructed to use dirhams is still a violation only if the value differs? The text indicates it is a violation regardless if the currency is different.
5
Synthesize the practical outcome for fatwa
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that any purchase of the specified item by the agent, even if intended for himself, belongs to the principal, unless the principal was present and the agent explicitly declared self-purchase. Deviating from instructions (different currency/price) makes the purchase for the agent.
This ruling is strict to protect the principal’s interest, consistent with the fiduciary nature of agency.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the ruling of agency for buying a specific item. The commentator explains that when a person appoints an agent to buy a specific item, the agent cannot buy it for himself. Two reasons are given: first, it leads to deceiving the principal who relied on the agent; second, it constitutes the agent dismissing himself from the agency without the principal's knowledge. The agent can only dismiss himself in the presence of the principal. If the agent buys the item for himself, whether he intends it or explicitly states it, the purchase belongs to the principal. The commentary then discusses various conditions: if the principal is absent, the agent cannot buy for himself; if the principal is present and the agent declares he is buying for himself, then it is validly for the agent because he can dismiss himself in the principal's presence. The passage also covers the scenario of buying with different currency or a different price than specified: if the agent buys the item with a different currency (e.g., dirhams instead of dinars) or at a different price, the purchase goes to the agent, not the principal, because he violated the instructions. The commentary includes a discussion on whether dirhams and dinars are considered the same genus or different; according to the Hanafi school, they are treated as different genera in terms of riba but as one genus for other purposes by istihsan. The passage concludes with a note on the agent's silence: if the principal asks the agent to buy a specific item and the agent remains silent, the ruling varies; if the agent later says 'I bought it for you', it is for the principal, but if the item is destroyed or defective, the agent is not trusted. This is based on a narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed by the principal to perform a specific transaction (here, buying a specific item).
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent; the person on whose behalf the purchase is made.
عزل (ʿazl): Dismissal of the agent; terminating the agency contract. The agent may dismiss himself only with the principal's knowledge.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; in this context, treating dirhams and dinars as one genus for non-riba matters to facilitate transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A principal appoints an agent to buy a specific gold necklace. The agent buys the necklace for himself using his own money while the principal is absent. Who owns the necklace according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) The agent owns it because he bought it for himself.
B) The principal owns it because the agent was appointed to buy it for him.
C) It is void; no one owns it.
D) Both have a share.

Key Takeaway
An agent appointed to buy a specific item is prohibited from buying it for himself; if he does, the purchase belongs to the principal, unless the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares self-purchase.

Agent's Deviation in Purchase: Specified Item, Currency, and Price
Expanding on agent's purchase deviation with proofs and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys a specified item with a different currency or price, identify the exception of Zufar, and apply the rule to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
If the agent buys a specified item with different currency or price, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the basic rule of agency in purchase. The key question: when does an agent's deviation from instructions make him personally responsible for the purchase? The answer depends on whether the item was specified, and whether the deviation was in the type of currency or price. We'll walk through the ruling, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an agent buying a specified item (العين المعينة) with something other than what was ordered (e.g., different currency, different price, or non-cash). The ruling: the purchase is for the agent (الوكيل), not the principal (الآمر).
This is the general rule; exceptions exist if the agent intended for the principal or used the principal's money.
2
Locate the dalil and its reasoning
The proof is based on the principle that dirhams and dinars are different genera in riba; changing them is a material violation. The text refers to al-Jami' al-Saghir where by qiyas they are different, but by istihsan they are treated as one in non-riba contexts. However, in agency, the change remains a violation.
The passage also discusses the ruling for non-specified items: purchase is for the agent unless he intends for the principal or uses the principal's money.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar differs on the second case (purchase with cash of different currency). He considers it not a violation if the value is equivalent. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) disagree.
The text says 'خلافاً لزفر في الثاني' indicating Zufar's view is exceptional.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the majority, the agent owns the item and must return the principal's money or bear liability. Under Zufar, if the value is equal, the principal may be bound.
This shows the practical consequence of the difference.
5
Examine the ruling on silence as acceptance
The passage also discusses whether silence of the agent counts as acceptance of the agency. According to al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, silence is acceptance. Quoting al-Bazzaziyyah, if the agent keeps silent and buys, there is a distinction between explicit acceptance and silence.
This is a separate but related issue; it affects whether the agency is valid.

حتى جاز بيع يكمل نصاب أحدهما بالآخر، والقاضي في قيم المتلفات بالخيار إن شاء قوم بالدراهم وإن شاء قوم بالدنانير والمكره على البيع بالدراهم إذا باع بالدنانير أو على العكس كان بيعه بيع مكره، وصاحب عليه. أطلقه فشمل المخالفة في الجنس وفي القدر كما في البزازية، وقيده في الهداية والمجمع بخلاف الجنس فظاهره أنه إذا سمى له ثمناً فزاد عليه أو نقص عنه فإنه لا يكون مخالفاً، وظاهر ما في الكافي للحاكم أنه يكون مخالفاً فيما إذا زاد لا فيما إذا نقص فإنه قال : وإن قال اشتر لي ثوباً هروياً ولم يسم الثمن فهو جائز على الآمر، وإن سمى ثمناً فزاد عليه شيئاً لم يلزم الآمر ، وكذلك إن نقص من ذلك الثمن إلا أن يكون وصفه له بصفة له ثمناً وسمى فاشترى بتلك الصفة بأقل من ذلك الثمن فيجوز على الآمر ا هـ. وإذا كان معيناً فهو كالموصوف فشمل ما إذا كان خلاف الجنس عرضاً أو نقداً خلافاً لزفر في الثاني. وما إذا كان ما اشترى الحسامية : قال الأسير لرجل اشترني بألف فاشتراه بمائة دينار أو بعرض جاز وله أن يرجع على الأسير بألف والوكيل بالشراء بألف درهم إذا اشترى بمائة دينار أو بعرض لا يلزم الموكل شيء ا هـ. وفي خزانة المفتين من الصرف: الأسير إذا أمر رجلاً أن يفديه بألف ففداه بألفين يرجع بألفين عليه وليس بمنزلة الوكيل بالشراء . قوله : (وإن كان بغير عينه فالشراء للوكيل إلا أن ينوي للموكل أو يشتريه بماله) هكذا أطلقه المؤلف وفصله في الهداية فقال : هذه المسألة على وجوه : إن أضاف العقد إلى دراهم الآمر كان للأمر هو المراد عندي بقوله أو يشتريه بمال الموكل دون النقد من ماله لأن فيه تفصيلاً وخلافاً ، هذا بالإجماع وهو مطلق. وإن أضافه إلى دراهم نفسه كان لنفسه حملاً لحاله على ما يحل له شرعاً أو يفعله عادة إذ الشراء لنفسه بإضافة العقد إلى دراهم غيره مستنكر شرعاً وعرفاً، وإن أضافه إلى دراهم مطلقة فإن نواها للآمر فهو للآمر، وإن نواها لنفسه الأول كان البيع فاسداً استحساناً. وتبين بما ذكر أنهما اعتبرا جنسين مختلفين في حكم الربا شهد بالدراهم والآخر بالدنانير أو شهد بالدراهم والمدعي دنانير أو على العكس لا تقبل الشهادة، وكذلك في
