Agent-Principal Dispute: Whose Word is Accepted?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent-principal disagreement over purchase intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent and principal dispute the intention of a purchase and apply the criteria of payment status and suspicion.

Core Idea
When agent and principal dispute, the denier's word is taken unless the agent paid the price and is a trustee.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common dispute in agency: the agent claims he bought for the principal, but the principal claims the agent bought for himself. The ruling turns on whether the price was paid and whether there is suspicion of bad faith. Within the Hanafi school, Imams Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on certain scenarios, making this a key intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The agent says 'I bought for you' and the principal says 'you bought for yourself'. Note whether the price has been paid to the agent.
The passage discusses both paid and unpaid price scenarios.
2
Apply the general rule: al-qawl lil-munkir
If the price was not paid, the agent's claim would obligate the principal to pay, which the principal denies. The denier's word is taken, so the principal's word prevails.
This is the default rule when no suspicion is involved.
3
Identify the exception when the price was paid
If the price was paid to the agent, the agent is a trustee seeking to exit the trust. His word is accepted even against the principal's denial.
This applies whether the slave is alive or dead.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference for unpaid price when the slave is alive
Imam Abu Hanifa introduces the concept of 'mawdi' tuhmah' (place of suspicion). If the price is excessively high, the agent may have bought for himself and now wants to shift a losing deal to the principal. Suspicion is assessed by experts. If suspicion exists, the principal's word is taken; otherwise the agent's word. The Sahibayn hold that the agent's word is always taken because he could have initiated the purchase for himself, so there is no incentive to lie.
The difference hinges on whether the agent's inherent ability to buy for himself creates potential bad faith.
5
Apply the summary rule for all cases
If the price was paid, always agent's word. If unpaid and the slave is dead, principal's word (by agreement). If unpaid and alive: according to Sahibayn, agent's word; according to Abu Hanifa, principal's word if suspicion, else agent's word. The matn's ruling aligns with Abu Hanifa's detailed approach and is considered mufta bihi.
Suspicion is determined by expert assessment of price excessiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قال اشتريت للآمر وقال الأمر لنفسك فالقول للآمر وإن كان دفع إليه الثمن فللمأمور) لأنه في الوجه الأول أخبر عما لا يملك استئنافه وهو الرجوع بالثمن على الآمر وهو ينكر والقول للمنكر، وفي الوجه الثاني هو أمين يريد الخروج عن عهدة الأمانة فيقبل قوله. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان العبد ميتاً أو حياً، ولا خلاف في الأول أنه على التفصيل المذكور، وفي الثاني اختلاف فقال الإمام الأعظم هو كذلك على التفصيل. وقالا : القول للمأمور وإن لم يكن الثمن منقوداً لأنه يملك استئناف الشراء فلا يتهم في الإخبار عنه . وله أنه موضع تهمة بأن اشتراه لنفسه فإذا رأى الصفقة خاسرة ألزمها الآمر بخلاف ما إذا كان الثمن منقوداً لأنه أمين فيه فيقبل قوله تبعاً لذلك ولا ثمن في يده هنا، وذكره هذه عقيب مسألة التوكيل بغير المعين دليل على أن الاختلاف فيه . قيد به لأنه لو وكله بشراء عبد بعينه فالقول للمأمور، سواء كان الثمن منقوداً أو غير منقود إجماعاً لأنه أخبر اختلفا والعبد حي عما يملك استئنافة، ولا تهمة فيه لأن الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شراءه لنفسه بمثل ذلك الثمن في حال غيبته على ما مر بخلاف غير المعين على قوله وإن كان ميتاً فكما إذا كان أنه غير معين من إذا كان غير منقود فالقول للآمر وإلا فللمأمور. وحاصله كما قاله الشارح أن الثمن إن كان منقوداً فالقول للمأمور في جميع الصور، وإن كان غير منقود فإن كان ميتاً فالقول للآمر وإلا فللمأمور عندهما وعنده في غير موضع التهمة وفي موضعها القول للآمر . وفي البزازية معزياً إلى العيون اشتر لي جارية فلان فذهب وساوم ثم قال المأمور قد الجارية تعينها والله تعالى أعلم قوله : وإلا) فالنقد من مالها لا يعين كونها لها كما قدمناه) قال الرملي : قدم أنه عند التكاذب يحكم النقد بالإجماع فتأمل قوله : ( وعنده في غير موضع التهمة) لعل المراد بها ما إذا كان بعد التعيب. ثم رأيت في حاشية مسكين قال : فإن قلت بماذا تثبت التهمة؟ قلت : بالرجوع إلى أهل الخبرة، فإن أخبروا أن الثمن يزيد على القيمة زيادة فاحشة تثبت وإلا فلا اهـ . قوله : (وفي البزازية معزياً إلى العيون الخ قال الرملي : هذا الفرع هو الفرع الذي قدمناه عن البزازية أيضاً في المقولة التي قبل هذه المقولة اشتر لي جارية فلان فسكت الخ. قوله: (وينبغي حمل حال الهلاك أو التعييب على ما إذا كان غير منقود الخ) لما قدمه أن الثمن إن كان منقوداً فالقول للمأمور في جميع الصور ومنها حالة الهلاك والتعييب. وقال الرملي : لا حاجة إلى تكلف الحمل على ما إذا لم يكن منقوداً مع عدم ذكره أصلاً كما هو ظاهر إذ الأصل عدمه .اهـ يعني أن فرض المسألة لم يذكر فيها الثمن والأصل عدم ذكره فلا حاجة إلى ما قاله لأنه المفروض.
His saying: (And if he says 'I bought for the orderer' and the orderer says 'for yourself', then the word is of the orderer, and if he paid the price to him then it is for the agent) because in the first situation he reports about something he cannot initiate, i.e., claiming the price from the orderer while he denies it, and the word is of the denier. In the second situation, he is a trustee who wants to exit from the trust, so his statement is accepted. He made it absolute, so it includes whether the slave is dead or alive. There is no disagreement in the first situation that it is according to the detailed ruling. In the second situation, there is a difference: Imam Abu Hanifa said it is thus according to the detail. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The word is of the agent even if the price was not paid, because he can initiate the purchase, so he is not accused in his report. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it is a place of suspicion that he bought it for himself, so if he sees the deal is losing, he imposes it on the orderer. But if the price was paid, he is a trustee, so his statement is accepted accordingly, and there is no price in his hand here. And his mentioning this after the case of agency for non-specific purchase indicates that the difference is in it. He restricted it because if he appointed him to buy a specific slave, the word is of the agent, whether the price was paid or not, by consensus, because he reports about something he can initiate, and there is no suspicion because the agent for buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself with that price in the absence of the principal, as previously mentioned. Unlike the non-specific case upon his opinion. And if the slave is dead, then it is like the case where it is non-specific: if the price was not paid, the word is of the orderer, otherwise of the agent. The summary is as the commentator said: if the price was paid, the word is of the agent in all cases; if not paid, then if the slave is dead, the word is of the orderer, otherwise of the agent according to them (the two), and according to him (Abu Hanifa) in a place of no suspicion it is of the agent, and in a place of suspicion it is of the orderer. And in al-Bazzaziyya, referring to al-Uyun: 'Buy for me the slave-girl of so-and-so' so he went and negotiated, then the agent said 'I have already... the slave-girl is...' and Allah knows best. His saying: 'Otherwise, the payment from her money does not determine that it is for her, as we have mentioned.' al-Ramli said: It was mentioned earlier that in case of dispute, the payment decides by consensus, so reflect. His saying: 'And according to him in a place of no suspicion' – perhaps what is meant is when it is after a defect. Then I saw in the gloss of Miskin that he said: If you say: How is suspicion established? I say: By referring to experts; if they report that the price exceeds the value excessively, then it is established, otherwise not. His saying: (And in al-Bazzaziyya, referring to al-Uyun) al-Ramli said: This branch is the one we already mentioned from al-Bazzaziyya also in the statement before this statement 'Buy for me the slave-girl of so-and-so so he remained silent...' His saying: (It is appropriate to apply the case of destruction or defect to when the price was not paid) because he already stated that if the price was paid, the word is of the agent in all cases, including the case of destruction and defect. And al-Ramli said: There is no need to strain to apply it to when the price was not paid, since it is not mentioned at all, as is apparent, for the original is its absence. This means that the assumption of the case does not mention the price, and the original is its absence, so there is no need for what he said because it is the assumption.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between the agent and the principal over the intention of the purchase. The matn says: If the agent claims he bought for the principal and the principal claims the agent bought for himself, then the principal's word is taken (the purchase is for the principal) unless the price was paid to the agent, in which case the agent's word is taken (he bought for himself). The commentator explains the reasoning: in the first scenario, the agent's claim would require the principal to pay the price, which the principal denies, so the principal's denial is accepted. In the second scenario, where the price was already handed over, the agent is a trustee and his statement that he bought for himself is accepted because he wants to be free from liability. This ruling applies whether the slave is alive or dead, but there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa (al-Imam al-A'zam) agrees with the detailed ruling. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that if the price was not paid, the agent's word is taken because he could have initiated the purchase for himself, so there is no suspicion. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: if the price was not paid, the agent might lie and attribute a losing deal to the principal. If the price was paid, the agent is a trustee and his word is accepted. The dispute only applies when the agency was for a non-specific purchase (bighayr 'ayn). If it was for a specific item (mu'ayyan), then the agent's word is taken by consensus because he cannot buy a specific item for himself with the same price in the principal's absence. The summary: if the price was paid, the agent's word is always taken. If not paid, then if the slave is dead, the principal's word is taken; if alive, the agent's word according to the Sahibayn, but according to Abu Hanifa, if there is suspicion (e.g., the price is excessive) the principal's word is taken; otherwise the agent's. Suspicion is determined by consulting experts. The commentator also discusses a subtlety about a case from al-Bazzaziyya and a comment by al-Ramli about not needing to force an interpretation when the text does not mention the price.

Key Terms
موضع تهمة (mawdi' tuhmah): A situation where the agent might be suspected of buying for himself and then attributing a bad deal to the principal. Suspicion is determined by expert opinion on excessiveness of price.
القول للمنكر (al-qawl lil-munkir): The statement of the one who denies the claim is accepted; in this case, the principal denies that the agent bought for him, so his word is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if an agent appointed to buy a non-specific slave buys one, and the price was not paid, and the agent claims he bought for the principal but the principal claims he bought for himself, when is the principal's word taken?
A) Only if the slave is dead
B) Only if there is suspicion (e.g., price excessive)
C) Only if the slave is alive
D) The principal's word is always taken

Key Takeaway
The resolution of agency disputes hinges on who paid the price and on the presence of suspicion, reflecting the Hanafi principle of al-qawl lil-munkir.

Dispute Between Agent and Principal Over Intended Recipient of Purchase
Explaining the ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute over who is believed when agent and principal disagree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who is believed when agent and principal disagree over the intended recipient of a purchase, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When price is unpaid, Abu Hanifa suspects the agent; the Sahibayn trust him—a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on agency.

In this lesson, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute over agency in sales. When an agent says he bought for his principal, and the principal denies this, who is believed? The answer depends on whether the price was paid and whether suspicion of bad faith exists. This case illustrates how different presumptions about trustworthiness shape Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute scenario
Recognize the two claims: agent says 'I bought for you,' principal says 'You bought for yourself.' Note the two subcases: price paid vs. not paid.
The dispute is about who bears the financial liability, not about the validity of the sale.
2
Apply the base ruling from the matn
When price not paid, principal's word is taken; when price paid, agent's word is taken because he is a trustee seeking to exit trust.
This is the starting point of the dispute.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Hanifa allows suspicion (e.g., inflated price) to override the agent's claim; the Sahibayn uphold the agent's trustworthiness unless bad faith is proven.
Focus on the different rationales: suspicion vs. ability to initiate.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its rationale
The relied-upon position (fatwa) is that of the Sahibayn: the agent's word is accepted when price is unpaid, unless suspicion is established by expert valuation.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال اشتريت للآمر وقال الآمر لنفسك فالقول للآمر وإن كان دفع إليه الثمن اشتراها لنفسه، وكذا لو قال الزوج للمرأة بعد الشراء هذه الجارية التي أمرتني بشرائها اشتريتها لنفسي فالجارية للمرأة ولا يقبل قول الزوج ا هـ . وكأنه أولاً أضاف الشراء لها وإلا فالنقد من مالها لا يعين كونها لها كما قدمناه .

فالنقد من مالها لا يعين كونها لها كما قدمناه) قال الرملي : قدم أنه عند التكاذب يحكم النقد بالإجماع فتأمل قوله : ( وعنده في غير موضع التهمة) لعل المراد بها ما إذا كان بعد التعيب. ثم رأيت في حاشية مسكين قال : فإن قلت بماذا تثبت التهمة؟ قلت : بالرجوع إلى أهل الخبرة، فإن أخبروا أن الثمن يزيد على القيمة زيادة فاحشة تثبت وإلا فلا اهـ . قوله : (وفي البزازية معزياً إلى العيون الخ قال الرملي : هذا الفرع هو الفرع الذي قدمناه عن البزازية أيضاً في المقولة التي قبل هذه المقولة اشتر لي جارية فلان فسكت الخ. قوله: (وينبغي حمل حال الهلاك أو التعييب على ما إذا كان غير منقود الخ) لما قدمه أن الثمن إن كان منقوداً فالقول للمأمور في جميع الصور ومنها حالة الهلاك والتعييب. وقال الرملي : لا حاجة إلى تكلف الحمل على ما إذا لم يكن منقوداً مع عدم ذكره أصلاً كما هو ظاهر إذ الأصل عدمه .اهـ يعني أن فرض المسألة لم يذكر فيها الثمن والأصل عدم ذكره فلا حاجة إلى ما قاله لأنه المفروض .

البحر الرائق ج۷ م ١٨
And if he says, 'I bought for the principal,' and the principal says, 'For yourself,' then the word is with the principal, even if he gave him the price (he bought it for himself). Similarly, if the husband says to the woman after the purchase, 'This slave-girl which you ordered me to buy, I bought for myself,' then the slave-girl is for the woman, and the husband's statement is not accepted. End. And it seems that first he attributed the purchase to her, otherwise the money from her wealth does not determine that it is for her, as we have presented earlier. His statement: (And if he says 'I bought for the principal' and the principal says 'For yourself' then the word is with the principal; but if he gave him the price, then for the agent) because in the first case he informs about something he cannot initiate, i.e., recourse for the price against the principal, and he denies, and the word is for the denier. In the second case, he is a trustee wanting to exit the trust, so his statement is accepted. He (the author) made it absolute so it includes whether the slave is dead or alive. There is no dispute in the first case that it is as per the mentioned detail. In the second there is disagreement: Imam al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa) said it is likewise as per the detail. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The word is for the agent even if the price was not paid, because he can initiate the purchase, so he is not suspect in informing about it. And for him (Abu Hanifa), it is a place of suspicion because he bought it for himself, and if he sees the deal is loss-making, he imposes it on the principal, contrary to when the price is paid because he is a trustee regarding it, so his statement is accepted accordingly, and there is no price in his hand here. He mentioned this after the issue of agency for an unspecified item, indicating that the difference is there. He qualified it because if he authorized him to buy a specific slave, then the word is for the agent, whether the price is paid or not, unanimously, because he informs about what he can initiate, and there is no suspicion, because the agent to buy a specific thing cannot buy it for himself for the same price in his absence, as has passed, contrary to the unspecified on his view. And if it is dead, then as if it is unspecified, from when it is unpaid, then the word is for the principal, otherwise for the agent. The summary, as the commentator said, is that if the price is paid, then the word is for the agent in all cases; if unpaid, then if it is dead, the word is for the principal, otherwise for the agent according to them (the two), and according to him (Abu Hanifa) in a non-suspicious place, and in a suspicious place the word is for the principal. And in al-Bazzaziyya, attributed to al-Uyun: 'Buy for me the slave-girl of so-and-so.' He went and negotiated, then the agent said, 'the slave-girl has been specified for you.' And Allah knows best. His statement: (Otherwise the money from her wealth does not determine that it is for her, as we have presented) al-Ramli said: He presented that in case of mutual lying, the money decides by consensus, so consider. His statement: (And according to him in a non-suspicious place) perhaps what is meant is when it is after a defect. Then I saw in the gloss of Miskin: if you ask how is suspicion established? I say: by referring to experts; if they report that the price exceeds the value by an exorbitant increase, it is established, otherwise not. End. His statement: (And in al-Bazzaziyya attributed to al-Uyun, etc.) al-Ramli said: This branch is the branch we presented from al-Bazzaziyya also in the statement before this statement: 'Buy for me the slave-girl of so-and-so' and he remained silent, etc. His statement: (It is appropriate to apply the case of destruction or defect to when it is unpaid, etc.) because he presented that if the price is paid, the word is for the agent in all cases, including the case of destruction or defect. And al-Ramli said: There is no need for the burden of applying to when it is unpaid, with no mention of it at all, as is apparent, for the default is its absence. End. Meaning that the premise of the issue did not mention the price, and the default is its absence, so there is no need for what he said because it is the assumed.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a sharh (commentary) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing an intra-Hanafi dispute within the chapter on agency regarding sales and purchases. The core issue is when an agent and principal disagree about for whom the purchase was made. The base ruling is that if the agent says 'I bought for you' and the principal says 'No, you bought for yourself,' the principal's word is taken. However, if the principal had paid the price (i.e., given the money to the agent) and the agent claims he bought it for himself (thereby having to repay the price), then the agent is believed because he is a trustee seeking to exit his trust. The passage then records a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Hanifa holds that when the price is not paid, the agent's word is not taken because there is a suspicion that he might buy for himself and later, if the deal is bad, claim it was for the principal. The two companions say that even without payment, the agent's word is accepted because he has the authority to initiate a purchase, so no suspicion attaches. The text also discusses the distinction between agency for a specific item (mu'ayyan) vs. unspecified. If the agency is for a specific slave, there is unanimous agreement that the agent's word is taken, because he cannot buy that specific item for himself in the absence of the principal. The commentary further distinguishes between the slave being alive or dead, and whether the price was paid or not. The final ruling summarized: if the price is paid, agent's word in all cases; if unpaid and item is dead, principal's word; if unpaid and alive, the two companions say agent's word, Abu Hanifa says principal's word if there is suspicion (e.g., price far above value).

Key Terms
الآمر (al-āmir): The principal who authorizes the agent to purchase.
المأمور (al-ma'mūr): The agent who is ordered to make the purchase.
المنقود (al-manqūd): The price that has been actually paid (handed over) by the principal to the agent.
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion of bad faith, e.g., the agent buying for himself and then attributing it to the principal to avoid loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent is authorized to buy an unspecified slave, and the agent says 'I bought for you' but the principal says 'You bought for yourself,' and the price was not paid by the principal, what is the ruling according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)?
A) The principal's word is taken, and the slave is for the agent.
B) The agent's word is taken, and the principal must pay the price.
C) The transaction is invalid since there is a dispute.
D) It depends on whether the slave is dead or alive.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute over agency, the mufta bihi is that the agent's word is taken when the price is unpaid, unless suspicion of bad faith is established by expert valuation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on 'Sell Me This for So-and-So' and Denial of Agency
Ruling with proof on ambiguous agency in sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer says 'sell me this for so-and-so' and later denies the agency, and explain the legal reasoning distinguishing it from a fuduli purchase.

Core Idea
When a buyer says 'sell me for so-and-so,' he owns the item himself; the 'for' indicates purpose, not agency.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced scenario in sales: a buyer says 'sell me this for Zaid,' buys it, then denies Zaid ordered him. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies who owns the item and under what conditions. Understanding this ruling hinges on appreciating the Hanafi distinction between direct purchase and suspended fuduli contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the exact scenario: buyer says 'sell me this for so-and-so', buys it, then later denies that so-and-so ordered him. What is the ruling?
2
Determine the effect of the buyer's initial statement
The buyer's initial statement is an admission (iqrar) of agency. His later denial is ineffective. However, if the principal denies ordering the purchase, the admission is revoked and the principal does not take the item.
Contrast: if the principal denies, the buyer's admission is revoked.
3
Analyze the nature of the sale
The sale is actually binding on the buyer who spoke; he becomes the owner. The transfer to the principal is a separate sale by mutual delivery (mu'atah), so liability is on the buyer.
This is inferred from the exception 'unless the buyer hands it over'.
4
Distinguish from fuduli purchase
Saying 'sell me for so-and-so' adds the purchase to the speaker (the 'ya' of the speaker), not to the principal. Therefore it is not a suspended fuduli contract.
If the buyer had said 'sell to so-and-so,' it would be a fuduli purchase.
5
Examine the four categories of fuduli purchases from al-Karabisi
The passage cites four types: suspended when the agent does not add to himself, valid when the agent intends for another but adds to himself, void when there is mismatch of intent, etc. This clarifies when a sale is suspended vs valid.
The fourth type (void) is relevant if the buyer says 'I bought for so-and-so' and seller says 'I sold to you'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فللمأمور وإن قال يعني هذا لفلان فباعه ثم أنكر الأمر أخذه فلان إلا أن يقول لم آمره إلا أن يسلمه المشتري إليه
قوله : (وإن قال بعني هذا لفلان فباعه ثم أنكر الأمر أخذه (فلان) أي أنكر المشتري أن يكون فلان أمره بالشراء لأن قوله السابق إقرار منه بالوكالة عنه فلا ينفعه الإنكار اللاحق قوله : (إلا) أن يقول لم آمره (به أي فلان لم آمر المشتري بشرائه فإنه لا يأخذه فلان لأن الإقرار ارتد برده، ولم يذكر المؤلف أنه ينفذ الشراء على المشتري لكن قوله بعده (إلا أن يسلمه المشتري إليه يدل على أنه نفذ الشراء عليه وصار ملكاً له، ثم تسليمه بعده لفلان وأخذ فلان له بيع بالتعاطي فتكون العهدة عليه . وفي الهداية ودلت المسألة على أن التسليم على وجه البيع يكفي للتعاطي وإن لم يوجد نقد الثمن وهو يتحقق في النفيس والخسيس لاستتمام التراضي وهو المعتبر في الباب ا هـ . وقلت : ودلت أيضاً على أن بعني لفلان ليس إضافة إلى فلان إذ لو كان إضافة الشراء له لتوقف لقولهم إن شراء الفضولي لا يتوقف إلا إذا إضافه إلى غيره، وصورة إضافته إلى غير المشتري أن يقول بع عبدك من فلان كما في فتح القدير من بحث الفضولي فلم يضفه المشتري إلى نفسه، وإنما أضافه إلى الغير بخلاف بعني لفلان فإنه أضافه إلى ياء المتكلم. وقوله «الفلان» يحتمل بشفاعة فلان كما قال محمد : لو أن أجنبياً طلب من الشفيع تسليم هذه الدار فقال الشفيع سلمتها لك بطلت الشفعة كأنه قال لأجلك لكن إذا أقر فلان بالأمر جعلنا اللام للتمليك .. وفي فروق الكرابيسي : شراء الفضولي على أربعة أوجه : الأولى أن يقول البائع بعت هذا لفلان بكذا والفضولي يقول اشتريت لفلان بكذا أو قبلت ولم يقل لفلان فهذا يتوقف. الثاني أن يقول البائع بعت من فلان بكذا والمشتري يقول اشتريته لأجله أو قبلت يتوقف . الثالث بعت هذا منك بكذا فقال اشتريت أو قبلت ونوى أن يكون لفلان فإنه ينفذ عليه الرابع أن يقول اشتريت لفلان بكذا والبائع يقول بعت منك بطل العقد في أصح الروايتين ا هـ . قيد بالتسليم لأن فلاناً لو قال أجزت بعد قوله لم آمره لم يعتبر ذلك بل يكون العبد للمشتري لأن الإجازة تلحق الموقوف دون الجائز وهذا عقد جائز نافذ على المشتري؛ كذا في المعراج
For the agent, if he said 'meaning this for so-and-so' and sold it, then denied the order, so-and-so takes it unless he says 'I did not order him' unless the buyer hands it over to him. His statement: ('If he said 'sell me this for so-and-so' and sold it, then denied the order, so-and-so takes it) meaning the buyer denied that so-and-so ordered him to buy because his earlier statement is an admission of agency for him, so the subsequent denial does not benefit him. His statement: ('except') that he says 'I did not order him' meaning so-and-so did not order the buyer to buy it, then so-and-so does not take it because the admission is revoked by his denial. The author did not mention that the sale is binding on the buyer, but his subsequent statement ('except that the buyer hands it over to him') indicates that the sale was executed on him and it became his property, then his handing it over later to so-and-so and so-and-so's taking it is a sale by mutual delivery, so liability is on him. And in Al-Hidayah: This issue indicates that delivery in the form of sale suffices for mutual delivery even if the price is not paid, and this applies to valuable and cheap items due to complete mutual consent, which is the criterion in this chapter. And I said: It also indicates that 'sell me for so-and-so' is not an addition to so-and-so, because if it were an addition of purchase for him, it would be suspended according to their statement that the purchase of a fuduli is not suspended unless he adds it to another. The form of adding to someone other than the buyer is to say 'sell your slave to so-and-so' as in Fath al-Qadir from the discussion of fuduli. So the buyer did not add it to himself, but added it to another, unlike 'sell me for so-and-so' where he added it to the letter ya of the speaker. And his word 'al-fulan' is probable as intercession of so-and-so, as Muhammad said: If a stranger asked the pre-emptor to hand over this house, and the pre-emptor said 'I handed it over to you', the pre-emption is void, as if he said 'for your sake'. But when so-and-so admits the order, we make the lam for ownership. And in Furuk al-Karabisi: The purchase of a fuduli is of four types: first, the seller says 'I sold this for so-and-so for such' and the fuduli says 'I bought for so-and-so for such' or 'I accepted' and did not say 'for so-and-so', this is suspended. Second, the seller says 'I sold to so-and-so for such' and the buyer says 'I bought it for him' or 'accepted', it is suspended. Third, 'I sold this to you for such' and he said 'I bought' or 'accepted' and intended it for so-and-so, it is binding on him. Fourth, he says 'I bought for so-and-so for such' and the seller says 'I sold to you', the contract is void in the soundest of the two narrations. He restricted it by delivery because if so-and-so said 'I have ratified' after his saying 'I did not order him', it is not considered, rather the slave is for the buyer because ratification attaches to the suspended contract, not the valid one, and this is a valid contract binding on the buyer; as in al-Mi'raj.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It deals with the case where a buyer says to the seller: 'Sell me this for so-and-so' (meaning that he is buying on behalf of so-and-so as an agent). The ruling states that if the buyer then denies that so-and-so ordered him, so-and-so still takes the item (i.e., he is considered the real buyer) unless so-and-so himself denies having given the order. The commentator explains that the buyer's initial statement is an admission of agency, so his later denial is ineffective. However, if so-and-so denies the order, then the buyer's admission is revoked. The commentator also notes that the sale is actually executed on the buyer himself (the one who spoke), as indicated by the exception 'unless the buyer hands it over to him'—meaning that the buyer owns the item and then transfers it to so-and-so via mutual delivery (mu'atah). The passage then cites Al-Hidayah to support that delivery suffices for a valid sale. The commentator further analyzes that saying 'sell me for so-and-so' is not a direct addition to so-and-so but rather to the speaker, so it is not a suspended sale by a fuduli. Then the four categories of fuduli purchases from al-Karabisi are cited to clarify when a sale is suspended or valid. Finally, it is emphasized that if so-and-so later ratifies after denying, it is invalid because the contract is already binding on the buyer.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): The person who enters into a contract without being authorized by the owner or principal
التعاطي (al-ta'āṭī): A sale concluded by mutual delivery without verbal offer and acceptance
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Admission or confession; here it refers to the buyer's statement 'I buy for so-and-so' as an admission of agency

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, if a buyer says to a seller 'Sell me this for Zaid' and then buys it, who is considered the immediate owner in the original transaction?
A) Zaid, because the phrase 'for Zaid' indicates agency.
B) The buyer (the speaker), because the phrase adds the purchase to himself.
C) The seller, until he delivers.
D) The contract is void due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer says 'sell me this for so-and-so,' he is the direct purchaser and owner; the transfer to the intended beneficiary is a separate sale by mutual delivery.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buying One of Two Specified Slaves Without Price
Ruling on the validity of buying one of two specified slaves without a price, and the case of a total price for both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity of an agent buying one of two specified slaves without a named price, explain the condition of fair market price, and apply the proportional allocation rule when a total price is set for both.

Core Idea
An agent may buy one of two specified slaves if the price is fair; buying more than half the total requires the remainder to be purchased.

This ruling addresses a common scenario in agency contracts: the principal orders the purchase of two specific slaves but does not name a price. The agent then buys only one. Is it valid? The Hanafi school, relying on absolute agency and customary practice, declares it valid within reasonable price limits. The case of buying both for a lump sum introduces proportional allocation. This lesson unpacks these rules and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The agent buys one of two specified slaves when the order did not specify a price. The matn states it is valid (sahih).
The ruling is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, commented upon by al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Locate the condition (qayd)
The ruling is restricted to cases where the price is fair (qimah) or within customary tolerance (taghabun). If the agent pays an excessive amount (ma la yataghābanu al-nās fīhi), it is invalid by consensus.
This condition is derived from later parts of the text (مما يأتي) and is not explicit in the matn.
3
Analyze the case of total price (bishirā'ihimā bi-alf)
If the agent is ordered to buy both slaves for a total of 1000 and their values are equal, buying one for half (500) or less is valid. Buying for more than half is invalid unless the agent buys the remaining slave with the leftover amount before any dispute.
The reasoning: the total price implies each is worth half; buying for more is a detrimental violation (mukhālafatu ilā sharr).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu al-Qasim al-Saghar held that when the agent adds the purchase to the principal (e.g., 'I bought them for so-and-so'), the agent becomes a fuduli and the contract is suspended pending ratification. The commentator leans against this view, favoring the validity of the purchase.
This is a critical refinement (critical_refinement) showing ijtihad within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الشراء بهما موافقة وبأقل منهما مخالفة إلى خير وبالزيادة إلى شر . قلت : الزيادة أو كثرت ولذا أطلق في قوله و أو بعه من فلان ونحوه فلا ينفذ عليه أيضاً وقد وضحه هذا الشارح بقوله «ودلت أيضاً الخ» قوله : (وقد ذكرت ذلك لأستاذنا (فصوبني أي نسبني إلى الصواب وقال الرملي : أي قال لي أصبت وتقدم في شرح قوله والحقوق فيما يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه أن ابن ملك فهمه من الخلاصة والبزازية فراجع ذلك وانظر ما كتبناه في الحاشية اهـ . قلت : الذي مر عن الخلاصة والبزازية هناك مخالف لما فهمه ابن الملك وقدمنا أن الذي في ابن الملك منقول عن الفصول نقله عنها في شرحه على المجمع. نعم ما ذكره هنا بحثاً تقدم هناك في عبارة الخلاصة والبزازية حيث قال : وقال أبو القاسم الصغار : الصحيح أن الوكيل يصير فضولياً ويتوقف العقد على إجازة الموكل اهـ. وانظر ما كتبناه هناك عن نور العين قوله : (ولم يذكر الشارحون فائدة التقييد بالمعينين الخ) قال في حاشية مسكين بعد نقله : وتبعه بعضهم كالحموي والدر وغيرهما وأقول دعوى أن التقييد اتفاقي غير مسلم لأنه عند عدم التعيين يبطل التوكيل لعدم تسمية الثمن أو ما يقوم مقامه من بيان النوع كالتركي والحبشي فهذا غفلة عن قول المصنف فيما سبق قريباً «أمره بشراء عبد أو دار صح إن سمى ثمنا وإلا فلا» اهـ. أقول: بيان الثمن أو النوع لا يخرجه عن كونه غير معين وقد قدم المؤلف أن الإضافة إلى المالك مثل جارية فلان لا تعينه، ونقل هناك عن البزازية وكله بشراء عبد بغير عينه فاشترى من قطعت يده نفذ على الموكل
And if he ordered him to buy two specific slaves and did not name a price, and he bought for him and said 'I bought them for so-and-so', it is for his principal; and if he said 'I bought them for myself', it is for him; and if he said 'I bought them without addition' then said before it develops a defect or perishes 'I bought them for so-and-so', it is for so-and-so; but after its perishing or developing a defect, it is not accepted without the principal's confirmation. And if he buys both for a total of one thousand and their value is equal, and he buys one for half or less, it is valid; but with more it is not valid unless he buys the remaining with what remains before the dispute. His statement: ('And if he ordered him to buy two specific slaves and did not name a price, and he bought one of them, it is valid') because the agency is absolute and it may not be possible to combine both in the sale. He released it but it is restricted to when he buys it for its value or with a slight overpayment that people tolerate; as for what is intolerable, it is not allowed by consensus. The excuse is that he will restrict the agent's purchase later, so he left it here. The commentators did not mention the benefit of specifying 'specific'; the apparent is that it is coincidental, so unspecified is like specified if he intends it for the principal or buys for him. His statement: ('And if he buys both for a thousand and their value is equal, and he buys one for half or less, it is valid; but with more it is not valid unless he buys the remaining with what remains before the dispute') because he placed the thousand against both and their value is equal, so it is divided between them indicating two halves, so he ordered to buy each one for five hundred. Then buying both is compliance, buying less is beneficial violation, buying more is detrimental. I said: The increase is rare, so he generalized in his statement... and 'or sell from so-and-so' etc. does not bind him either, and this commentator clarified it with his statement 'it also indicates' etc. His statement: ('I mentioned that to my teacher and he corrected me') meaning he attributed correctness to me. Al-Ramli said: meaning he said 'you are correct'. And it preceded in the explanation of his statement 'the rights in what the agent adds to himself' that Ibn Malik understood it from al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya, so refer to that and see what we wrote in the marginalia. I said: What passed from al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya there is contrary to what Ibn Malik understood, and we stated that what is in Ibn Malik is transmitted from al-Fusul quoted in his commentary on al-Majma. Yes, what he mentioned here as a discussion preceded there in the statement of al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya where he said: Abu al-Qasim al-Saghar said: The correct view is that the agent becomes a fuduli and the contract is suspended pending the principal's ratification. And see what we wrote there from Nur al-Ayn. His statement: ('The commentators did not mention the benefit of specifying 'specific' etc.') He said in Hashiyat Miskin after quoting: Some followed him like al-Hamawi and al-Durr etc. And I say: The claim that the specification is coincidental is not accepted, because without specification the agency is invalid due to lack of naming the price or what substitutes it like specifying the type (e.g., Turkish or Abyssinian). This is negligence regarding the author's statement earlier: 'He ordered him to buy a slave or a house; it is valid if he named a price, otherwise no.' I say: Stating the price or type does not remove the item from being unspecified, and the author has stated that adding to the owner (like 'so-and-so's slave') does not specify it. And he quoted from al-Bazzaziyya: 'He appointed him to buy a slave without specification, and he bought one whose hand was cut off; it binds the principal.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on agency in purchase. The matn states that if someone is ordered to buy two specific slaves without specifying a price, and the agent buys one of them, it is valid. The commentary explains that since the agency is absolute, it includes the possibility of buying only one, as it may not be feasible to buy both together. However, this is restricted to cases where the price is fair or within customary tolerance; an excessive price is not allowed by consensus. The restriction of 'specific' slaves is noted as likely coincidental, as an unspecified slave could be treated similarly if intended for the principal. The second part deals with a scenario where the agent is told to buy both slaves for a total price (e.g., 1000) when their values are equal. Buying one for half or less is valid because the total price implies each is worth half; buying for more is not allowed unless he later buys the remaining with the leftover amount before any dispute arises. The commentary also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the agent becomes a fuduli when he adds the purchase to the principal, citing Abu al-Qasim al-Saghar's view that the contract is then suspended. This is a critical refinement. Finally, the issue of specifying the item is addressed: some argue that without specification the agency is invalid, but the commentator counters that even a general agency can be valid if the price or type is mentioned.

Key Terms
المعين (al-mu'ayyan): Specified or identified; here refers to slaves that are individually distinguished
التغابن (al-taghābun): The slight difference in price that buyers and sellers normally tolerate; 'ما يتغابن الناس فيه' refers to acceptable market fluctuation
الفضولي (al-fuduli): In this context, an agent who adds the purchase to the principal without clear authority, making the contract suspended

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where an agent is ordered to buy two specific slaves for a total of 1000 dirhams, and their values are equal, what happens if he buys one for 600 dirhams?
A) The purchase is void because it exceeds half the total price
B) The purchase is valid only if he buys the other with the remaining 400
C) The purchase is valid and binding on the principal
D) The purchase is suspended pending the principal's approval

Key Takeaway
Buying one of two specified slaves is valid if the price is fair; when both are ordered for a total price, each is proportionally allocated and buying more than half requires the remainder to be purchased.

Price Dispute in Agency: Principal vs Agent
States the ruling from Al-Hidayah on price dispute between principal and agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on price disputes in agency and apply the conditions for when the principal's word is taken versus when mutual oaths are taken.

Core Idea
If an agent overpays relative to market value, the principal's word prevails; if the price matches value, mutual oaths annul the contract.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in agency: the principal gives a general order to buy but no specific price. When a dispute arises about the price, the ruling depends on whether the agent acted contrary to the principal's implicit instruction by paying an excessive amount. Understanding this ruling requires mastering the concepts of mukhālafa (violation) and taḥāluf (mutual oaths).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The principal (al-āmir) claims a lower price; the agent (al-mā'mūr) claims a higher one. The seller confirms the agent's claim. The dispute is about the price actually agreed upon.
2
Determine the market value
The ruling hinges on whether the market value of the item equals the agent's claimed price or is lower. The principal's implicit instruction is to buy at a fair price, typically the market value.
If the value is unknown, the passage does not provide a ruling; it is left unresolved.
3
Assess if the agent violated (mukhālafa)
If the market value is lower than the price the agent claims, the agent violated the principal's implicit instruction. This is because the principal's order encompassed buying a thing worth a certain amount, and the agent paid more.
Violation triggers liability (ḍamān).
4
Apply the ruling based on violation
If the agent violated (value < agent's claimed price), the principal's word is taken, and the agent is liable for the difference. If the agent did not violate (value = claimed price), the parties take mutual oaths (taḥāluf), which annuls the contract and makes the item the agent's responsibility.
Oaths are taken because the principal and agent are treated like seller and buyer when no violation occurred.
5
Understand the consequence of taḥāluf
When taḥāluf occurs, the original contract is rescinded, and the agent must take the item (since it was bought in his capacity) and pay the seller. The principal is free from liability.
This mirrors the ruling in sale disputes where parties take oaths and the contract is set aside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد ولم يسم كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء ثمناً فقال المأمور اشتريته بألف وصدقه البائع وقال الآمر بنصفه تحالفاً مقيد بما إذا كانت تساوي ألفاً فإن كانت تساوي خمسمائة فالقول للآمر لأنه خالف حيث اشترى جارية تساوي خمسمائة والأمر تناول ما يساوي ألفاً فيضمن؛ كذا في الهداية، ولم يذكر ما إذا كانت قيمتها بينهما قوله : (وإن لم يدفع (فللآمر أي وإن لم يكن دفع إليه الألف فالقول للآمر . أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا كانت قيمتها خمسمائة لكونه مخالفاً، وأما إذا كانت قيمتها ألفاً فإنهما يتحالفان لأن الموكل والوكيل نزلاً منزلة البائع والمشتري وقد اختلفا في ثم يفسخ العقد الذي جرى بينهما حكماً فتلزم الجارية المأمور .
The slave and was not named. Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. Then the agent said: I bought it for a thousand, and the seller confirmed him, and the principal said: for half of it, they take oaths, conditioned upon it being worth a thousand. If it is worth five hundred, then the statement is that of the principal because he violated, as he bought a slave-girl worth five hundred and the order covered what is worth a thousand, so he is liable; thus in Al-Hidayah. And he did not mention if its value is between them. His statement: 'And if he did not pay, then it is for the principal' meaning if he did not give him the thousand, then the statement is that of the principal. He released it, and it is conditioned upon its value being five hundred because he violated. But if its value is a thousand, then they take oaths because the principal and agent are placed in the position of seller and buyer, and they disputed, then the contract that occurred between them is annulled legally, so the slave-girl is binding on the agent.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from Al-Hidayah about a dispute between a principal (al-āmir) and an agent (al-mā'mūr) regarding the purchase price of an item, when the agent was not given a specific price to buy at. The ruling depends on the actual market value of the item. If the agent bought an item worth 500 for 1000, he violated the implicit instruction to buy at a fair price, so the principal's word is taken and the agent is liable for the difference. If the item is worth exactly 1000, the agent did not violate, but the parties disagree on the price; they then take mutual oaths (taḥāluf) and the contract is annulled, with the item becoming the agent's responsibility. This is a well-known Hanafi ruling from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, as recorded by Imam al-Sarakhsī in Al-Mabsūṭ and others.

Key Terms
تحالف (taḥāluf): Mutual oaths taken by two parties disputing a contract, leading to its rescission.
مخالف (mukhālif): An agent who acts contrary to the principal's implicit instructions, e.g., buying at an excessive price relative to value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from Al-Hidayah, when do the principal and agent take mutual oaths (taḥāluf) in a price dispute?
A) When the market value is less than the agent's claimed price.
B) When the market value equals the agent's claimed price.
C) When the market value is greater than the agent's claimed price.
D) In all cases of price dispute without exception.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on price disputes in agency hinges on whether the agent violated the implicit instruction to buy at a fair price, determined by market value.

The Agent's Oath in a Price Dispute: Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath requirement and mutual oaths in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether an agent requires an oath in a price dispute, apply the general rule that a trustee's statement is accepted with an oath, and identify the circumstances requiring mutual oaths (tahāluf).

Core Idea
A trustee's statement is accepted only with an oath — the agent's claim requires swearing, and mutual oaths may follow.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi debate: does the agent in a price dispute need to take an oath when asserting the price? Some later scholars claimed no oath is needed, but the relied-upon Hanafi position, based on the texts of al-Hidayah and general principles of trust (amānah), requires the agent to swear. We will also explore the related issue of mutual oaths (tahāluf) when the value equals the claimed price.

Guided Path
1
State the relied-upon Hanafi position
Identify the default rule: the agent's statement is accepted only with an oath. The agent is a trustee (amīn) and trustees always swear when claiming discharge.
This is the position of al-Hidayah and the author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some later Hanafi scholars (Ṣadr al-Sharī'a, Ibn al-Kamāl, author of al-Durar) hold that the agent's word is accepted without an oath.
They rely on silence of al-Hidayah in one scenario and the phrase 'bila yamīn' in some texts.
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Review the textual proofs from al-Hidayah ('al-qawl qawl al-ma'mūr ma'a yamīnih') and Nūr al-'Ayn ('al-qawl fī kull amānah lil-amīn ma'a yamīnih'). Also consider the rational argument: judging based on a mere statement without oath is far-fetched.
The general principle for trustees requires an oath.
4
Analyze the response to the opposition's silence argument
The opposition argues that silence of al-Hidayah in one scenario implies no oath. The response: silence is because the necessity is obvious; the explicit mention elsewhere settles the rule.
Also, the phrase 'bila yamīn' might be a textual corruption.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical difference: without oath, the agent could be burdened without defense; with oath, he can swear to avoid liability. The mufta bihi is the view requiring the oath, and in some cases mutual oaths (tahāluf) are taken.
Author prefers the view of Abū Manṣūr, which is aẓhar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن كانت تساوي خمسمائة فالقول للآمر زاد في الدرر تبعاً لصدر الشريعة بلا يمين، وعبارة الصدر وابن الكمال والمراد بقوله صدق في جميع ما ذكر التصديق بغير الحلف، وفي حاشية العلامة الواني على الدرر يمينه» بدل على أن المأمور يصدق فيما قال وفي التحالف لا يصدق واحد منهما ولو كان مراده التحالف لما قال ذلك. قوله : (وقدمنا بحثاً الخ) أي في أول كتاب الوكالة قوله : (بخلاف الوكيل بشراء العبد من غيره الجار والمجرور في قوله متعلق بالوكيل قال في الكافية : أي بخلاف ما لو وكله غير العبد
His statement: (If it is equal to five hundred, then the statement is that of the principal) He added in al-Durar following Sadr al-Shariah: without an oath. And the expression of al-Sadr and Ibn al-Kamal: the meaning of his statement 'he confirms' in all that he mentioned is confirmation without an oath. And in the gloss of al-Allama al-Wani on al-Durar, I say: what the commentator mentioned of his statement 'without an oath' is contrary to reason and transmission. As for reason, because if the statement is that of the principal, he judges that the slave is binding on the agent, so this judgment by mere statement of the opponent without his oath is very far-fetched. As for transmission, because he said in al-Hidayah: 'If he ordered him to buy for him this slave and did not name a price, and he bought it, and the principal said I bought it for five hundred, and the agent said for a thousand, and the seller confirmed the agent, then the statement is that of the agent with his oath.' So the confirmation of the seller, if the agent needs to be made to take an oath, then without it is more appropriate. If it is said: the silence of the author of al-Hidayah and others about mentioning the oath in the previous case, and their dealing with it in this case suggests that the oath is not obligatory in it as the commentator said. We say: perhaps their silence in the mentioned case is based on its obviousness, while their dealing with it in this case is to prepare for the explanation of the upcoming difference: whether only the oath is obligatory or mutual oaths between the two sides. Do not say if the gross defect is not binding on the principal, whether he swears or not, then there is no benefit, and the commentator's statement 'without an oath' is in its place, because we say: its benefit is that the agent may be harmed by the slave remaining on him, so if he makes the principal take an oath, he may say he bought it for more, and a similar objection refutes Sadr al-Shariah as well, for he said without the oath, as if it is the commentator's source. And it is possible that the word 'without' (bighayri) is a corruption of 'after' (ba'da), and this is the weakest guidance, and Allah is the Guide. End. And that was also objected in al-Hawashi al-Ya'qubiyya where he said: this is not mentioned in other than this book, and there is discussion: he declared in al-Kafi in the previous case mentioned in the text: 'If he said I bought a slave for the principal and he died and the principal said etc.' that the meaning of confirming the agent is confirming him with his oath because the price was a trust in his hand and he claimed to have discharged the trust in the manner ordered, so the statement is his, and there is no difference in confirming the agent due to being a trustee between one place and another, so the clarification in one place suffices, so the commentator's statement is not complete, as is not hidden, so reflect. End. I say: and it was mentioned in Nur al-Ayn in the chapters on oath before the sixteenth chapter: the statement in every trust is for the trustee with his oath, and likewise the evidence is his evidence, and the guarantor is accepted with his evidence, not his oath on fulfillment. End. Based on this, how can the statement be for the agent without an oath in the first case, and likewise how can it be for the principal without an oath in the second? So reflect. His statement: (And he did not mention if its value is) exclusive. Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. 279. The principal says half of it, they take oaths due to the difference in price, and we have preceded it. And it was said: there is no tahaluf here because the dispute is removed by the confirmation of the seller, as he is present, and in the first case he is absent, so the dispute is considered. And it was said: they take oaths as we mentioned, and most of the oath of tahaluf is mentioned, and it is the oath of the seller; the seller after receiving the price is a stranger to them both, and before it he is a stranger to the principal, as no contract between them was valid, so he is not believed, so the dispute remains. This is the statement of the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur, and it is more apparent; thus in al-Hidayah. The summary: the correction differs; Qadikhan corrected the absence of tahaluf following al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, and the author in al-Kafi corrected tahaluf following al-Hidayah, based on the interpretation of his statement 'more apparent' as meaning 'more correct' as in al-Mi'raj. As for Imam Muhammad, he only stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that the statement is for the agent with his oath. Some looked at its apparent meaning and said he negated tahaluf, and some said his intent is tahaluf based on what he mentioned elsewhere of its occurrence when they differ, and he only mentioned the oath of the agent here because he is the claimant, and there is no oath on him except in tahaluf, so he was the intended one, and the principal is the denier, so the oath is apparent on him, so he did not need to clarify both. It is conditioned upon their agreement that he did not name a price, because if they differed in naming it, and the principal said: I ordered you to buy it for me for five hundred, and the agent said: you ordered me to buy it for a thousand, then the statement is that of the principal with his oath, because that is derived from his side, so his statement is taken, and the slave is binding on the agent due to his violation. If they both produce evidence, then the evidence of the agent is more worthy because it is more confirming. And we have presented a discussion: if the principal gave money to give to another, and he gave it, then they differed, and the principal said: I only ordered you to give it to someone else, and the agent said: you ordered me to give it to him, that the statement is for the agent and there is no liability on him because he is a trustee, and we adduced for it a branch in mudaraba. It may be difficult for him what they mentioned here, because both agents are trustees, but the agent for purchase is placed in the position of a seller. The end of the matter: when the order is not proven, he leaves being a seller and the purchase is executed on him and no liability attaches to him, unlike the agent for receipt, because liability attaches to him if his statement is not accepted, even though he is a trustee, so they differ. Unless there is a transmission, then it must be followed. My statement here that they agree on not naming the price is better than the statement of the commentator. This is in the case that they agree that he ordered him to buy it for a thousand, because the issue was presented by the author and others in cases where he did not name. It is understood from the expression of Ibn al-Kamal in al-Islah: if he gave him the thousand, he is believed if it is equal, otherwise for the principal; and if he did not give him the thousand and it is equal to less, then the principal is believed, and if equal, they take oaths. His statement: (And some said his intent is tahaluf etc.) Al-Zayla'i questioned it: although it indicates what they mentioned in terms of meaning, his wording does not indicate that nor the first. Because his statement 'the statement is for the agent with his oath' indicates that the agent is believed in what he says, and in tahaluf neither is believed, and if he intended tahaluf, he would not have said that. His statement: (And we have presented a discussion etc.) i.e., at the beginning of the Book of Agency. His statement: (Unlike the agent for buying the slave from someone else) the prepositional phrase is attached to 'the agent'. He said in al-Kafiya: i.e., unlike if he appointed him for other than the slave.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the agent's statement in a price dispute requires an oath. The author of Al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, while commenting on Kanz al-Daqā'iq, engages with the claim from some later scholars (e.g., Ṣadr al-Sharī'a, Ibn al-Kamāl) that the agent's word is accepted without an oath. He refutes this vigorously, arguing from reason and from the text of Al-Hidāyah, which explicitly requires the agent to swear. He also cites Nūr al-'Ayn and other sources showing that a trustee (amīn) must always swear when claiming to discharge the trust. The discussion then extends to whether there is mutual tahāluf (both parties swear) or just an oath from the agent. The author notes differences among Ḥanafī authorities: Qāḍīkhān following Abū Ja'far denies tahāluf, while the author of al-Kāfī affirms it, following Al-Hidāyah. The text also resolves the apparent contradiction from Imām Muḥammad's statement in al-Jāmi' al-Ṣaghīr: some understood it as negating tahāluf, but the preferable interpretation is that he intended tahāluf, and his mention of the agent's oath alone is sufficient because the principal's oath is obvious. The passage concludes with a discussion of analogous cases and the distinction between an agent for purchase and an agent for receipt. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position in the Ḥanafī school is that the agent must take an oath, and if the item's value equals the claimed price, there is mutual tahāluf.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): An oath taken by the agent to confirm his claim about the price or discharge of trust.
تحالف (taḥāluf): Mutual oaths taken by buyer and seller (or principal and agent) when they dispute the price, leading to rescission of the contract.
أمين (amīn): A trustee; the agent is an amīn regarding the price and the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how is a price dispute between principal and agent resolved when the item's market value equals the agent's claimed price?
A) The agent's word is taken without an oath
B) The principal's word is taken with an oath
C) They take mutual oaths (tahāluf)
D) The dispute is dismissed because no defect exists

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a trustee (amīn) must always swear to confirm his claim; in agency price disputes, the agent's oath is required and may lead to mutual oaths when the price matches the market value.

Mutual Oath in Principal-Agent Price Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahaluf in agency price disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding tahaluf when principal and agent dispute the price without prior specification, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When principal and agent disagree on price, the seller's confirmation does not settle the dispute—mutual oath is required.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue in Hanafi fiqh: when a principal and agent disagree on the purchase price after agreeing that no price was specified, does the seller's confirmation resolve the dispute, or must they take mutual oaths? The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two Hanafi views, traced from Imam Muhammad's text through later authorities like Qadikhan and al-Hidayah. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate procedural rulings in sales and agency contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The principal and agent agree that no price was named when the agent was appointed. After purchase, they differ on what price was actually paid. Does the mutual oath procedure (tahaluf) apply?
The seller is present and confirms one side, but the principal and agent remain in dispute.
2
Examine the two Hanafi positions
One position (Qadikhan, following al-Faqih Abu Ja'far) holds that no tahaluf is needed because the seller's confirmation removes the disagreement. The other (Abu Mansur, preferred in al-Hidayah) holds that tahaluf is required because the seller is a stranger to the principal-agent contract.
Both positions are based on interpretations of Imam Muhammad's statement in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
3
Interpret Imam Muhammad's text
Imam Muhammad said: 'The word is of the agent with his oath.' Some scholars take this at face value to negate tahaluf; others argue it implies tahaluf because the agent is the claimant and only swears in a mutual oath scenario.
The difference hinges on whether the agent's oath is part of a mutual oath or a standalone oath.
4
Analyze the proofs for each side
Those against tahaluf argue the seller's testimony eliminates the dispute. Those for tahaluf argue the seller is an outsider (ajnabi) to the principal-agent contract, so his statement does not bind the principal; the disagreement persists.
The preferred view in al-Hidayah is deemed 'azhar' (more apparent).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
According to al-Hidayah and al-Kafi, the mufta bihi is that they do take mutual oath. This is because the seller's confirmation does not remove the disagreement, and the agent's oath alone does not settle the matter.
Qadikhan's view is considered weaker; the majority of later Hanafi authorities follow al-Hidayah.

Source Text

الآمر بنصفه تحالفا للاختلاف في الثمن ،وقدمناه وقيل لا تحالف هنا لأنه ارتفع الخلاف بتصديق البائع إذ هو حاضر، وفي المسألة الأولى هو غائب فاعتبر الاختلاف. وقيل: يتحالفان كما ذكرنا وقد ذكر معظم يمين التحالف وهو يمين البائع والبائع بعد استيفاء الثمن أجنبي عنهما وقبله أجنبي عن الموكل إذ لم يجز بينهما عقد فلا يصدق عليه فبقي الخلاف، وهذا قول الشيخ الإمام أبي منصور وهو أظهر؛ كذا في الهداية. والحاصل أن التصحيح قد اختلف فصحح قاضيخان عدم التحالف تبعاً للفقيه أبي جعفر، وصحح المصنف في الكافي التحالف تبعاً للهداية بناء على أن قوله أظهر» بمعنى أصح كما في المعراج . وأما الإمام محمد فإنما نص في الجامع ولا يمين عليه إلا في التحالف فكان هو المقصود والموكل منكر واليمين عليه ظاهراً فلم يحتج إلى بيانهما. قيد باتفاقهما على أنه لم يسم له ثمناً لأنهما لو اختلفا في تسميته فقال الآمر أمرتك أن تشتريه لي بخمسمائة وقال المأمور أمرتني بالشراء بألف فالقول قول الآمر مع يمينه لأن ذلك يستفاد من جهته فكان القول قوله ويلزم العبد المأمور لمخالفته فإن أقاما البينة فالبينة بينة الوكيل لأنها أكثر إثباتاً، وقدمنا بحثاً لو دفع الآمر مالاً ليدفعه إلى آخر فدفعه ثم اختلفا فقال الأمر إنما أمرتك بدفعه إلى غيره وقال المأمور أمرتني بالدفع إليه أن القول للمأمور ولا ضمان عليه لكونه أميناً، واستشهدنا له بفرع في المضاربة فربما يشكل عليه ما ذكروه هنا بجامع أن ذلك يستفاد من جهته وكل من الوكيلين أمين لكن الوكيل بالشراء منزل منزلة البائع، فغاية الأمر أنه لما لم يثبت الأمر خرج عن أن يكون بائعاً ونفذ الشراء عليه ولم يلحقه ضمان بخلاف الوكيل بالقبض فإنه يلحقه الضمان لو لم يقبل قوله مع أنه أمين فافترقا إلا أن يوجد نقل فيجب اتباعه. وقولي هنا إنهما اتفقا على عدم تسمية الثمن أولى من قول الشارح، وهذا فيما إذا اتفقا على أنه أمره أن يشتريه له بألف إذ المسألة إنما فرضها المؤلف وغيره فيما إذا لم يسم بينهما يفهم من عبارة ابن الكمال في الإصلاح فإن أعطاه الألف صدق هو إن ساواه وإلا فالأمر وإن لم يكن أعطاه الألف وساوى أقل منه The principal with half [unclear], they take mutual oath due to disagreement on the price, and we have mentioned it. And it was said: there is no mutual oath here because the disagreement is removed by the seller's confirmation since he is present, whereas in the first case [the seller] is absent so the disagreement is considered. And it was said: they take mutual oath as we mentioned, and most [scholars] have mentioned the oath of mutual oath which is the seller's oath, and the seller after receiving the price is a stranger to both, and before it he is a stranger to the principal since no contract was concluded between them, so he is not believed [to resolve the dispute], and thus the disagreement remains. This is the statement of the Shaykh al-Imam Abu Mansur, and it is more apparent; as in al-Hidayah. As for Imam Muhammad, he stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that the word is of the agent with his oath. Some looked at its apparent meaning and said he negated mutual oath, while others [said] his intent was mutual oath due to evidence he mentioned elsewhere of its occurrence, that it is when they differ, and he only specified the agent's oath here because he is the claimant and there is no oath on him except in mutual oath, so he was the intended [party], while the principal is the defendant and the oath is on him apparently, so there was no need to mention both. Condition: they agree that no price was named for him, because if they differ on naming it and the principal says 'I ordered you to buy it for me for five hundred' and the agent says 'You ordered me to buy it for a thousand,' then the word is of the principal with his oath because that [information] is obtained from his side, so his word is accepted, and the agent [slave?] is compelled for his violation. If they both bring evidence, then the evidence of the agent is preferred because it is more establishing. And we have previously presented a discussion: if the principal gave money to [the agent] to give to another, and he gave it, then they differed, and the principal said 'I only ordered you to give it to someone else' and the agent said 'You ordered me to give it to him,' then the word is of the agent and there is no liability on him because he is a trustee. We adduced for this a sub-case in mudarabah. Perhaps what they mention here might be problematic with that, due to the common factor that [the specification] is obtained from his side, and both agents are trustees, but the purchasing agent is placed in the position of the seller. The upshot: since the order is not proven, he departs from being a seller and the purchase is executed on him without incurring liability, unlike the agent for receipt, who incurs liability if his word is not accepted despite being a trustee, so they differ. Unless there is a transmitted [text], then it must be followed. And my statement here that they agree on the absence of naming the price is more appropriate than the commentator's statement. This applies when they agree that he ordered him to buy it for him for a thousand, because the issue is presupposed by the author and others when he did not name between them. It is understood from the statement of Ibn al-Kamal in al-Islah: if he gave him the thousand, he [the agent] is believed if it is equal; otherwise, the principal [is believed]. If he did not give him the thousand and it is equal to less, the principal is believed; if equal, they take mutual oath.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a dispute between principal (muwakkil) and agent (wakil) over the price when both agree that no specific price was named in the agency contract. The author presents multiple Hanafi views on whether mutual oath (tahaluf) applies. The primary ruling from Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir is that the agent's word is taken with his oath, which some scholars interpret as negating tahaluf, while others see it as implying tahaluf since the agent is the claimant. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) according to al-Hidayah is that of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi that they do take mutual oath because the seller's testimony does not resolve the dispute. Qadikhan followed al-Faqih Abu Ja'far in negating tahaluf, while the author of al-Kafi followed al-Hidayah. The passage then turns to a related case: if they agree no price was named but later differ on what the intended price was, the principal's word is taken with his oath because the specification comes from him. However, if the agent brings evidence, his evidence is preferred as it is more establishing. A comparison is made with an agent for delivery of money: if the principal gives money to the agent to give to someone, and they differ on the recipient, the agent's word is accepted without liability because he is a trustee (amin). The purchasing agent is different because he is like a seller; once the purchase is executed, he is not liable, whereas the agent for receipt would be liable if his word were not accepted. The author notes that his phrasing (that they agree on no price naming) is more precise than the commentator's. The passage ends with a note that if the principal gave the agent the exact amount, the agent is believed if it matches the price; otherwise, the principal's word is taken, and if equal, they take mutual oath.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath where both parties swear to their claims, and the contract is dissolved if they cannot reconcile.
الوكيل بالشراء (al-wakil bi al-shira'): Agent appointed to purchase something on behalf of the principal.
الوكيل بالقبض (al-wakil bi al-qabd): Agent appointed to receive or take possession of something (e.g., money or goods).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between principal and agent over the price when no price was named, what is the relied-upon Hanafi position regarding mutual oath?
A) No mutual oath is needed because the seller's confirmation settles it.
B) Mutual oath is required because the seller is a stranger to the principal.
C) The agent's word is taken without oath.
D) The principal's word is taken without oath.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that mutual oath (tahaluf) is required when principal and agent disagree on price without prior specification, as the seller's confirmation does not bind the principal.
