Agent Buys Principal Himself from His Master: Manumission or Sale?
Ruling on agent buying the principal (slave) from master: manumission vs sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys the principal (a slave) from his master, and distinguish the consequences based on the agent's declaration.

Core Idea
When a slave buys himself through an agent, it is manumission in legal effect, not a sale.

This lesson examines a unique scenario in Hanafi fiqh where agency intersects with slavery and manumission. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the critical role of the agent's declaration in determining whether the transaction is a manumission or a sale. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct legal consequences, such as patronage (wala') and liability for the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two declarations by the agent
The agent may say 'I bought him for myself' or simply 'I bought him' without clarification. The wording determines the legal nature of the transaction.
The first declaration triggers manumission; the second triggers a sale.
2
Understand the legal basis for manumission
When the agent says 'for myself' meaning for the slave (the principal), the purchase is treated as if the slave bought himself, which is legally manumission (i'taq) because the slave cannot own himself.
The agent is merely a safir (messenger), so rights do not revert to him.
3
Trace the consequences of each declaration
If manumission: the slave is freed, patronage (wala') remains with the master, and the slave owes compensation. If sale: the agent owns the slave and owes the price to the master.
The thousand paid is always for the master, but additional payment may be required.
4
Note the rationale for the agent's clarification requirement
Unlike ordinary agency, here the two possible contracts (manumission vs sale) have different legal effects, and the master may prefer a sale, so clarification is necessary to determine intent.
Without clarification, the literal meaning of sale is preserved.
5
Apply the rule to sub-cases
The text mentions that if the slave buys himself with a partner, only his share is valid; also, manumission is immune to khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) and corrupt conditions.
These consequences reinforce that the transaction is legally manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشراء نفس الأمر من سيده بألف ودفع فقال لسيده اشتريته لنفسه فباعه على هذا عتق وولاؤه لسيده وإن قال اشتريته فالعبد للمشتري والألف لسيده وعلى المشتري ألف مثله لأن بيع نفس العبد منه إعتاق وشراء العبد نفسه قبول الإعتاق ببدل والمأمور سفير عنه إذ لا ترجع عليه الحقوق فصار كأنه اشترى نفسه بنفسه، وإذا كان إعتاقاً أعقب الولاء وإن لم يبين للمولى فهو عبد للمشتري لأن اللفظ حقيقته للمعاوضة وأمكن العمل بها إذا لم يبين فيحافظ عليه بخلاف شراء العبد نفسه لأن المجاز فيه متعين وإذا كان معاوضة يثبت الملك له والألف للمولى لأنه كسب عبده وعلى المشتري ألف مثله ثمناً للعبد فإنه في ذمته حيث لم الأداء بخلاف الوكيل بشراء العبد من غيره حيث لا يشترط بيانه لأن العقدين هناك على نمط واحد ففي الحالين المطالبة تتوجه نحو العاقد، وأما ههنا أحدهما إعتاق معقب للولاء ولا مطالبة على الوكيل والمولى عساه لا يرضاه ويرغب في المعاوضة المحضة فلا بد من البيان . وقوله والألف لسيده راجع إلى المسألتين، وكان ينبغي أن يقول بعده وعلى العبد ألف أخرى بدل الإعتاق وعلى المشتري في الثانية ألف ثمن العبد لبطلان الأداء فيهما لاستحقاق المولى ما أداه بجهة أخرى وهو أنه كسب عبده فكان مملوكاً له قبل الشراء وقبل العتق . وأشار باحتياج الوكيل إلى إضافته إلى العبد الموكل إلى أنه سفير لا ترجع الحقوق إليه فالمطالبة بالألف الأخرى على العبد لا على الوكيل وهو الصحيح، وحيث علم أن شراء العبد نفسه من مولاه إعتاق معنى وإن كان شراء صورة لم تعتبر فيه أحكام الشراء، ولذا صرح في المعراج بأنه إذا اشترى نفسه إلى العطاء فعلى هذا لا يبطل بالشرط الفاسد ولا يدخله خيار شرط . وفي بيوع الخانية من الاستحقاق : عبد اشترى نفسه من مولاه ومعه رجل آخر بألف واحدة؛ ذكر في المنتقى أنه يجوز في حصة العبد وحصة الشريك باطل ولا يشبه هذا الأب إذا اشترى ولده مع رجل آخر بألف درهم فإنه يجوز العقد في الكل ا هـ
And (the case of) buying the principal himself from his master for a thousand and (the agent) paid, and said to his master 'I bought him for myself' and he sold him on this basis: he is freed and his patronage is for his master. If he said 'I bought him' then the slave is for the buyer and the thousand is for his master, and the buyer owes a thousand like it. Because the sale of the slave himself from him is manumission, and the slave's purchase of himself is acceptance of manumission in exchange for a consideration, and the agent is a messenger for him since rights do not revert to him. So it is as if he bought himself by himself. And if it is manumission, it entails patronage. If he did not clarify to the master, then he is a slave for the buyer, because the literal meaning of the utterance is for exchange and it is possible to act upon it when he did not clarify, so it is preserved. Unlike the slave's purchase of himself, because the metaphorical [meaning] is necessary there. And if it is exchange, ownership is established for him and the thousand is for the master because it is the acquisition of his slave; and the buyer owes a thousand like it as the price of the slave, it being in his responsibility since he did not pay. Unlike the agent's purchase of the slave from someone else, where his clarification is not required, because the two contracts there are of one pattern, so in both cases the claim is directed towards the contractor. But here, one of them is manumission that entails patronage, and there is no claim on the agent, and the master may not be satisfied with it and may desire pure exchange, so clarification is necessary. And his statement 'and the thousand is for his master' refers to both cases. It would have been appropriate for him to say afterwards: 'and the slave owes another thousand as the compensation for manumission, and the buyer in the second case owes a thousand as the price of the slave,' because the payment is invalid in both cases due to the master's entitlement to what was paid via another channel, namely that it is the acquisition of his slave, so it was owned by him before the purchase and before the manumission. And he indicated by the agent's need to attribute to the slave (that he is the principal) that he is a messenger and rights do not revert to him, so the claim for the other thousand is against the slave, not the agent, and this is correct. And when it is known that the slave's purchase of himself from his master is legally manumission, even though it is a purchase in form, the rulings of purchase are not considered for it. Hence it is stated in al-Mi'raj that if he buys himself to a term, then on this basis it is not invalidated by a corrupt condition nor does the option of stipulation enter it. And in Buyu' al-Khaniyah from al-Istihqaq: A slave bought himself from his master with another man for one thousand dirhams; it is mentioned in al-Muntaqa that it is valid for the slave's share and the partner's share is void. This is not like the case of a father buying his child with another man for a thousand dirhams, where the entire contract is valid. End.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage treats a specific scenario in agency: the principal is a slave who appoints an agent to buy him from his master. The agent buys the slave from the master, and there is ambiguity whether the agent bought for himself or for the slave (principal). The author explains that if the agent says 'I bought him for myself,' the purchase is effectively manumission (i'taq) because the slave's purchase of himself is legally emancipation. The master receives his price (the thousand), and the slave is freed with his patronage (wala') remaining with the master. If the agent does not clarify, the literal meaning of the sale is exchange, so the slave becomes owned by the agent (the buyer) and the agent must pay another thousand as the price. The logical reasoning: selling a slave to himself is manumission, not a true sale. The agent is merely a messenger (safir) for the slave; rights do not revert to him. Thus, if the agent clarifies, it is manumission; if not, it is treated as a sale to the agent. The author notes that the phrase 'the thousand is for the master' applies to both cases (manumission and sale). Ideally, the text should specify additional payments: in the manumission case, the slave owes another thousand as compensation; in the sale case, the agent owes a thousand as price. This is because the master is entitled to the thousand paid by the agent as the slave's acquisition, so that payment is invalid from the slave's side. Therefore, the slave must pay again or the agent must pay again. The author confirms that the claim for the extra thousand is on the slave, not the agent, and this is the correct position. The passage also notes that since this is legally manumission, it is not subject to the rules of sale, such as invalidation by a corrupt condition or option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). A sub-case from al-Khaniyah illustrates that if a slave buys himself jointly with another person, the slave's share is valid but the partner's share is void, distinguishing from a father buying his child jointly.

Key Terms
إعتاق (i'taq): Legal act of manumission (freeing a slave). Here, the purchase of a slave by himself is treated as manumission.
الولاء (al-wala'): Patronage (clientage) that arises from manumission; the former master retains inheritance rights and other ties.
سفير (safir): Messenger or intermediary; the agent acts as a mere conduit with no personal liability in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master, and the agent says 'I bought him for myself,' what happens to the slave?
A) The agent owns him.
B) The slave is freed and patronage goes to the master.
C) The slave is freed and patronage goes to the agent.
D) The transaction is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when an agent buys the slave-principal from his master, the legal outcome hinges on the agent's declaration: clarifying for the slave results in manumission, while ambiguity results in a sale to the agent.

Agent Instructs Slave to Buy Himself: Manumission and Liability
Ruling when an agent instructs a slave to buy himself from the master.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent tells a slave to buy himself from the master and identify who bears the liability for the price.

Core Idea
A slave buying himself is manumission; the slave owes the price, not the agent.

In Hanafi sales law, a peculiar situation arises when an agent instructs a slave to purchase himself from his master. The phrase 'buy yourself for me' is legally equivalent to the slave buying himself, which triggers the rules of manumission (itq) rather than a normal sale. This lesson unpacks the subtle legal consequences: the slave becomes free, and the price becomes a debt on the slave, not on the agent. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between agency in purchase and the unique status of the slave as both subject and object of the transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Action
The agent says to the slave: 'Ishter li nafsaka min mawlak' – buy yourself for me from your master. Recognize that this instruction is directed to the slave, not to the master.
The slave is the one performing the purchase, not the agent.
2
Determine the Legal Effect of the Slave's Action
When the slave says to the master 'Sell me to so-and-so' (the agent) and the master agrees, the legal effect is that the slave is buying himself. In Hanafi law, a slave buying himself from his master constitutes manumission (itq), not a standard sale.
The slave cannot own himself; purchasing himself equals freedom.
3
Assign Liability for the Price
Since the transaction is manumission, the price (thaman) becomes a debt on the slave, not on the agent. The agent is not liable for the price because the instruction was for the slave to buy himself, not for the agent to buy the slave.
The slave owes the price to the master and must pay it.
4
Confirm the Ruling from Context
The source text is incomplete but the Hanafi ruling is consistent: the slave is free (hurr) and the price is upon him (al-thamanu 'alayhi). The agent's role ends with the instruction.
This ruling is found in major Hanafi works such as al-Hidayah and al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Source Text

وإن قال لعبد اشتر لي نفسك من مولاك فقال للمولى بعني نفسي لفلان ففعل فهو...
And if he said to a slave: 'Buy yourself for me from your master,' and he said to the master: 'Sell me to so-and-so,' and he did so, then it is...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This is a brief ruling about an agent instructing a slave to buy himself from his master. The agent says: 'Buy yourself for me from your master.' The slave then goes to the master and says: 'Sell me to so-and-so (the agent).' The master agrees. The text cuts off, but from context and similar rulings in Hanafi fiqh, the legal outcome is that the slave becomes free (since the slave buying himself is manumission) and the slave owes the price to the master. The agent is not liable; the debt is on the slave. This is consistent with the previous lesson where the slave buying himself is treated as manumission. The incomplete text likely continues with 'he (the slave) is free and the price is on him' or similar. Since the passage is truncated, the commentary is based on the logical flow from the preceding text.

Key Terms
اشتر لي نفسك (ishtar li nafsaka): An instruction from the agent to the slave to buy himself (the slave) on behalf of the agent.
عتق (itq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the consideration paid in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When an agent instructs a slave to buy himself, the slave becomes free and owes the price to the master; the agent bears no liability.

Agent Selling to Those Whose Testimony is Rejected
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent selling to disqualified witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding an agent selling to persons whose testimony is rejected, explain the proofs for each, and apply the ruling to a given scenario involving price conditions.

Core Idea
Places of suspicion are excepted from agency; selling to close associates creates a conflict of interest.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether an agent may sell to persons whose testimony is not accepted for the principal. The disagreement hinges on whether the benefits are connected and thus create a suspicion of collusion. We will dissect each position, its proofs, and the practical consequences, equipping you to navigate similar conflicts of interest in agency transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn permit selling to such persons except to the agent's own slave and mukatab, because the authorization is absolute and properties are diverse, so no suspicion arises.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Imam prohibits selling to any such person unless at market value, because places of suspicion are excepted by default; the connection of benefits (e.g., slave's property belongs to master) creates a conflict akin to selling to oneself.
3
Identify the core point of dispute
The core disagreement is whether the connection of benefits automatically creates a conflict of interest (Imam) or only when the buyer is an extension of the agent (Sahibayn).
4
Examine the exceptions and price conditions
Note that if the agent is given absolute discretion ("sell to whomever you wish"), even the Sahibayn allow it. Price conditions: more than value is valid; less with gross fraud invalid by consensus; less with slight fraud invalid only for Imam; at equivalent value, two narrations from Imam.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the preferred view
Practical differences: under Sahibayn, an agent can sell to his father at market value; under Imam, such a sale is invalid. The text leans toward Imam's cautious position, widely accepted in later fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب كالة بالبيع والشراء
وقالا : يجوز بيعه منهم بمثل القيمة إلا من عبده ومكاتبه لأن التوكيل مطلق ولا تهمة إذ الأملاك متباينة والنافع منقطعة بخلاف العبد لأنه بيع من نفسه لأن ما في يد العبد للمولى، وكذا للمولى حق في كسب المكاتب وينقلب حقيقة بالعجز وله أن مواضع التهمة مستثناة من الوكالات وهذا موضع التهمة بدليل عدم قبول الشهادة ولأن المنافع بينهم متصلة فصار بيعاً . من نفسه من وجه ودخل في البيع الإجارة والصرف والسلم فهو على هذا الخلاف. قيد بكونه وكيلاً بلا تعميم لأنه لو أطلق له بأن قال بع ممن شئت فإنه يجوز بيعه لهم بمثل القيمة. وأطلق في منع عقده وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن بأكثر من القيمة فإن كان بأكثر جاز بلا خلاف، وإن كان بأقل بغبن فاحش لا يجوز ،بالإجماع، وإن كان بغبن يسير لا يجوز عنده خلافاً لهما، وإن كان بمثل القيمة فعنه روايتان وأطلق الوكيل فشمل المضارب إلا أنه إذا كان بمثل القيمة فإنه يجوز عنده باتفاق الروايات وشمل من ترد شهادته مفاوضة فهو كعبده وشريكه شركة عنان يجوز عقده معه إذا لم يكن ذلك من تجارتهما؛ كذا في الخانية من السلم. وفي الخلاصة : لو باع من ابنه الصغير لا يجوز بالإجماع ا هـ . فعلى هذا كان ينبغي للشارحين أن يقولوا في تقرير قولهما إلا من عبده ومكاتبه ومفاوضه وابنه الصغير فالمستثنى من قولهما أربع. وقيد العبد في المبسوط بغير المديون وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لو كان مديوناً فإنه يجوز؛ كذا في المعراج. وقيد بقوله «له» لأنه لو عقد مع من ترد شهادته للموكل كأبيه وابنه ومكاتبه وعبده المديون جاز، وكذا الوكيل العبد إذا باع من مولاه؛ كذا في الخلاصة.
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase: And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible for him to sell to them at the equivalent value except to his slave and his mukatab, because the authorization is absolute and there is no suspicion (of collusion) since properties are diverse and benefits are disconnected, unlike the slave because it is a sale from himself since what is in the hand of the slave belongs to the master, and likewise the master has a right in the earnings of the mukatab and he (the mukatab) reverts to the state of slavery by incapacity. And (Imam Abu Hanifa) said: Places of suspicion are excepted from agencies, and this is a place of suspicion, evidenced by the non-acceptance of testimony, and because the benefits between them are connected, so it becomes a sale from himself in some respect. And included in the sale is lease, currency exchange, and salam, so it is subject to this difference. It is conditioned that he is an agent without generalization, because if he gave him broad authority by saying 'Sell to whomever you wish,' then it is permissible for him to sell to them at the equivalent value. And his prohibition of the contract is absolute, but it is restricted to when it is not more than the value; if it is more, it is permissible without dispute; if it is less with gross deception, it is not permissible by consensus; if it is less with slight deception, it is not permissible according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them (the two). If it is at the equivalent value, then there are two narrations from him. And the word 'agent' is general, so it includes the mudarib, except that if it is at the equivalent value, it is permissible according to him by agreement of narrations. And it includes one whose testimony is rejected for a partnership of mutual investment, so he is like his slave; and his partner in a partnership of equal investment, his contract with him is permissible if that is not from their trade; as in al-Khaniya from the Salam. And in al-Khulasa: If he sold to his minor son, it is not permissible by consensus. So according to this, it was befitting for the commentators to say in explaining their statement: 'Except from his slave, his mukatab, his mutual investment partner, and his minor son,' so the exceptions from their statement are four. And the slave is restricted in al-Mabsut to the non-indebted, and there is an indication that if he was indebted, it is permissible; as in al-Mi'raj. And it is restricted by his saying 'for him' because if he contracted with one whose testimony is rejected for the principal, like his father, his son, his mukatab, and his indebted slave, it is permissible; likewise the slave agent when he sells from his master; as in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding an agent selling to persons whose testimony is not accepted for the principal. The text begins by presenting the position of the Sahibayn: it is permissible to sell to such persons at the equivalent market value, except to the agent's own slave and mukatab. Their reasoning is that the agency is general and there is no suspicion of collusion because properties are separate. Imam Abu Hanifa disagrees, arguing that places of suspicion are excepted from agency, and this is a place of suspicion because the benefits are connected. The commentary then discusses conditions: if the agent is given absolute discretion ("sell to whomever you wish"), even the Sahibayn allow sale to these persons. The price conditions are detailed: selling for more than value is unanimously permissible; selling for less with gross fraud is unanimously invalid; selling for less with slight fraud is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa but valid according to the Sahibayn. At equivalent value, there are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage also covers exceptions including the mudarib, the mutual investment partner, and the minor son (by consensus not permissible). The text clarifies that the slave mentioned is restricted to non-indebted slave, and that selling to one's father, son, or indebted slave is valid because the suspicion is removed. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that agency contracts are interpreted strictly to avoid conflicts of interest.

Key Terms
من ترد شهادته له (man turiddu shahadatuhu lahu): Persons whose testimony is not accepted in favor of the principal, such as father, son, slave, etc.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fahish): Gross deception in price, where the difference is significant and not normally tolerated in trade.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, which of the following sales by an agent is absolutely invalid?
A) Selling to his father at market price.
B) Selling to his own slave at market price.
C) Selling to his son at a 10% loss.
D) Selling to his partner in a limited partnership.

Key Takeaway
An agent must avoid selling to close associates unless at a fair price, because even a slight conflict of interest can invalidate the sale according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Agent Selling to Himself: Permissibility and Restrictions
Rulings on agent and executor self-dealing with conditions and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for an agent, executor, and mudarib regarding self-dealing and state the conditions that validate or invalidate the sale.

Core Idea
An agent cannot sell to himself without authorization; an executor may if beneficial; the contradiction of being both buyer and seller is resolved by consent or ward's benefit.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical conflict-of-interest scenario in agency law: can an agent who is appointed to sell also buy the item himself? The default ruling is prohibition, but Hanafi law carves out exceptions based on the principal's explicit authorization and the special status of executors and mudaribs, who may engage in limited self-dealing for the benefit of those they represent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and its rationale
Note that the agent (wakil) appointed to sell cannot buy the item for himself because one person cannot be both buyer and seller simultaneously. This is a matter of logical contradiction and conflict of interest.
The text cites al-Bazzaziyya: 'al-wakil bi'l-bay' la yamlik shira'ahu li nafsihi'.
2
Locate the exception: principal's authorization
If the principal explicitly orders the agent to sell to himself, his minor children, or those whose testimony is not accepted, the sale becomes permissible. This lifts the prohibition.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj states that it is permissible by consensus when ordered, except for selling to the principal himself or his minor child without debt.
3
Contrast with executor (wasi) and mudarib
The executor has more flexibility: selling to himself or relatives at market value is valid, and if beneficial (khayr) even more so. The mudarib is compared to the executor, not to the general agent.
Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow executor self-dealing if beneficial; Muhammad prohibits absolutely.
4
Apply the rules to marriage agency and receipt agency
The same principles extend to marriage agency (tazwij) and agency for receipt (qabd). An agent for marriage cannot marry his minor daughter to the principal without authorization, while an agent for receipt is trusted in his claim of collection.
Al-Bazzaziyya and al-Kafi are cited for these extensions.
5
Understand the definition of 'beneficial' (khayr)
Khayr is defined as the executor buying orphan's property above market price or selling his own property to the orphan below market price. This ensures the orphan benefits from the transaction.
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi's interpretation is recorded in al-Khaniya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف عقد الوكيل إلى منع بيعه مرابحة ما اشتراه منهم بلا بيان قال في المعراج معزياً إلى الكافي : ولو اشترى من هؤلاء عيناً بثمن معلوم وأراد بيعه مرابحة لم يجز بلا بيان عنده خلافاً لهما بناء على هذا الأصل ا هـ. وأشار المؤلف إلى منع بيعه من نفسه بالأولى. قال في البزازية : الوكيل بالبيع لا يملك شراءه لنفسه لأن الواحد لا يكون مشترياً وبائعاً فيبيعه من غيره ثم يشتريه منه، وإن أمره الموكل أن يبيعه من نفسه أو أولاده الصغار أو ممن لا تقبل شهادته فباع منهم جاز ا.هـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : لو أمره بالبيع من هؤلاء فإنه يجوز إجماعاً إلا أن نفسه أو ولده الصغير أو عبده ولا دين عليه فلا يجوز يبيعه من الموكل ا هـ. وقيد بالوكيل لأن الوصي لو باع منهم بمثل القيمة فإنه يجوز وإن حابا فيه لا يجوز وإن قل والمضارب كالوصي؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج وفي جامع الفصولين: لو باع القيم مال الوقف أو أجر ممن لا تقبل شهادته له لم يجز عند أبي حنيفة وفيه : المتولي إذا أجر دار الوقف من ابنه البالغ أو أبيه لم يجز عند أبي حنيفة إلا بأكثر من أجر المثل كبيع الوصي، ولو أجر من نفسه يجوز لو خيراً وإلا لا ا هـ . ولو حذف قوله بالبيع والشراء» لكان أولى ليدخل النكاح. قال في البزازية : وكله بتزويج فزوج ابنته الصغيرة لا يجوز ولو كبيرة أو ممن لا تقبل شهادته لها لا يجوز عنده خلافاً لهما ا هـ . وفي السراج الوهاج : ولو اشترى الأب مال ولده الصغير بمثل القيمة أو بأكثر أو بأقل بمقدار ما يتغابن فيه صح الشراء، وبما لا يتغابن فيه لا يصح، وكذا لو باع ماله من ولده الصغير والجد أبو الأب كالأب عند عدمه ووصيه، وأما حكم الوصي فهو كالأب والجد إذا عقد مع أجنبي، وأما مع نفسه فقال الإمام: يجوز إن كان خيراً. وذكر الطحاوي قول أبي يوسف معه. وقال محمد: لا يجوز بحال ا هـ . وتفسير الخيرية في وصايا الخانية. وقيد بالعقد احترازاً عن الوكيل بالقبض. قال الحاكم في الكافي : ولو وكله بقبض دين له على أب الوكيل أو ولده أو مكاتب لولده أو عبده فقال الوكيل قد قبضت الدين وهلك وكذبه الطالب فالقول قول الوكيل، فإذا كان الوكيل عبداً فقال قد قبضت من مولاي أو من عبد مولاي فهلك مني فهو مصدق أيضاً، فإن كان الوكيل ابن الطالب أو المطلوب فهو كذلك اهـ .
قوله : (ويصح بيعه بما قل وكثر وبالنقد أو النسيئة) يعني عند الإمام. وقالا : لا يجوز بيعه بنقصان لا يتغابن الناس فيه ولا يجوز إلا بالدراهم والدنانير لأن مطلق الأمر يتقيد بالمتعارف لأن التصرفات لدفع الحاجات فتتقيد بمواقعها والمتعارف البيع بمثل الثمن وبالنقود، ولهذا يتقيد التوكيل بشراء الفحم والجمد والأضحية بزمان الحاجة ففي الفحم بالشتاء، وفي باب البيع إذا باشر العقد من الجانبين يؤدي إلى تضاد الأحكام فإنه يكون مشترياً ومستقضياً، قابضاً ومسلماً، مخاصماً في العيب ومخاصماً، وفيه من التضاد ما لا يخفى اهـ. وهذا موافق لما يأتي عن السراج
وكأن في المسألة ،قولين والوجه ما في النهاية إلا إذا جاز الموكل بعد البيع فلا يرد ما ذكره تأمل .
قوله : ( وتفسير الخبرية في وصايا (الخانية وعبارته : فسر شمس الأئمة السرخسي الخيرية فقال : إذا اشترى الوصي مال اليتيم لنفسه ما يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر يكون خيراً لليتيم وإن باع مال نفسه من اليتيم ما يساوي عشرة بثمانية يكون خيراً لليتيم انتهت قوله: (ولا يجوز إلا بالدراهم والدنانير) قال الزيلعي : حالة أو إلى أجل متعارف قوله : (والجمد) بسكون الميم لا غير هو ما جمد من
And the author indicated the prohibition of the agent selling on profit (murabaha) what he bought from them without disclosure. He said in al-Mi'raj quoting al-Kafi: If he bought from these persons an item for a known price and wants to sell it on profit, it is not permissible without disclosure according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them (the two), based on this principle. And the author indicated the prohibition of his selling to himself a fortiori. He said in al-Bazzaziyya: The agent for sale does not own buying it for himself because one person cannot be both buyer and seller, so he sells it to another and then buys it from him. If the principal orders him to sell to himself or his minor children or to one whose testimony is not accepted, and he sells to them, it is permissible. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If he orders him to sell to these, it is permissible by consensus, except if it is himself, his minor child, or his slave with no debt, then he cannot sell to the principal. And it is restricted to the agent because if the executor sells to them at the equivalent value, it is permissible; if he gives a preference (rebate), it is not permissible even if small; and the mudarib is like the executor. Similarly in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the trustee of waqf property sells or leases to one whose testimony is not accepted for him, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa. It includes: If the mutawalli leases the waqf property to his adult son or his father, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa unless at more than the market rent, like the sale of the executor; and if he leases to himself, it is permissible if he is good, otherwise not. And if he omitted his saying 'for sale and purchase', it would be better to include marriage. He said in al-Bazzaziyya: If he authorized him to marry and he married his minor daughter, it is not permissible; if an adult or one whose testimony is not accepted for her, it is not permissible according to him (Abu Hanifa) contrary to them. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If the father buys the property of his minor child at the equivalent value or more or less by a margin that people tolerate in trade, the purchase is valid; by a margin not tolerated, it is not valid. Similarly, if he sells his property to his minor child; and the grandfather (father's father) is like the father in his absence; and the executor is like the father and grandfather when he contracts with a stranger. But with himself, Imam said: It is permissible if he is good; al-Tahawi narrated that Abu Yusuf agrees with him; Muhammad said: It is not permissible at all. The interpretation of 'goodness' is in the Wasiyya section of al-Khaniya. And it is restricted to the contract as opposed to the agent for receipt. Al-Hakim said in al-Kafi: If he authorizes him to collect a debt that he owes to the agent's father, son, or his son's mukatab, or his slave, and the agent said 'I have collected the debt and it perished' and the claimant denies it, the statement of the agent is accepted. If the agent is a slave and says 'I collected from my master or from my master's slave and it perished', he is also believed. If the agent is the son of the claimant or the debtor, it is likewise. Then the text discusses the validity of the agent's sale for any amount, cash or credit, according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two companions say it is not permissible to sell at a loss that people do not tolerate (slight fraud) and it is only permissible with dirhams and dinars because the absolute command is restricted by custom. They analogize to the purchase of charcoal, ice, and sacrificial animal being restricted to the time of need. This is consistent with what comes from al-Siraj. It appears there are two sayings in the issue, the correct is what is in al-Nihaya unless the principal authorizes after the sale, then it is not rejected. Consider carefully. Then the interpretation of 'goodness' in the Wasiyya section of al-Khaniya: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi explained that if the executor buys an orphan's property for himself, worth ten, for fifteen, it is good for the orphan; if he sells his own property to the orphan worth ten for eight, it is good for the orphan. Then the clause about not permissible except with dirhams and dinars: al-Zayla'i said: on the spot or to a customary term. And the clause about 'al-jamad' (ice): with sukun on the meem, it is what has frozen from water.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage covers several interrelated rulings about the agent selling to himself, his minor child, his slave, and similar parties, with comparisons to executors and trustees. It begins by noting that the author (of the matn) indicated the prohibition of the agent selling on murabaha (cost-plus profit) what he bought from those whose testimony is rejected without disclosing the purchase details. This is based on the principle that places of suspicion are excluded. The prohibition of selling to oneself is even stronger (a fortiori). The agent cannot buy for himself because one person cannot be both seller and buyer; he must sell to another first. However, if the principal explicitly authorizes selling to himself or his minor children or to those whose testimony is rejected, it becomes permissible by consensus, except for selling to the principal himself, his minor child, or his non-indebted slave (according to al-Siraj al-Wahhaj). The ruling is specific to the agent; an executor (wasi) may sell to such persons at market value, but if he gives a preference (rebate), it is invalid even if small. The mudarib (investment agent) is like the executor. For the trustee (qayyim) of waqf property, selling or leasing to those whose testimony is rejected is invalid according to Abu Hanifa unless at a higher price than market. The text also covers marriage agency: an agent for marriage cannot marry his minor daughter to the principal, but if she is adult or the marriage is to one whose testimony is rejected, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to the two companions. The father buying from his minor child at market value or with a small acceptable difference is valid, but with gross deception it is invalid. The grandfather is like the father in his absence. The executor's purchase from himself is permissible if it is beneficial (khayr) to the orphan; Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit it if beneficial, but Muhammad prohibits it absolutely. 'Beneficial' is defined as selling above market or buying below market. The text then discusses an agent for receipt (wakil bi'l-qabd): if he claims to have collected the debt from his own father, son, etc., and the money is lost, his word is accepted. This contrasts with the agent for contract who has restrictions. The passage ends with a discussion on the validity of the agent's sale for any amount (cash or credit) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, while the two companions restrict to customary currency and prohibit selling at a loss not tolerated. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that absolute agency is restricted by custom ('urf).

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): Executor of a will, or guardian appointed to manage the affairs of orphans or the estate.
الخيرية (al-khayriyya): The condition of being beneficial (khayr) to the orphan, i.e., selling above market price or buying below market price.
المضارب (al-mudarib): An investment agent who operates a mudaraba (profit-sharing) partnership.
الوكيل (al-wakil): Agent, someone appointed to act on behalf of another in a specific matter.
الغبن الفاحش (ghabn fahish): Gross deception or fraud in the price, usually a significant difference that people do not tolerate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when can an executor (wasi) buy the orphan's property for himself?
A) Only if the principal authorizes it
B) Only if it is beneficial (khayr) for the orphan, e.g., buying above market price
C) At any time, as long as the price is not fraudulent
D) Never, according to all Hanafi imams

Key Takeaway
An agent can sell to himself only with the principal's explicit authorization; an executor may do so if it benefits the orphan, illustrating how Hanafi law balances conflict of interest with the ward's welfare.
